Then pour a small amount of hot water into the cannula; hold your hand, and revolve it with pressure from your hand for a short time, so that the edge of the cannula is imprinted on the root of the nail, and the hot water finds its way deep down to the root of the nail; and let the patient wait for a little while above the bite of the hot water for an hour.
Then dismiss him, for the nail will be extracted with its roots.
Do this one by one until you come to all of them that are in the body.
Then care for them after they are extracted with that which produces flesh from ointments.
On the method of cauterizing in a tremor.
When it happens to someone that a tremor occurs from cold in the nerve, or from quartan fever, or some other cause: then it is necessary that you cauterize him with four cauteries, or five on the vertebrae of the back, and between every vertebra a cautery, and one on his chest, and one on his stomach.
For the tremor subsides with an olive-wood cautery, and hastens the healing of a cold illness.
On the method of cauterizing Albathir.
Chapter Fifty-Seven.
Sometimes foul Albathir are expelled from the body, that is, pustules which are made from corrupted thick humors.
Therefore, when they first appear, it is necessary that they are cauterized over the entire head of the pustule with a fine cautery made from myrtle wood, whose tip is ignited with fire. Or with a long aristolochia root. Or with a lentil-shaped cautery.
And sometimes they are cauterized at the beginning of their expulsion according to this method; and they do not grow larger; and the excess is broken down making them, and they are healed from them.
However, it must be done after evacuation with bloodletting.
On the method of cauterizing arterial bleeding in a wound.
Very often it happens that blood flows from an artery which has been cut at a wound, which occurs externally, or at the incision of an abscess, or a cautery of a limb, and similar things: and it is difficult to stop it.
Therefore, when this happens to someone, then hurry with your hand to the opening of the artery, and place your index finger upon it: and press it hard until the blood is blocked under your finger, and nothing comes out from it.
Then place a small olive-wood cautery in the fire and many more of a large number: and blow upon them until they become very hot.
Then take one from them, either small or large, according to the wound and the place where the artery has been ruptured, and apply the cautery upon it, after which you quickly remove your finger: and hold the cautery until the bleeding is stopped.
And if it is expelled when you raise your finger from the opening of the artery, and you extinguish the cautery, then quickly take another cautery from those which are prepared in the fire.
Therefore, do not cease to do that with one after another until the bleeding is stopped.
And be careful not to burn the nerve which is there, and cause other harm to befall the weak patient.
And know that when blood flows from an artery, then it is not possible to stop it: especially when the artery is large, except with one of four methods. Or with a cautery, as we have said. Or with extraction when it has not been extracted. For when it is extracted, its extremities are contracted and the bleeding is stopped. Or it should be tied with a strong ligature. Or let medications of a kind that stanch blood be placed upon it, and let it be compressed with a decent ligature.
But he who performs its excision with a ligature, and constriction with cloth, or the application of burning agents, and similar things, does not thoroughly heal it except rarely.
But if it should happen to someone and there is neither a physician present nor medicine: then let him begin by placing the index finger upon the orifice of the vein itself, as we have described, and constrict it tightly until the blood is obstructed from the upper part of the wound and above the artery. And let the finger not be removed, but constantly apply cold water of intense coldness over it until the blood congeals and thickens, and is cut off.
And meanwhile, let him consider what he needs from cautery or medicine.
On Incision, Perforation, and Phlebotomy in Wounds and Similar Things.
We have already said in the First Chapter that every ailment in which it is fitting to use fire for cautery and burning medicine, its occasions, its causes, its instruments, and the forms of cauteries.
And I have placed there chapters from head to foot.
And I will proceed in this chapter the same way, so that it may relieve the inquirer seeking his question.
And before I begin that, it is necessary for you to know, O son, that in this chapter there is terror from what is in the First Chapter on cauterization.
And on account of that it is necessary that there be more intense caution in it, because in the operation which is in this chapter, evacuation often occurs with blood, with which life stands at the opening of a vein, or incision over an abscess, or opening of an exit wound, or treatment of a wound, or extraction of an arrow, or incision over a stone, and things similar to these: from all of which association trembling and fear arise, and death falls upon many of them.
And I warn you, lest it fall upon you in which there is hesitation, for sometimes there fall upon you in this art ways of men with ways of infirmities. For among them is he who becomes angry with his illness, and death is light to him because of the violence he finds from his infirmity and the length of his malice. And with illness there is fear which signifies death.
And among them is he who gives you his lute player and enriches you with him, hoping for healing, and his illness is pernicious.
Therefore it is not fitting that you consent to him who comes to you from among them, whose manner is such. And let your caution be stronger than your desire and your greed. And do not presume anything from him unless after certain knowledge which verifies with you that which has come to a laudable outcome.
And use in the care of all your sick, prognosis and indication concerning it so that health may suitably come about.
For in that there is help for you in acquiring fame and glory, and memory and praise.
May God inspire in you his understanding, and not take away from you direction and inspiration for it is in his hand.
And I indeed arranged this chapter distinguishing it by chapters, according to what preceded in the chapter on Caustic, from the neck down to the feet so that what you desire and need from it may be relieved for you.
On the treatment of water which collects in the heads of infants.
Chapter I.
Know that this infirmity often happens to boys at birth: or when the midwife tightens the head of the boy without ease.
And whenever it happens also from a hidden cause which is not known.
And I myself have not seen this illness in anyone but children, and death hastened to all of them whom I saw: therefore it seemed best to me to forgo operating on it.
And I myself saw a boy from them whose head was filled with water, and his head swelled every day until he could not sit upon himself because of the size of his head: and the moisture increased until he died.
And this moisture indeed either gathers between the skin and bone or gathers beneath the bone upon the suture.
And the operation on that, if the moisture is between the skin and bone, and it is a small abscess, then it is necessary that you make a single fissure in the middle of the head one finger wide: and let the length of the fissure be about two knuckles, until the moisture runs out.
And this is the form of a spatula.
If therefore the moisture is greater and the abscess is larger, then make those two fissures according to this form.
And if the moisture is beneath the bone, and its sign is that you see the sutures of the head open from every side: and water is depressed when you press it with your hand towards the interior, and it does not hide from you: then it is necessary that you make three fissures in the middle of the head according to this manner.
And after the incision extract all the moisture. Then bind the fissures with cloths and lint. Then embrocate it from above with wine and oil until the fifth day.
Then loosen the bandage, and heal the wound with tents and ointments.
And do not cease to bind the head evenly. And feed the sick person with all dry food of little moisture until the limb is strengthened and healed.
And another method of incision is to consider where the magnitude of the abscess appears and the collection of water.
For sometimes there is more in the back of the head, or in the forehead, or on the right, or on the left.
Therefore ascend with an incision where you see the abscess and filling of water; and split it according to what is possible for you.
And observe that you do not cut into an artery and that a flow of blood occurs, and the sick person dies from that flow with the evacuation of moisture.
Concerning the incision of arteries which are behind the ears.
Chapter II.
When it happens to someone that there are catarrs to the eyes, or to the chest, and it becomes old, and treatment with medicine does not help; then the final treatment in that case is the incision of these two arteries.
Therefore it is necessary that when you wish to cut into them, that the sick person should shave their head. Then rub the place with a rough cloth so that the arteries appear. Then let the sick person tighten their neck with any cloth of theirs. Then consider where the vein pulses. And the locations of both are places which are behind the ears depressed: and it is rarely hidden, except in certain people.
Then mark with hot wax over each one; and cut both with a scalpel on both sides, the incision reaching the mouth; and let it be done across the width of the head.
And if you wish, insert the scalpel under the artery, and cut it upwards with an incision.
And let the length of the incision be like two joined fingers.
For when a vein is cut, blood comes out with a throbbing flow which leaps to the insides by frequent jumps.
But if the artery does not appear to the senses, then it is necessary that you measure from the ear a distance of three fingerbreadths. Then mark with hot wax and cut upwards to the mouth.
And that which must be emitted from the blood is six ounces, and perhaps you will emit more, or less from it. All of that according to the quantity which appears to you from the strength of the weak person and the repletion of their artery.
Then consider the wound. Then if anything remains at the mouth of the wound from the incision, then cut that out so that a hot abscess may not occur; then bind the wound with lint from linen cloth.
Then care for it with ointment until it heals.
Concerning the extraction of arteries which are in the temples.
Chapter III.
When it happens to a man that an old hemicrania, or acute catarrh occurs because of acrimonious humors, and heat in the fleshy parts of the temples, or an old, vehement soda, and similar things to those; and he is treated with methods of medical treatment, and it does not help: then we have experienced in these illnesses the extraction of arteries from the temples, or their cauterization, as we have described.
And the method of procedure in their extraction is that the weak person shaves the hairs which are in the temples, and stretches towards the apparent artery in the temple. For it itself shows itself to you from its pulse, and rarely is it concealed except in a few men, or extreme cold.
But if it is concealed from you, then let the weak person bind their head with any cloth; then rub the place with a rough cloth. Or steam the place with hot water until it appears to you with manifest appearance. Then take the speculum, which I had previously prepared. Then scrape away the skin with it easily, until you reach the artery. Then project a hook into it and draw it upwards until you draw it out from the skin, and make it come out from all the tunics which are under it.
If, however, the artery is slender, then envelop it with the extremity of a hook. Then make an incision into it according to the amount by which its extremities will be extended and contracted so that a flow of blood does not occur. For if it is not incised and not cut off, the blood does not rise completely.
Then evacuate from six ounces of blood up to three.
But if the artery is large, it is necessary that you tie it in two places with a doubled, strong silk thread or with silkworm gut so that decay does not hasten to it before the wound consolidates and a flow of blood occurs.
Then make an incision into the excess which is between the two ligatures. Do this in that hour or after another hour.
And if you wish, cauterize it with a cautery up to the bone with a knife cautery until its extremities fall away and it remains in place of the same operation.
And this is a better procedure unless the patient has a fever or an overheated complexion. Because a cautery is one of those things that help to finish humidities. Therefore it is a better remedy.
And it is necessary after the extraction of arteries that the place be filled with old cotton and poultices be placed on top, loosely made.
And after it is dissolved, it should be treated with dry medicines that cause flesh to grow and with dressings until it heals.
But if meanwhile while you are operating a flow of blood occurs from the artery, then hurry to cut it off with a cautery or to fill the place with wax and bind your hand until the bleeding is stopped. But if none of these things are present to you, then place your finger on top of the place until the blood clots. Embrocate the place with water of sharp cold until the sharpness is stilled, and bind it as needed.
And because that which is lighter and easier to extract is the vein, it is so that you should cauterize the vein with this cautery having two knives; and let the sharpness of the two knives be similar to a razor. However they are of much less sharpness than two knives. For if they are sharp like two knives, cold will quickly occur to both; and they will not strike the flesh with speed. Therefore since there is a little thickness in them, the heat of the fire is retained in them and the flesh is cut quickly; with this therefore it is better, lighter, and easier work.
And this is the form of the cautery.
And let the space which is between the two knives be the thickness of a finger, after you have marked two places with a cautery; and lay both ignited strongly until they reach the mouth, and fall upon the vein in two places: so that what is between its ends may be lengthened. For they are not entirely consolidated with this cautery.
Chapter Four.
On the treatment of a sharp, persistent flow of tears from the eyes.
When the tears are persistent, and their flow is from the veins which are above the outer skull; and no medicinal treatment confers anything with medicines, and you see the face of the sick person already reddening, and he feels a movement on his forehead like that of ants walking; and his eyes are weakened, moist, so that his eyelashes have already been corroded: and his eyelids are heated from the sharpness of the tears; then the ancients used to employ this operation in their treatment, and they called it treatment with the sword.
And it is so that you order the sick person to shave the hairs which are on his forehead. Then make three incisions in the forehead equidistant according to the length of the forehead; and let the length of an incision be like two fingers, and one incision be equidistant from the length of the nose in the middle of the forehead, and the second a little behind the length of the motion of the masticatory muscles, and the third on the other side.
Then introduce a speculum with two pointed ends, which soon follows this form. From the incision which is from the side of the temple up to the second incision; and with it all the skin which is between the two incisions, together with the sifac, which is above the mouth. Then also do that from the middle incision up to the second incision. Then extract this speculum and introduce into the first incision again another instrument which is called a sharp curette, blunt on one side, not sharp on the other side. And this is its form.
And place its sharp side against the flesh joined with skin, and its blunt side towards the mouth; and press it until it reaches the middle fissure; and cut off with it all the vessels which descend from the head to the eyes: except that the incision continues until it appears from the skin.
Then do that in the middle fissure up to the other fissure.
And after an equal quantity flows out from the blood, press out the places from lumps of clotted blood.
Then place in all the fissures a lint made from old linen and put over them compresses soaked in wine, oil, or vinegar. And see that a hot abscess does not occur.
And on the third day loosen the ligature and administer a plentiful infusion with warm water. Then care for it with royal ointment and other remedies that heal wounds until it is healed.
Chapter Five.
On the treatment of acute tearings and discharges to the eyes from within the head.
When someone has acute, sharp, and persistent discharges, and you see their eyes are weakened, small, and their gaze has already become feeble; and the eyelids are ulcerated, and hair falls out from them, and there is a sharp, piercing pain deep in the head, and consequent sneezing: then take from these occurrences that those humors and discharges do not come except from many veins.
And the better cure for them is this. And it is, that you order the sick person to shave their forehead. Then make one fissure in the middle of the forehead, or a little to one side, by its width, and begin with the fissure from the left temple to the right temple; and let the incision be up to the mouth; and extend your hand away from the temporal muscles which move at mastication: until when the mouth is exposed; and you will dry up all the blood with a sponge.
Then separate between the two lips of the fissure with old cotton or linen cloths. Then bind over it with lint; and saturate the lint with wine and oil so that a hot abscess does not occur.
And when you loosen that, and you see that the hot abscess has already subsided; then it is necessary that you freeze the mouth until flesh begins to grow in it.
Then care for it with a drying regimen that causes the flesh to grow; such as if you take two parts of wheat flour and four parts of colophony; and prepare an adhesive plaster from them, and apply it to the birth of flesh in these wounds.
Chapter VI.
On the treatment of what falls into the Ear.
Everything that falls into the ear is of one of four kinds: either a mineral stone or something like a stone, such as iron and glass; or it is from a plant, such as chickpeas and beans; or it is a flowing substance, such as water and vinegar, and things similar to them; or some animal.
Therefore, when a stone or something like a stone falls into the ear that neither adheres nor grows in the ear, expose the ear to the sun: then if you see the stone, distill something from oil of violets or sesame into the ear. Then perform its extraction, and make him sneeze with something sugared, and press his nostrils at the time of the sneeze, after you have placed around the ear wicks from cloth or wool, and extended the ear upwards.
Very often it comes out with this care.
But if it does not come out, then perform its extraction with forceps, that is, thin Pizigaroli: whose form is this.
If, therefore, it comes out with forceps, it is good.
And if not, then perform its extraction with a thin blind hook of few twists.
If, therefore, it does not come out through that, then make a tube from bronze, and insert the end of the tube into the ear canal much, and pack what is around the tube with softened wax mixed with oil so that there is no way for air except through the tube: then extract it with your own breath with strong suction.
Very often it comes out by what we have described. But if it does not come out this way: then take a small amount from hellebore or viscus, that is, prepared gluten with which birds are caught: and place it on the end of a probe after you have wrapped a well-positioned cotton over it: then insert it into the ear canal with ease, after you have dried the ear from moisture.
Therefore if it does not exit with all that we have described, then hurry to incise it before a hot abscess or spasm occurs.
And the method of incision is that phlebotomists make it first on the cephalic vein and extract from it blood according to his strength. Then make the infirm person sit between your hands and turn his ear upward, and make a small incision at the root of the ear near its fat in a depressed place; and let the incision be moon-shaped until you reach the stone; then remove it with what is possible for you from the instruments.
Then sew up the incision quickly with care, and attend to it until it heals.
But if the thing falling into the ear is a single grain that grows and swells, then administer its extraction.
But if it does not yield to your efforts, then take a thin, delicate spatula of this shape.
And administer with it the cutting off of that kind of grain that falls into the ear, and do not do this when you think that the grain is already moistened by the vapor of the ear, until you put many splinters on it; then extract them with hooks or with a delicate probe or with suction as we have said, for its extraction is alleviated.
But with regard to water that enters the ear, it is necessary for the infirm person to use sneezing when it is first instilled; and he has already filled his ear with a little old cooked oil; and he lies upon the ear in which there is water. If therefore it exits through that route, it is good.
And if not: then take many stones according to the length of a finger, thin and smooth, and heat them slightly with fire; and let the infirm person insert one of them into the opening of his ear; and jump on one foot from that side, and strike it with another stone on top of the stone which is in the ear. Therefore it shall not cease to do this with stone and stone, until all the water has come out.
And now the water comes out so that you may take one from a crucible, or from a reed, and insert one end into the ear; and set fire to the other end until most of it is burned. Then repeat with another reed and do this many times until all the water has come out; and extract it with a tube as was previously described in the stone.
But in the extraction of an animal entering it, if it is of a small body, such as a flea and the like, take care of it as I said in the division.
But if it is of large body, which appears to the senses; then administer its extraction with gestures and hooks; and its matter is easier than all that are held in the ear.
But in the extraction of a generated worm in the ear, when you care for it with that which we said in the division and in the treatise on distillations, and your treatment does not succeed; then it is necessary that you look at the ear in the sun. If therefore anything from worms appears there, then extract it with gestures or with delicate hooks.
But if nothing appears there, then take a tube, whose form is narrow below, wide above; and insert the slender end into the ear according to the quantity that the weak one bears it: then suck with it with strong suction. Do this many times until all the worms come out.
But if they do not obey you to go out, then smear the circumference of the tube with wax, as I said in the stone.
But if they do not come out with what we said; then administer distillations which the ancients have already experienced in the infection of worms; and you will find that in the treatise on distillations.
Let your effusion with which you pour oils and medicines into the ear be with this instrument, whose form is depicted here. Make it yourself of silver or bronze, narrow below, in which there be a small hole, wide above.
Let it therefore be such that driving what is in the cavity of the cannula is made of bronze cleanly. And if you wish, take a ray and wrap cotton about its end with decent winding; then pour oil or juice or whatever you wish from these medicines into the cannula; and let it be in the ear; then insert the ray with the cotton on top and press it with your hand with temperate compression until the oil is driven into the cavity of the hearing and the sick person feels it within; and let what is poured into the ear already be warmed a little with fire; and beware that nothing which distills into it is very cold, for medicines do not endure it.
Chapter VII.
Concerning the treatment of accidental hair loss of the ear.
Sometimes certain infants depart from the wombs of their mothers not perforated.
And it also happens to certain men hair loss in the ear canal from a wound or flesh born therein.
And this hair loss sometimes happens deep within the cavity of the ear which does not follow sight; and sometimes it happens near the outer opening a little and follows sight.
That therefore which occurs deep within the ear and which does not grasp sight is in many dispositions difficult healing.
And in that which follows sight, it is necessary that you place the ear of the sick person in the sun and look into it; then if you see an apparent hair loss, open it with a delicate speculum, whose form is as follows: let its extremity in which there be a certain small width have a certain pointed part; and let the remaining speculum be of two smooth sides so that it does not injure the ear.
If therefore the growth is fleshy and arises within the ear canal: then seize it with a fine hook, and cut into it with vigorous ease until all the flesh is removed.
But if the growth is deep within the ear: then take a fine, smooth probe and heat it slightly with fire; then insert it into the ear canal.
If therefore you feel the growth with it, as the one afflicted finds with hearing impairment, try to open it with ease: and beware not to wound the nerves of the ear.
Then place in the ear a lint according to the size of the opening which you enclosed in Egyptian ointment; do this for several days until you are sure of the wound's healing.
Or take lint and steep it in rose water, and sprinkle upon it ground tragacanth, and use it.
But if you see that the ear is already abscessed: then it is necessary to remove the lint and let it remain with another lint which you wrap in cerate made with rose oil until the hot abscess subsides; then care for it until it heals.
But if bleeding should occur: then immerse a sponge or cloths in cold water and place them over the ear; and use the remaining treatment which stops the aforementioned bleeding as described in the treatise on distillations.
Concerning the care of Althea eyelids.
Chapter Eight.
These Althelias which occur on the eyelids are sometimes moist and sometimes dry.
Therefore it is necessary to hold them with tweezers or a hook and cut them from their roots with a scalpel. Then place ground tragacanth on the spots.
But if blood flows from them, then cauterize them with a fine lentil-shaped cautery.
And to cauterize them is better after the incision. For it often happens that they return when something remains from their roots. And when they are cauterized with fire, those roots are burned and they do not return.
On the treatment of Hailstones on the eyelids of the eye.
Chapter IX.
It often happens that something occurs in the eyelids which resembles a Hailstone in its firmness and hardness, and on account of this it is called a Hailstone: and it is an aggregation of thick humidity in the upper eyelid and the lower eyelid.
The operation on them therefore is, that you consider then if it is a Hailstone arising in it, which appears from the eyelid and moves to every part, and its matter is easy.
Make therefore a fissure upon it across its width. Then pare it away from every part with ease until it comes out. Then suspend it with a hook and cut it off.
But if its excision is not prepared for you except after you penetrate the eyelid with an incision a little, then it harms the weak one in some way.
But if the fissure is large: then join it with suture and care for it until it heals.
And if it is small, no harm will come to you from it. For ointment restores it and makes it flesh.
And if the Hailstone is inclined towards the inner parts of the ear towards the inner surface, then invert the eyelid and suspend the Hailstone with a hook: besides which you will need an incision and press it from every part.
But if the eyelid is lengthened by the incision, it harms no one.
Then wash the eye after the incision of the Hailstone with salt water and care for the place with that which makes flesh until it heals the weak one.
On the treatment of Axirnaths of the eyelids.
Chapter Ten.
Axirnath is a fat which is generated in the tunics of the upper eyelid; and it most often happens to boys; and it weighs down their eyes, and catarrhs happen to them in the mornings, and they are not able to look at the light of the sun, therefore because tears hasten to them, and for that reason you see them lying down on their faces always, and sleeping upon them, and their eyelids are moist under their eyebrows, which have already been covered by swelling; and indeed the prominence of Axirnath is apparent to the eye, and when you press the place with your fingers, you feel Axirnath between them.
And the method of its operation is that the sick person should put his head in your lap.
Then take a linen cloth and prepare on it lint, and make from that lint a circle according to the quantity which contains the Axirnath from every side. Then put it on top of it and press with your fingers from every side so that the Axirnath gathers in the middle of the circle.
Then find in the middle of that moisture with a scalpel making incisions across its width, and let there not be an incision larger than that which is made in bloodletting deep.
Or it is necessary that you cut the entire skin until you reach the Axirnath, for in many dispositions it proceeds from the incision according to the shape of a white tallow candle.
Therefore extract it with the cloth which you infiltrated between your fingers to the outside; and you turn your hand to the right and left until it is dispersed; and be careful not to add to the incision so that you do not reach with the scalpel to the eye, and injure it.
But if Axirnath does not appear to you at the beginning of the incision, you add a little to the incision with ease until the Axirnath proceeds. Then extract it as we have said.
Then immerse cloths in vinegar and water, and place them over the area, and bind it with bandages.
And there are those among men who grind salt, and place it in the cavity of the fissure, so that it liquefies what remains from that moisture.
Then care for it until it heals.
But if an abscess occurs in a warm place, then treat it with soothing plasters until it heals.
Concerning the ways of softening the eyelid of the eye, that is, when the eyelid of the eye softens, from which it becomes swollen as it should not.
Chapter XI.
When hairs are born on the eyelids of the eye in addition to the course under the natural hairs, and they grow long: then they harm the eye, and cause illnesses to occur, such as constant tears, and softening of the eyelids, and leukoma, until it becomes a cause for the destruction of the eye.
And indeed Ataxmir of the eye is cured according to four methods. Or by cautery with fire. Or with a sharp medicine, according to what has been stated in the chapter on cautery. Or Ataxmir is cured by incision and suture. Or through reeds, according to what I will say.
It is necessary that you place the head of the sick person in your lap. Then turn back the eyelid of the eye with your left hand.
If therefore it is turned back, it is good. And if not: introduce a needle into it, in which there is a thread from the lower part of the eyelid, and make the needle with the thread penetrate from above, and let it be near the hairs themselves, that is, near the edge of the eyelid, in which the hairs are born: and draw the thread upwards with the eyelid, and turn it back with a rod.
Then find on the inner part of the eyelid under the added hairs with the speculum entering from the greater lacrimal to the lesser lacrimal.
Then extract the thread, and place under the eyelid a small pledget from linen, or cloth; then mark upon the eyelid with resin, so that it be like the shape of a myrtle leaf.
However, it is necessary that the shape be made according to the quantity and elevation of the eyelid; because now it is diversified in men.
For among them there are some who need a large quantity to be cut from the eyelid, according to the quantity which is softened by the eyelid.
Then with the speculum find upon the two lines which you have marked, and begin from the greater lacrimal to the lesser lacrimal, and let there be one fissure near the natural hairs, such as is the thickness of a radius.
Then introduce the hook into one of the two angles of the skin. Then flay the whole thing itself. Then unite the two lips with a suture, with a sharp needle and fine woolen thread; and cleanse away the blood, and make what runs over from the threads adhere to the two eyebrows, with certain imperceptible things, if you wish to do it; and if not, do not trouble yourself.
Then leave the suture and threads until three or four days; afterwards attend to it.
And if you wish, leave the wound without a suture and attend to it with something that dries and constricts it. For the eyelid is raised at the sealing of the wound and its union; but it will be better in the future.
This method of healing was taught by the ancients from Ataxmir.
However there is solicitude over the sick person in it and it is from a good operation, and there is no fear in it.
And another method is in Ataxmir also: and it is, that you make a sign upon the eyelid in the shape of a myrtle leaf, as we have described.
Then raise the eyelid with three hooks separate or joined together, according to this following form, then cut off what is superfluous with small forceps, according to this form, with equal incision.
But if it is not possible for you to hold a hook, and it does not align: then take a needle in which there is a thread, and insert it into the middle of the figure; and insert another thread near the larger lacrimal, and a second thread near the smaller lacrimal: and gather the threads between your fingers with equality.
Then raise your hand with equal elevation; and raise the skin above which the cotum is signed, as we have described.
Then join the two lips of the wound with suture; and care for it until it heals.
But if an abscess should occur at your incision or fissure: dry it up with cerate and such like, from soothing ointments.
And sometimes it happens to the eyelid also, that its hairs turn.
Therefore it is necessary for you to use what we have said in the incision; but the suture of both is more artful. Concerning the care of Ataxmir with dogs.
And it is, that you turn the eyelid, and split the fissure which is inside, according to what I have described. Then with art make two reeds or two slender pieces of wood: whose length is according to the length of the eyelid, and their width is less than the width of a spatula; and you have already cut into the ends of each from both sides where you hold the threads.
Then gather each with subtlety above that which overflows from the eyelid of the eye, and tighten the two reeds from both sides with a strong binding, and leave it for several days. For indeed the tightened skin will die and blacken and putrefy until it falls off by itself.
Therefore if it is delayed, then cut it with forceps. Then care for it until it heals.
When therefore it firms up, the eyelid is elevated: and the hairs do not sting.
And this is the form of two tubes, which you will make according to this figure and quantity in length and width.
Chapter Twelve.
Concerning the elevation of stinging hairs in the eye with a needle.
The operation on it is that you take a fine needle and insert into it a fine, smooth silk thread. Then you gather its ends and tie each with a fine knot: and let the length of the threads be one palm.
Then you place another fine thread underneath it briefly in a linen cloth and tie its ends if you wish.
Then let the patient place their head in your lap and be near the sun so that the operation is illuminated: because it is a subtle or minute operation.
Then introduce the needle with the thread into the root of the eyelid and make it penetrate the eyelid quickly until you pull out the natural hair from above.
Then draw the hair up to above the thread and introduce it into the thread; if there is one, or two, or three, no more.
Then draw your hand with it until the hair comes out with the thread in the eyelid with the natural hair.
But if the thread is pulled and the hair does not come out with it, pull the thread down, with the thread which you have placed in it, until the thread comes out from the upper hole, and look at it with the eye; then pull the thread and the thread and take both out and close the eye, and leave it closed for two days or three until the hair adheres to its place and flesh grows over it.
But if the hair is short, then either join to it a long hair from the natural hairs and it is raised with it, or leave it until it lengthens after a time, and then raise it.
Chapter XIII.
When this alxatrati happens to the eye, it is called Leporina.
And this alxatrati is either natural or accidental.
Accidental therefore comes from the consolidation of a wound, or an incision, or a cautery, and things similar to these.
And the method of treatment for it is that you cut open that consolidation and separate its lips, and place in it a licorice from flax, and bind it until it heals.
And it is not necessary that you use things in its treatment which dry and constrict; for if you do that, the alxatrati returns worse than it was: rather use things in it which soften, such as fenugreek: and embrocate with water in which marshmallow has been cooked, flaxseed: and an ointment of dyaquilon, which has been liquefied with some oil; and wrap it with licorice and care for it.
And the treasure of your healing is to try with all your ingenuity that it does not become consolidated according to the form which it had at first.
And this operation which we have said is nothing but a certain rectification of the Alxatrati so that the shape of the place does not return according to what it was at all.
On the treatment of the Alxatrati which is done in the lower eyelid and it is a wrinkling or contraction upwards to the right and left.
Chapter Fourteen.
This Alxatrati which occurs below is the one that is truly called Alxatrati. And it is natural and accidental.
And the accidental kind arises from a wound, or fissure, or cautery, and things similar to these.
And the way of operation on it is to take a needle with a double thread and dye it in flesh and make it penetrate from the left tear duct to the right tear duct until the thread reaches both ends of the flesh.
Then extend the flesh upwards with the needle and cut it with a broad spatula. If therefore the shape of the eyelid returns as it should be, it is good.
And if not, take a ray and place it in the fissure and turn it with the eyelid; and make two fissures on the inner side of the eyelid, and let the ends of the two fissures be from the two angles of the incision until they come together and an angle is made from them: until when they are joined the shape of them becomes this shape, which is the Greek letter lambda.
Then remove that flesh by the amount which is the sharp side below it from that which follows the eyelid. Then join the separated parts; or sew them together with wool thread.
Then care for them with what we have said, about softening medicines and cleansing ones until they are healed.
And if a laceration should occur from a fissure, and by suture or cautery: then it is necessary that you split the fissure flat under the eyelashes, even according to what preceded. Then separate between the two lips with a ligature, according to what we have said.
And the sum of the matter concerning the treatment of a laceration, whether it is above or below, is that the operation should proceed therein according to what is prepared from the form of the laceration.
For it is of many different forms; and a prudent craftsman prepares his skill in whatever way is possible for him, until he restores the shape to its natural form or near it, and himself, according to what suits him from the operation and instruments in very many arrangements.
Chapter Fifteen.
Concerning the adhesion of the eyelid of the eye with the conjunctiva or cornea.
Sometimes this consolidation happens to many people, so that the upper eyelid becomes consolidated with the white of the eye and prevents it from its natural operations.
Therefore it is necessary that you introduce the extremity of a radius under the eyelid and raise it upward: or extend it with a hook: then incise the adhesion with a fine spatulim which is not sharp in its incision but rather blunt; so that the infirm person will not be agitated at the operation, and the spatulim be removed and injure the eye with its sharpness: and let your incision be as if you were scraping away that adhesion with ease until when the eyelid returns to its natural form and the whole adhesion is healed.
Then pour salt water into the eye, with blue water washing it: or distill into it from a sieve made, from ziniar dissolved in water.
Then separate between the eyelid and the eye with a ligature of flax: and place wool infused with egg white on top of the eye: and after the third day use a sieve and apply solid dressings until it is healed.
On the cutting of the nail and the prominence of flesh of the tear duct.
Chapter Sixteen.
Know that the nail is according to two manners. Either it is nervous; and it is similar to a hard, subtle reed. Or it is not nervous, similar to frozen white humidity; which when it touches iron, or you try to take it with a hook, it is cut, and the hook does not hold fast in it.
And both kinds do not begin, except from the greater tear duct, until it goes gradually, until it covers the sight, and prohibits light and the movement of the eye.
And the manner of operation in its cutting is: that the patient should place his head in your lap. Then open his eyes, and lift the eyelid with your hand. Then collect the nail with a hook of few duplications, and extend it upward. Then take a needle, and insert into it a hair from the hairs of a horse, an ox, or a cow; and let the thread be strong, and double the extremity of the needle a little, and fix it in the middle of the nail: and penetrate it with the needle, and tie the nail with thread, and extend it upward; and skin with the hair the side of the nail which follows the pupil, as if you press it with the hair up to its last part.
Then cut the remainder at the root of the tear duct with a fine scalpel, or with small forceps; and let the natural flesh of the tear duct be released, so that a continuous flow of tears does not occur from its incision.
And sometimes the nail is extended with a hook alone; or with thread, as we have said. Then it is skinned with a gentle scalpel, of which this is the form.
And be careful that the scalpel does not touch the corneal tunic, and a rupture occurs in it, and the pupil is perforated.
Then after the incision, throw into the eye something crushed salt, or a powder made from alum, and leave it until another day. Then take care of it with what is necessary until it heals.
But if the nail is not nervous, and you cannot insert a point into it, nor does a hook take hold in it, then this kind of nail is often cured with medicines and heals as I described in the division.
But if you wish to cut it, then open the eye of the weak person and take a subtle spatula, gentle, of weak sharpness, according to this form. And shave off the nail from above with subtlety. But if you see that it becomes thinner and breaks, and the razor presses into it, then distill from your own hour into the eye a powder made from alum, or red ochre, or crushed salt: and bandage the eye until another day.
Then repeat the operation on it until the whole of it is removed. Unless there is a hot abscess in your eye, then let it be and take care of the abscess until it heals.
Then repeat the operation on it with a razor until it heals.
But if the prominence of the tear duct flesh harms the eye greatly, suspend it with a hook and cut out a part of it: and do not finish the incision so that the course of the tears does not occur.
Then distill into the tear duct red ochre or powder made from alum until it heals.
On the incision of the Alguardenic and flesh born in the eye.
Chapter 17.
Sometimes red flesh grows in the eyes of some men, superimposed or placed on top until it covers the pupil, or approaches it, or constricts upon the eyelids, and perhaps turns the eyelids outward; and it resembles a rose balustrade.
And the operation on it is also such that the sick person should place their head in your lap. Then open their eyes and gather all that flesh with suitable hooks; and hold it with one forceps or hand.
Then cut the flesh first, until all of it is finished with a scalpel. And be careful from the eye so that you do not injure it during the operation; and let your incision be with which you cut it, or with a spathe, as we have described in the incision of a nail; or with small forceps with which asebel is collected, according to the shape that will come after this chapter.
When therefore your incision is completed and all of the albuginea is removed, then fill the eye with crushed salt; or distill into it red powder and those similar to it from corrosive medicines; and place on the eye outside cotton wool with white lead: so that it is secured from a hot abscess.
But if anything remains from the albuginea, and hot blood prevails over you, and you fear a hot abscess; then leave the eye, and treat it with that which calms an abscess.
Then repeat upon it with the operation until it is healed.
And do similarly with added flesh which occurs in the eye from this kind.
However it is necessary that others treat the care of all weak eyes from a natural or accidental way by operating with iron; and do not insert your hand into any of these operations until you see that that member tolerates that operation.
On the Gathering of Pterygium from the Eye.
Chapter 18.
Pterygium is reddish veins which are woven over the eye and prevent vision from its operation; and weaken the eye with the length of days.
It is therefore necessary for you first to consider if the eye in which there is pterygium is strong and if there is no illness in it except for the pterygium; then gather its pterygium.
And it is, that you order the sick person to put his head on your lap.
Then suspend those good things with one hook, or two according to your prudence; and let the hook be subtle, of a doubling, according to this form, or let there be two hooks graded in one body, according to this form.
Then gather with subtle forceps those veins with subtlety, and wipe away the blood hour by hour, until you see those veins remove from the eye, and they are resolved; and beware lest you injure the eye with the extremities of the speculum.
And let your operation be in the middle of the day before the sun, and be firm in your operation very much: so that you do not fall except upon those veins.
And when you have finished, then distill into the eye green vitriol, or red; so that with its sharpness it corrodes what remains from the rheum.
But if it is not possible for you to gather all of it in that hour, then plaster the eye with what soothes the pain of the eye; and leave it for several days, until its pain subsides: and guard against a warm poultice; then repeat upon it according to the same manner, until it is healed.
And this is the form of the forceps.
Concerning the reduction of arrisati to the nose.
Chapter Nineteen.
Physicians call arrisati a fistula, which you cure with a cautery, or with sharp burning medicine, according to what preceded its account.
And if it does not heal, then there is no skill in it except that you cleave upon the abscess at its nativity and extract all the moisture which is in it and the pus until the mouth is exposed.
When therefore the mouth is exposed, and you see in it corruption, or blackness, then extirpate it with an instrument, whose form is this.
And it is called Asperi of the head.
Let it be made of tempered iron, and let its head be round like an awl, which shall be punctured with the puncture of a file, or the puncture of a fine aliskilfer; and place it upon the corrupted place of the bone.
Then revolve it between your fingers, and press your hand upon it a little while, until you know that corruption is already planed; and do that several times.
Then restore the place with drying astringent medications.
And if the place is consolidated and flesh arises in it, and the course of blood is cut off; and it remains for forty days and does not renew and does not fester and no incident occurs in it: then know that it has already been healed.
And if not, there is no remedy in it except to reduce the fistula to the nostril, according to this form.
And it is to uncover the second bone with iron or with a sharp medicine; and when the bone has been uncovered, then take an auger according to this form, let its iron end be triangular: and let its wood be a pyramid thinned down to the extremity, as you see.
Then place that upon the bone itself; and let it be near the lacrimal; and extend your hand a little from the eye. Then revolve your hand with the auger until it penetrates the bone, and feels it weak; through this it finds wind coming out of it, when it holds its hand over its nose.
Therefore then restore the place with astringent drying medications as I taught you.
When flesh arises and the place hardens, then the matter which was flowing outwards returns to the nose; therefore it is lighter upon the weak one.
On the Reduction of an Eye Prominence
Chapter 20.
When the eye protrudes with all its entirety, and there is neither injury nor diminution in vision; then it is necessary that you loosen what is weak; afterwards phlebotomize him; afterward place cupping glasses on the neck without scarification, but with suction.
Afterwards place upon the eye a liniment which is made from acacia, and aloe, and olibanum, and sarcocolla. Afterwards bind the eye above with a plaster with many poultices.
And the manner of the poultices is: that you take many soft cloths, and double every cloth from them over four; make many from them; then place them one upon another, according to the amount which the eye needs; then bind above them with a hard cloth with strong binding.
And repeat cupping glasses with suction without scarification.
Then remove the cupping glasses, and let the eye be bound day and night.
But if the bandage again becomes soft, then it is necessary that it be bound tightly; then remove the bandage after a day and night, as we have said.
If therefore you see the eye already having returned to its tunic, it is good; and if not, then repeat the plaster, and poultices, and binding, and cupping glasses until it is healed.
Concerning the incision of the tunic.
Chapter Twenty-One.
When a rupture occurs in the tunic of the eye, and then it protrudes and proceeds outside the eyelids like a grain of the eye, and the form of man is spoiled because of that, and vision enters into it: then the operation on it is as follows, according to what I narrate; and it is, that you insert a needle into the root of the eye from the lower to the upper part.
Then insert another needle in which there is double thread from the lacrimal part, and make it penetrate; and let the first needle go according to its disposition.
Then cut the place with a double-edged knife and tie it to the part you want to take out with thread around the point of a needle: and tighten it very much.
Then take out the needle and place on the eye wool steeped in the white of an egg: and leave it until the thread falls with the part you want to take out.
Then care for the eye with what strengthens it until the wound is healed.
And sometimes the part is compressed from every side with a circle of cloth, until the eye gains great prominence.
Then pierce it with a fine lancet, which reaches the depth of the eye, and let the white moisture run out, and let the eye descend and enter immediately: then tighten it until it is healed.
On the treatment of Alcomnatia.
Chapter XXII.
This disease which is called Alcomnatia is nothing but a humor that gathers in the eye, like descending water and it is not itself.
And the way to operate on it is to have the sick person sit on a prepared seat, or straight.
Then take his head with your hands from both sides and move it until you see the humor reach the lower parts of your eyes. Then fix it so that it does not descend and illuminate the light: and let the sick person see things as they saw them: and if it does not descend below, you know that it is water.
But if the descent of the humor is not prepared with what we have said, then make the sick person sit between your hands. Then take a fine lancet and pierce above the corneal membrane a little at the continuation of the conjunctiva with the corneal tunic, until the humor comes out.
Therefore when it comes out, then distill into the eye warm water in which you mixed honey: or water in which you already boiled fenugreek and honey: then treat it with the rest of the treatment until it is healed.
Concerning the treatment of water descending into the eye, or cataract.
Chapter XXIII.
We have already spoken of the kinds of water in division, and what is suitable for perforating by speech declaring and expounding, then take that same according to health.
Then it is necessary that you make the weak person sit between your hands square, opposite the light, near the sun; and bind his sound eye, and constrict him strongly; then pull down the eyelid of his eye with your right hand, if the eye in which there is water, is the left eye; or with your left hand, if the eye is the right.
Then take an almond with your right hand, if the eye is the left; or with your left hand, if the eye is the right.
Then place the extremity of the almond near the cornea for the thickness of a ray in the very whiteness of the eye apparent to the lachrymal minor; then press the almond with force; and you revolve with it your hand, until it penetrates into the albuginea of the eye; and feel that the almond has now reached the empty thing.
And it is necessary that the measure of the journey of the almond to the depth be the measure of the space which is from the pupil to the end of blackness; and this is the cornea of the eye. For you will see it in the very pupil by vision of the eye, because of the clarity of the tunic of the cornea.
Then place the almond up to the place in which there is water. Then press it down below with juice one after another.
If therefore the water descends immediately, then the weak person sees that above which he opens his sight immediately, and the almond in his eye.
Then rest a little. If therefore the water descends, then lay it aside second, beyond which the almond goes forth.
Therefore when it is fixed and does not rise: then extract the almond with ease: and you turn your hand gradually.
Then dissolve something from clear salt of gems in water: and bathe the eye inside with it. Then place an entire cotton plug, or wool soaked in rose oil, and egg white over the eye: and bind it with the healthy eye.
We however, and those who are of our time, place ground cinnamon with egg white over it.
But if the almond does not obey you to enter the eye because of its hardness, since it is from a man whose eye is very hard: then it is necessary that you take an instrument, which is called Alberid, which has this shape.
Therefore perforate only the conjunctiva with it, and do not insert your hand into the hole: and it is only so that you make a way for the almond to a subtle place.
Then introduce the almond, as we have said.
And at the completion of your work, prepare a decently made bedroom for the weak one, in which he sleeps on his back in a darkened house: and prohibit him from all movements, and coughing: and place his food that which soothes his nature: and let him not move his head to the right, nor to the left at all.
And let the bandage be according to its arrangement until the third day.
Then loosen it in that darkened house and you will want to test his sight: and make him see things. Then reduce the bandage until the seventh day.
And it is not necessary that he do this at the hour of healing, or immediately after the incision: rather it must be done by others: because water rises quickly through a strong gaze.
But if a hot abscess occurs: then it is necessary to loosen the eye before the seventh day: and correct it with that which soothes the abscess until it is soothed: then restore his sight, and place an oral remedy upon his face by which his vision is regulated under it for several days, himself existing in that darkened house.
Then let him go out of the house gradually, and walk about in his own rooms.
And know that in surgery a student is not excused from watching many times; then let him dare to operate.
And indeed there has come to us a certain one of those who are from Alacrech, who said that there was made in Alacrech a well perforated, by which water is drawn up.
But therefore I have not seen anyone in your land who has done that; nor have I read it in any of the ancient books; and it is possible that it is new.
And these are the forms of species from a well, so that he who does not know them may stand upon it.
Let them be made of bronze properly; and let the extremities of them be triangular with the same subtlety, and the extremity pointed.
Chapter XXIV.
On the care of flesh born on the nose.
Sometimes there are born in the nose various added fleshes, from which there is something that resembles a scorpion of many feet; and from it is cancerous flesh, stony, of a dark color; and from it is that which is soft flesh, not of a dark color.
Therefore, in that which is of these fleshes soft, not deceitful, nor cancerous, it is necessary that you make him sit weak between your hands opposite the sun, and open his nostrils, and project a hook into those fleshes: then draw them outwards, and cut what you have grasped from them with a fine sharp scalpel on one side, until you know that all the flesh has already been removed.
But if there should remain something of it whose incision is not possible: then scrape that with one of the subtle instruments easily, until nothing remains of it. But if blood should overpower you, or a hot abscess should occur: then oppose to it what is necessary: or if it is from malignant abscesses, then hurry in cauterizing it until the blood is cut off, and all the flesh is removed.
Then vinegar and water, or wine, should be poured into the nose after the incision. But if the nose opens and moisture proceeds from it as far as the palate, then you know that it is already healed.
But if the moisture does not penetrate as it should, then you know that there is flesh grown within it upon the upper bones of the nose, to which the instrument did not reach with the incision.
Then it is necessary that you insert a linen thread which has a certain thickness and many knots in it, placing a finger's breadth or less between each knot; and  you must guide the feeble-minded man so he inserts one end of the thread into his nose with a reed, or whatever else is possible for him after he has made it like an awl; and he draws out his own scent until it reaches the palate, and exits over his throat.
And indeed they often do this very thing to young boys in schools; and it is an easy matter for him who wishes to do so.
Then place the two ends of the thread, one end which exits above the nose and the other which remains in the nose. Then apply a flesh-hook with the knots on the thread. Do this until you know that the flesh is already caught with the knots of the thread.
Then pull out the thread; and after washing away the blood, place licorice which you have steeped in Egyptian ointment into the nose. Do this for three days or more, until the ointment eats away all that remains of the flesh.
Finally, at the end of the treatment, place a lead tube into the nose for several days until it heals.
But if it needs drier care, use that.
To make a head-plunger with which oils and other medicines are distilled into the nose, make it from silver or bronze; let it be like a small lamp, open like a scallop shell, and its passageway similar.
And if you wish to make a closed cannula, like a reed.
And the shell of the instrument for making a head bandage should be full, open; to which there should be a handle at its end, with which you hold it when you heat oil or whatever you wish from juices and flowing things in it.
Chapter XXV.
Very often a swelling arises at the end of the nose, and it magnifies and increases with the days until its appearance is foul.
And therefore it is necessary that you cut into it at the beginning of its appearance and eradicate all of it. Then place either a cautery or a burning medicine on the spot which takes its place.
But if the incision is delayed until it magnifies, then consider, if it is stony, hard, dark colored, and has little feeling: then do not touch it with iron; for it is a cancerous swelling.
For I have often seen those who cut into such a swelling, and great harm befell him who had it.
And if the swelling is soft to the touch, not dark-colored, and you see that incision is possible throughout all of it, then evacuate the weak part and cut into it without fear, without dread; and care for the spot with something that dries and constricts until it is healed.
Chapter XXVI.
Concerning the suturing of the nose, lip, and ear.
Know that when a break in continuity occurs in one of these cartilages, little benefit results from an operation except in some individuals.
Therefore, when it happens to someone that they have one of these injuries, you must consider if it is a recent wound with its own blood; then join the two lips of the wound with suture; then care for it until it is healed.
And if the continuity of the solution has already been separated at its lips and each part has become healthy, then it is necessary to flay all the parts from their own apparent skin until it bleeds. Then join the two lips with suture and tighten them, and sprinkle dragon's blood and ground frankincense upon it; and place upon the powders a piece of diaphanicon plaster and some other of the consolidating plasters; and leave it bound for two days or three. Then unbind it and change the medicine; and leave it until the threads fall away by themselves. Then care for it with ointment until it heals.
The method of suture is to join the continuity of the solution either with needles as we have described in suturing the abdomen or to join the solution with thread as I have taught you.
Chapter 27
On the Extraction of Nodes from the Lips
Many people indeed have hard boils resembling grains of herb, often in the inner parts of their lips; and some of these are smaller, and others larger.
Therefore it is necessary to turn the lip and cut upon each node of it, and to hang it with a hook, and to cut into it from every side.
Then fill the place after cutting with ground terebinth until the blood is stopped.
Then wash it with vinegar and care for the place with it, because of its astringency, until the wounds are healed.
Chapter 28
On the Incision of Flesh Born Upon the Gum.
Very often flesh grows upon the gum, which the ancients call abuluss
Therefore it is necessary that you hang it with a hook or hold it with forceps and cut into it at its root, and let the serum or blood flow.
Then place on the spot ground saw dust, or other astringent drying powders.
But if that flesh returns after healing, for it often does, then cut it a second time and cauterize it; for it will not return after cautery.
Chapter XXIX.
On the reason of Teeth with Iron.
Sometimes crusts rough and unsightly gather on the surfaces of teeth, both inner and outer, and sometimes they become blackened and turn yellow, and green, until corruption from them reaches the gum, and the teeth become foul through it.
It is therefore necessary for you to make the weak person sit between your hands, and place his head in your lap, and scrape the tooth with a millstone, in which crusts appear to you like sand; until nothing remains of them, and do likewise with blackness, greenness, yellowness and others until they are clean.
Therefore if what is in them from the beginning of the reason is removed it is good; and if not then repeat the reasoning on them another day, a second and third until finally you achieve your aim in that which you desire.
And know that a tooth needs razors of various shapes and many figures according to what is prepared for your operation; therefore the razor with which the tooth is scraped from the lower part is different from the razor with which it is scraped between the teeth, according to other forms.
And these shapes of razors should be prepared by you, as you see them.
Chapter XXX.
On the Extraction of Toothache.
It is necessary for you to cure the tooth from its pain with all your skill, and delay in its extraction. For there is nothing to replace it when it is extracted; because it is a noble substance: until there is no excuse for its extraction, then it is necessary when the weak person is compelled to extract it, that you strengthen and assure yourself of the aching tooth.
Very often pain deceives the sick person and he thinks it is in a strong tooth and extracts it; then the pain does not go away until the weak tooth is extracted.
For we have already seen that from the operation of those who cut veins.
Therefore when a tooth that is aching itself is shown to you, it is necessary that you make incisions around the tooth with a scalpel, in which there is some strength, until the gum is loosened from every part: then move it yourself with your fingers, or with delicate forceps at first gradually, until you shake it.
Then place large forceps in it with a good hold, and the head of the weak tooth between your knees: which you have now held well so that it does not move: then pull the tooth according to straightness so as not to break it.
But if it does not come out, then take one of those instruments and insert it under it from every side with ease, and try its motion as you did at first.
But if the tooth is perforated or corroded, then it is necessary that you fill that hole with cloth and tie it with the end of a thin ray, so that it does not break in the hour of your constriction upon it with forceps.
And it is necessary that you act with much care in the incision around the gum from every side.
And guard your strength so that you do not break it and a part of it remains, and returns upon the weak one from that evil which is greater than its first pain.
And beware lest you do what ignorant phlebotomists do in their haste and boldness concerning its eradication beyond what we have related.
For very often they cause great infirmities to happen upon men, which are more difficult to break and the roots of it all remain or some of them, or that they extract it with some bones of the jawbone, as we have testified many times.
Then after its removal it should be washed with wine, or with vinegar and salt.
If it should happen that there is a flow of blood from the places (for this often happens) then apply something from zedoary and fill the place with it: and if not, cauterize it if you do not have zedoary.
The shape of the forceps which moves a tooth shall first be of long shanks, short handles, thick, so that they do not bend when pressed upon the tooth: let them be as you see with thick handles so that when you squeeze them they do not give way nor double: of short shanks and made of good iron or of well-made steel, straight shanks; and at their ends shall be teeth, some which enter others and constrict with a proper force.
And sometimes the ends are made like the beak of a bird which is called a stork and they are of strong constriction.
Chapter Thirty-One.
On the removal of teeth, and the extraction of fractured bones of the jaw.
When there remains something broken at the removal of a tooth then it is necessary to put a poultice with butter upon the place for one or two days until the place is softened; then introduce an elevator, or forceps into it, whose ends are assimilated to the beak of a bird which is called a stork. This is the shape of these forceps.
Let their ends be made like a file inside: or like a small sickle.
But if it does not yield to you with these forceps then you must carefully cut the flesh over the root and insert an instrument called a small lever, whose shape is this:
Let it have a short shank, slightly thick, and let it not be wet so that it does not break.
If therefore the root comes out through this, it is good; and if not, you must help with these other instruments whose shapes are these.
The shape of the first triangular extremity, in which there is a certain thickness.
The shape of the subtle triangular extremity.
And with other instruments and tools which preceded in the matter of teeth.
It should also be joined with this instrument which is assimilated to a large hook, of this form, a twisted triangular extremity, in which there is a certain thickness so that it does not break, and let it not be imbibed.
And know that dental instruments are many, and likewise the remaining instruments are not counted, and a prudent artisan, subtle in his art, finds an instrument for himself according to what the operation and illnesses themselves signify to him.
Because from illnesses there are those to which the ancients did not say an instrument, on account of the diversity of their species.
But if the jawbone breaks from the mandible or from other bones of the mouth, or it rots: then carefully examine it in its place with what suits it from instruments and forceps which I said in the extraction of roots, and join it with a gesture, which has this form.
Let it have a certain gradual thickness so that it is strung or held with the bone and does not flee and wander until the bone comes out; and restore the place with appropriate medicines for that.
But if there is a small putrefaction in the bone, then scrape it from its putrefaction and care for it until it heals.
Chapter Thirty-Two.
Of the Locking of Teeth Prominent Above Others.
When teeth protrude not according to their natural course, it becomes ugly by that: and especially when it happens in women, and girls, namely, those of marriageable age.
Therefore, it is necessary for you to consider. If a tooth arises after another and is not positioned, or it is possible to file it down, but the tooth cannot be filed: then eradicate it. And if it is joined with another tooth, then cut it out with this instrument, which has this shape.
It is like a small beak and is made of hard iron, with a very sharp point.
Let your incision be such that you cut it over many days, and because of the hardness of the tooth, so that another is not moved from the teeth.
But if it is prominent and exposed for filing: then file it with a file of hard iron, which has this shape.
Let it be entirely of iron, and its handle of very small points, like the file with which needles are made: with which the tooth should be filed gradually over many days with ease, so that the tooth is not moved and falls out; then smooth it, and finally eradicate it with certain razors.
But if there is a tooth from which a part breaks off and injures the tongue: then it is necessary that you file it also until the roughness of that fracture is removed and it is made even; and smoothed so that it does not injure the tongue nor corrupt speech.
Chapter Thirty-Three.
Concerning the Retention of Teeth with Gold Wires.
When the front teeth suffer a concussion and jolting from a blow or fall, and the infirm person cannot bite down on it so that it does not come loose; and you have treated them with astringent medicines, but the treatment has not been effective: then there is a plan to bind them with a gold wire, because silver turns green and rots after a few days, but gold never changes.
And let the wire be medium in subtlety and thickness according to the amount that fits between the teeth.
And the form of retention indeed is, that you take a wire and insert a double length of it between two healthy teeth; then weave with the two ends of the wire among the loose teeth, whether there is one or more, until you reach with the wire to a healthy tooth on the other side. Then repeat the texture up to the part from which you began; and tighten your hand with subtlety and wisdom, until it is completely removed; and let your constriction where you are stringing the thread be at the roots of the teeth, so that it does not come loose.
Then cut off the superfluous ends of the thread with forceps; and join both, and turn them over with your hand; and conceal them between a healthy tooth and a moved tooth, so that it does not injure the tongue; then let it remain thus tightened.
But if they become loosened, or broken, tighten them with another thread, and it will stand thus for the whole time.
And this is the form of Teeth, and the figure of the retention of two sound teeth, and two moved teeth, as you see.
And sometimes it happens that one tooth, or two return after falling out and into their places, and are retained as we have described, and remain; and this is not done except by a craftsman, prudent, and experienced.
And sometimes one of the vaccine bones is adapted; and it is made into the shape of a tooth, and placed in its place, from which the tooth is fortified and tightened, as we said; and it remains, and is used with it for a long time.
Chapter Thirty-Four.
Sometimes this ligament that occurs under the tongue is either natural, with which a man is born, or accidental, from a wound that has already consolidated.
And the operation on it is that you open the mouth of the infirm one in his head in your lap, and raise his tongue; then cut that nervous ligament across its width, until the tongue is loosened from its restraint.
But if there is something hard and knotty in it, and it is from the consolidation of a wound; then project a hook into it, and pierce it across its width, until you strike the ligament, and the knot is loosened.
And take care that there is not a fissure into the depth of the flesh, and you cut an artery there, and bleeding should occur.
Then it should be washed in the trace of the incision with rose water, or vinegar, or cold water.
Then place under the tongue a ligature from linen, which the weak person should hold in every night so that it does not become consolidated on the second day.
But if bleeding should occur, then place over the spot boiled zedoary. But if blood overcomes you, then cauterize the place with a lenticular cautery, which is suitable for that; then care for it with the remaining care until it heals.
Chapter Thirty-Five.
Sometimes it happens that under the tongue there occurs a swelling which is similar to a small frog, preventing the tongue from its natural operation; and perhaps it grows so much that it fills the mouth.
And the operation on it is that you open the weak person's mouth before the sun, and consider the swelling.
If therefore you see it of a dark color, or black, hard, to which the weak person does not find sensation; then do not touch it, because it is cancer.
And if it is inclined towards whiteness, in which there is moisture; then project into it a hook, and cut it with a fine scalpel, and free it from every part. And if blood overcomes you in the hour of your operation, then place over it boiled zedoary until the blood is cut off; then return to your operation until you extract it with all its complement.
Then wash it with vinegar and salt; and care for it with the remaining suitable care for that until it heals.
On the Care of a Swelling of Two Tonsils.
Chapter Thirty-Six.
Sometimes there occur between the throat glands, which resemble almonds, that protrude outward; and they are called two tonsils: when you treat these as we have said in the division, and they do not heal, then consider.
Therefore if it is a hard abscess, dark in color, with little sensation: then do not touch it with iron.
And if it is reddish in color, and its root is thick: then do not touch it even with iron, fearing a flow of blood, but rather let it alone until it matures. And then either you will pierce it, or it will break open on its own.
And if it is white in color, round, and its root is slender: then it is necessary to cut into it.
And the operation on it is, that you consider before your operation whether its hot abscess has quieted with rest completely, or diminishes somewhat: then therefore make the sick person sit before the sun, and let his head be in your lap; and open his mouth, and let a servant receive between his hands, and press his tongue down with an instrument, which has this shape.
Let it be made of silver, or bronze, that is thin like a knife.
When therefore you press with it the tongue, and the abscess is shown to you, and your sight falls upon it: then take a hook, and fix it in the tonsil, and draw it outward as much as you can, besides that you pull out with it some of the tunics.
Then cut into it with an instrument which has this following shape. It resembles forceps: but nevertheless let its points be curved, so that the opening of each one is in a straight line with the other acutely; therefore take care that it be made of tempered iron, or aluminum imbued.
But if this instrument is not present, then cut into it with a scalpel, which has this shape. Let it be sharp on one side and not sharp on the other.
And after you have cut one amygdala, cut the other according to this same kind of incision.
Then after the incision, let the patient gargle with cold water, or with vinegar and water.
But if there should happen to him a flow of blood, let him gargle with water in which pomegranate rinds have been boiled, or myrtle leaves and those like them from astringents, until the flow of blood is stopped; then care for him until he is healed.
And sometimes other abscesses arise in the throat besides the two amygdales. Therefore cut them according to what I said in the incision of the two amygdales equally.
And I myself have already treated a woman for an abscess that had arisen between her throat, which was dragging it to suffocation, with few senses, because it had almost closed the throat; and the woman was breathing from a narrow passage, and had already forbidden her both food and drink of water; and she had already come to death if she had remained one day or two days.
And the abscess had risen so much that it had sent out two branches until they had gone out above the two nostrils.
Therefore I hurried quickly and fixed a hook into one of them. Then I drew it out, and a large portion was drawn from it.
Then I cut it where I grasped it from the nostril.
Then I did that same thing to her from the other nostril.
