Then I opened her mouth and pressed her tongue; and afterward I fixed a hook into the abscess itself; then I cut off a part of it, and there did not flow from it except for a little blood. Therefore the woman's throat was completely opened, and she immediately began to drink water, and then took food.
Therefore I did not cease to cut from that abscess many times over a long period; and the abscess would succeed in the place where I was cutting, until it was prolonged excessively and came out with her.
Therefore I ingeniously cauterized the abscess between the throat.
So it remained from addition; then I walked away, and I did not know what God did with it after me.
On the incision of an abscess of the uvula.
Chapter Thirty-Seven.
When catarrh descends to the uvula and becomes abscessed and is long, then it is called perpendicular; and if it is in the lower part thick, round, then it is called a uva.
When it is treated with what we said in the division, and the treatment does not avail; and you see that the hot abscess has subsided from it, and it is subtle: then it is necessary that you incise it.
And that which is gathered round from it, and there is no length to it, and it is bloody, or dusky colored, or black, and there is no sensation to it: then it is necessary that you avoid the incision of it. For in this there is fear over the weak one.
Therefore it is necessary when you see it according to the manner which I said from its whiteness and length, that you make the weak one sit before the sun; and press their tongue with an instrument of which narration preceded: then fix a hook in the uvula and draw it downward: and incise it with one of two instruments which I said in the incision of two tonsils.
And it is necessary that you not incise from it except what is added beyond the natural limit without addition.
For if you incise from it more, you injure the voice and speech.
Then after the incision use what we narrated in the incision of two tonsils; and care for it until it heals.
But if the weak one is terrified from the incision, then it is necessary that you use ingenuity in its cauterization without fear and dread.
And the manner of cautery in this is not except with sharp water; and it is, that you place the head of the weak one in your lap. Then press his tongue with the instrument we have mentioned. Then take from the water which I told you in the chapter on cauterization and mix it with a calx that has not been extinguished and make it neither thick nor thin and fill the cavity of this instrument, which has this shape.
Let the extremity in which the medicine is placed have a cavity like the cavity of a ray's ear.
And place the instrument with the medicine on his tongue; and let the sick person lie on his side so that saliva runs from his mouth from within the medicine, so that nothing descends from it to his throat and harms him.
Then hold your hand with the medicine. And you from the first place it on his tongue for half an hour, until you see it already blackened and the bite of the medicine subdued.
And if you wish to take cotton and wrap it around the extremity of the ray and infuse the cotton with the medicine and insert the ray with the cotton into the cannula from above until the cotton adheres to the tongue.
Do that many times until you achieve what you desire from the cauterization of the vein. Then let it go; for it will dry up and fall off after three or four days.
And if it is necessary for you to iterate the medicine, iterate it; and after cauterization cleanse around the vein with cotton steeped in butter, and purify what is around it from the medicine; and wash with cold water.
And let it be treated externally with embrocations, and internally with gargles until it is healed.
This therefore is a healthier kind of treatment than incision and further from fear.
And sometimes the tongue is also cured with something more subtle than cautery and incision; for it is cured with fumigation according to this method. Take calamint, and hyssop, and origanum, and rue, and sage, and chamomile, and abrotanum, and herbs like these; and put them all together, or some of them in a pot, and submerge them with vinegar, and let them boil; and the pot should be smeared with clay neatly; and there should be a hole in the middle of the pot, above which an instrument concave is to be placed according to this manner.
Let it be made of silver, or bronze; and insert the extremity, in which there is a pomegranate seed, into the mouth of the sick person, until the vapor rises to the uvula above the cannula, until the uvula is darkened much.
Then repeat over it many times, until it dries up.
And beware, lest you do this treatment at the beginning of the arrival of an abscess. For it most often adds to the abscess; and it is not necessary for you to do that except at the decline of its heat.
But if this instrument is not present to you, then take a cannula, and compose bark of the fig tree in its extremity; so that the mouth of the sick person is not burned; for the bark of the fig tree prevents the vapor from burning the mouth; and this is from good treatment with safety.
On the Extraction of Thorns, and Bones from the Throat.
Chapter XXXVIII.
Often a bone, or a fishbone, or something else adheres in the throat.
Therefore it is necessary for you to extract what of them appears above which the sight falls upon, after you press the tongue with an instrument toward the sun, and it is made clear to you what is in the throat.
And what does not appear to you and is concealed in the throat, it is necessary for you to make the sick person vomit before food is digested in the stomach. For perhaps the attached thing will go out through vomiting.
Either the weak person swallows a piece of turnip or a lettuce root or he swallows a mouthful of dry bread; or he takes a piece of dried sea sponge which is soft and ties it on a thread, then swallows it. And when therefore it reaches the place where the spines or bone are, and is drawn out, then it comes out.
If therefore it does not come out with what we have said, then use an instrument of lead according to this form.
Let it be thicker than the radius a little and let there be a curve at its extremity which it introduces into his throat with ease; and let he himself have his head elevated upward.
And let him beware of touching his epiglottis so that he does not have a coughing fit and he pushes with it the mouth or the spine or the doctor introduces it himself with his hand.
But what the weak person introduces is better because of his knowledge of the place of the thing attached and he pushes it down or pulls his hand up with the instrument all the way according to the quantity prepared for him until it comes out.
On the Extraction of a Leech.
Chapter XXXIX.
When a leech is applied with what I said in the division on treatment with medicines and it does not detach itself then consider in the throat of the weak person in front of the sun or by the sun after you have pressed his tongue with the instrument which I related to you.
If therefore your sight falls upon the leech extract it with a small hook or with delicate forceps carefully.
And if it is not possible to do so with them then take a concave tube and introduce it into the throat of the weak person up to near the throat; then introduce into the concavity of the tube iron heated with fire. Do that many times and let the weak person abstain from all water for the whole day.
Then he should take a cup full of cold water and open his mouth in it; and gargle with it, but not swallow anything from it; and stir the water with his hand one hour after another. For the leech immediately falls when it feels the water.
But if it does not come out with what we have described, then fumigate the weak one with bugs, or with asafoetida with the instrument which I have described in the fumigation of the uvula.
Do that many times, for it will fall by itself.
And the method of operation in fumigation is that you should take a jar, in which there are live plums with fire; and let the jar be covered with a lid, in the middle of which there is a hole; and let the end of the instrument be placed in that hole.
Then direct the fumigation, and let the weak one place his mouth at the end of the cannula, and close his mouth so that the fumigation does not escape until you know that the fumigation has already reached the leech. For the leech immediately falls.
But if it does not fall, then repeat the fumigation many times.
And let the weak one endure thirst, and eat salt, and garlic, and not drink water.
Therefore it is necessary that he comes out with this regimen.
And this is the form of the instrument with which the leech is extracted from the throat when it falls on top of the vision.
It is similar to forceps, as you see: except that it has this curve which is inserted into the throat. And its end is similar to the beak of a bird which is called a stork, in which there is roughness like a file; when it grasps something, it does not let go of it at all.
On the opening of abscesses, and their incision, and care in general.
Chapter 40.
There are many kinds of abscesses, of many types, according to how we remember them, namely one, and one in this book; and they are different in their perforation and incision in two ways.
One of which is from the type of abscess itself, and because it consists of humors.
And the second way is because of the places where they occur in the body.
For an abscess occurring on the head is different from an abscess occurring in the anus; and an abscess occurring in a fleshy place is different from an abscess occurring in the joints; and each of them has its own judgment according to its operation.
And of abscesses, there are some which ought not to be perforated except after the ripening of blood in them and its completion; and of these, there are some which ought to be perforated while they are raw and do not ripen according to their completion.
Such as abscesses which are near the joints.
For when an abscess occurs near a joint and is prolonged to be its own, until it putrifies what is around it, perhaps the ligaments or nerves of that joint are corrupted; therefore, it is the cause of closer contraction of that limb.
Or there is an abscess at a principal limb; for if you delay its perforation until it ripens, it will harm either that or the principal limb through it.
Or it is near the anus, therefore perforate it while immature; and you ought not to perforate a raw abscess, not of complete digestion, except that which is near the anus, lest it putrefy deeply and extend to the interior parts of the anus and become a fistula, or becomes incurable at its end.
You ought to know the hour of perforating an abscess which has ripened according to its completion; and it is at sedation of pain, recession of fever, and diminution of redness, and throbbing, and sharpness of the head of the abscess; and the other signs.
And it is necessary that you make the same perforation in the lower place of the abscess, and of the more prominent eminence; and let the perforation be directed in length of the body, if the abscesses are in the parts of the hands and feet, and places of tendons, and cords, and nerves, and arteries, and finally in all equal places where there is no reflection.
In those which are reflected, proceed with the perforation according to that member.
But when an abscess is in fleshy places: then it is better that you leave its perforation alone until its digestion is confirmed, as we said according to the complement.
For if you pierce it before that, the course of the virus is prolonged from it, and there is much filth and foulness, and perhaps the lips of it will become hardened, and its depth.
And certain abscesses are perforated according to the width of the body as needed: or according to that which the member requires.
And it is necessary that you administer a small perforation in small abscesses, and a large perforation in large abscesses: or many incisions through similarity of the size of the abscess.
And there are abscesses from which it is necessary that the skin be concave: and it is cut when already made like cloth, and made at the end, which already is dead: such as happens in many, like a hernia, and buboes.
And from those are those which are cut with an incision having three angles. And from those is one from which it is cut like the figure of a myrtle leaf.
Like an abscess of the groin.
And from those are those in which a round incision and a crescent incision are administered: and similar to those from incisions.
But that which does not have a head, such as soft, superficial abscesses, it is necessary that you pierce it with a superficial perforation, or only a flat one.
And it is necessary, when the abscess is large and has already collected much pus, and you pierce it, that you do not hurry and draw out all the pus at that time; rather, draw out a part of it. Then stitch up the abscess until another day. Then again draw out some pus.
Do this many times according to the degree until it comes out; especially if the patient is weak of body, or a pregnant woman, or a young child, or an old man: for the animal spirit is often resolved with the exit of pus; and perhaps the patient dies, and you do not perceive.
Therefore be very careful in this chapter.
And after you pierce it by which you pierce these abscesses, it is necessary that you wash the wound and consider. And if therefore the wound of the abscess is small, or the incision is flat, then apply a bandage from linen, or old cotton. And if the abscess is large, and there are many fissures from the perforation, it is necessary then that you insert into every fissure a bandage until they reach one another.
And if you have cut off a part of the skin in the abscess, or hollowed it around: then it is necessary that you fill it with old cotton, or as when cloth is woven, or shaved with linen fluff without moisture; and tighten it until the third day.
Then throw it off, and care for it with what is necessary from ointment until it heals.
But if bleeding should occur during the hour of your operation, then apply cold water and vinegar, after a cloth is soaked in them; and place it upon the place of bleeding many times.
But if the bleeding persists, then it is necessary that you administer treatment and care, and powders, which I have related in many places of this book of ours, and by division.
If, however, it is wintertime and the place of the abscess is full of nerves, then you must pour warm broth with wine and oil upon it and place them over the area.
And if it is summertime and the areas are fleshy, then you must place compresses soaked in water and oil, or wine and oil, upon them entirely cold. Until the third day, as we have said, then you must open the abscess and cleanse it, and administer to its cure what is suitable for it until it is healed.
This, therefore, is all that is necessary from the knowledge of treating abscesses universally.
According to the path of distinction, I have already spoken about every abscess and how its cure is approached chapter by chapter individually.
Chapter 41.
On the incision of abscesses which occur on the skin of the head.
Small abscesses occur on the skin of the head, and they are of the kinds of nodules, which contain sheaths that are like sacks to them, as if they were poultices of hens.
And there are many kinds of them.
Some of them are fatty; some contain moisture like slime; some contain moisture like curds and pulps, and those similar; and some are hard and stony.
And in the incision of all of them and their extraction there is no fear, as long as an artery does not oppose during their incision.
And the operation in incising them is to test them with an instrument whose shape comes after this, which is called an Intromissum, until you know what it contains.
For if it is one that contains moisture, then dry it with a longitudinal incision, shallow in depth, according to this form.
And begin with an incision from line b to line g. When, therefore, the moisture is evacuated, then remove the skin that contains it and do not leave anything behind in it.
For it often returns if something remains from it.
Then immerse the dressing in Egyptian ointment, if it is present; and if not, in water and salt; and fill the wound with it, and leave it until another day. For it will corrode what remains from the cyst.
Then repeat the dressing on it with a second Egyptian ointment, and a third, and if it does not digest with that, until you are confident that nothing remains from the cyst, then heal the wound with ointment until it is cured.
But if the abscess contains a fatty node, then dry it according to this manner, with an incision in the shape of a cross; and insert hooks between the two lips of the incision, and flay it from every part; and exert your strength in extracting the tunic which contains it.
But if an artery opposes you, then do what I will tell you after.
And do likewise in a stony abscess, from an incision and the same treatment, according to what we have said.
And the incision over a stony abscess is easier; because it has little blood and moisture. And sometimes there are certain of these abscesses which are on the head in some people with which there is no moisture at all.
For it is this: because I cut on an abscess on the head of a young woman, and found the abscess like a solid and hard stone, rough, white, which could not be broken; and if anyone struck it with something throwing, he would wound himself.
But in other abscesses which happen on the head, except these abscesses which happen on children's heads, and at the roots of the ears, let the incision of all of them be a superficial one.
Or always make a perforation of them from their lower part so that the course of blood is relieved downwards.
Then care for it with what suits them from treatment.
On the Section of Strangulation in the Neck.
Chapter Forty-Two.
These swellings often occur in the neck, and under the armpits, and groins; and sometimes they occur in the rest of the body.
And regarding those which occur in the neck from them, they are one, and many; and they are generated from each other; and every swelling from them is within its own tunic, just as it is in knots and swellings of the head, as we have narrated.
And the kinds of these swellings are many. And of them indeed are stony ones; and of them are those which contain moisture; and of them are malignant ones, which do not obey treatment.
Therefore, in that which you see of them of good disposition to the touch, whose proximity to the color of the skin is manifest, and moves in every direction, and is not joined with the nerves of the neck, nor with the gullet, nor with the artery, and is not deep: it is necessary that you section it superficially from the upper part to the lower part of the body from line, b. to line, g.; and flay it from every side; and extend two lips of skin with a hook, or with many hooks if needed by it, as we have said in swellings of the head, and extract it gradually.
And it is for caution that a vein or nerve not be cut.
And let the scalpel not be very sharp so that it does not add to your hand with the incision, or disturb the sick person, and you cut what is not necessary.
But if you cut a vein or artery, or it prevents you from the operation, then place in the wound chewed oak, or certain powders which stanch blood, and constrict the wound, and leave it until the sharpness of the swelling subsides, and the wound softens: or until it begins and wants to putrefy.
For blood is severed by a tunic, therefore return to your operation until you finish it.
Then inquire with your index finger if anything remains from the small ligatures; therefore cut them and clean them.
But if there is a large vein in the root of the ligature then it is necessary that you do not cut that ligature from its root; rather it is necessary that you tie it with a strong doubled thread, and let it fall off by itself without harm.
Then fill the wound with old fat which you have already submerged in Egyptian ointment. Then take care of it yourself.
If the whole ligature is cut, then it is necessary that you bring together the two lips of the wound and sew it up immediately after you see that there is absolutely no excess remaining.
But if you see that excess remains because of the size of the ligature then it is necessary for you to intend to cut its root and remove it; and administer the suture which we have described.
And that which the ligature contains of moisture, perforate it with a superficial perforation so that the place of its maturity appears to you and place the perforation from that which follows below the body as we have said.
Then administer after the perforation lint with ointment and the like so that it corrodes what remains from decay until when you see that the wound is already cleansed; then take care of it with a flesh-generating ointment until it is healed.
Chapter 43.
On the incision of the epiglottis from an abscess within the throat by accident.
The ancients said this incision was made on the epiglottis and I have not seen anyone in our land who has done it; and this is the writing of their speech.
But in those who have synanche, it is necessary that the epiglottis be cut; since benefit is not made through it because all the veins and lungs are weak.
In those who have a hot apostema in the mouth, or throat, or both almonds, when there is no pain in the canal, it is necessary to use the section of the epiglottis, to avoid the destruction which arises from pre-speech.
Therefore, when we wish to do this, it is necessary to cut the epiglottis under three or four circles of the epiglottis, with a small incision across its width, in the space between two circles, so that the incision be made in flesh, not cartilage; and indeed this place is suitable for the incision because it is private of flesh, and blood vessels are distant from it.
But if the practitioner is timid, then it is necessary for him to extend the skin of the throat with a hook. Then he should grasp the skin until he reaches the canal; divert the blood vessels if he sees anything from them.
Then he should cut the flesh as we have described; and it is shown above the incision of the canal from phlegm that issues forth from it, with what is broken up, and cessation of voice.
And leave the wound open for some time.
When therefore the hour arrives in which pre-speech is feared, bring together the two lips of the wound of the skin, and sew it alone without cartilage.
Then administer medicines that promote the growth of flesh until it heals.
Says Albucasis. The sum of this discourse which we have recounted is not explained except when they see that one of these apostemas has already coalesced the throat of the sick person; and he is raised up on the brink of death, and desires to interrupt his breathings: they go to cut the epiglottis so that the sick person may breathe upon the wound with a certain respiration and be saved from death; and for this reason they prescribed leaving the wound open until the malice of the sickness is consumed, and its malice is three days, and as if it were itself; then therefore they prescribed suturing the wound and taking care of it until it heals.
And what I myself have seen is that one maidservant took a knife and thrust it into her throat, and with it she struck part of the pulmonary canal.
And I was summoned to treat her, and I found her groaning, as is the groan of one dying.
I therefore examined the wound, and I found that little blood had come out from it. I was therefore perplexed, because she had not severed a vein, nor a sinew; and wind was escaping from the wound.
I hastened therefore and sewed up her wound, and I cared for it until it was healed; and nothing happened to the maidservant except hoarseness in her voice; and after a few days she returned to a better state of health.
Therefore I say from this: that there is no fear in the incision of the epiglottis.
On the incision of a hernia of the throat.
Chapter Forty-four.
This swelling called a hernia of the throat is a large swelling, according to the color of the body; and it is common in women.
And it occurs in two ways: either it is natural; or it is accidental.
In the natural kind, there certainly is no artifice.
The accidental kind, however, occurs in two ways, one of which is similar to fatty nodules. And the other way is similar to a swelling that arises from the knotting of an artery. And there is fear in this case and it should not be presumed to be incised with iron unless it is small; and you must examine it and investigate it with a probe. If therefore you find it similar to a fatty nodule and it is not suspended with any veins; then cut it out as nodules are cut; and extract it with what it contains from the cyst if it is in a cyst; and if it is not, then carefully examine all of it. Then care for the place with what is necessary for healing, until it heals.
Chapter Forty-Five.
On the Section of Kinds of Nodes.
There are many kinds of nodes, and I have already mentioned all their kinds in the division; and it is necessary that I tell here the difference between a node and an excrescence: since there is a doubt-causing one.
I say therefore that when there is an excrescence, there is heat and fever and violent pains until the boiling of the superfluity subsides and putrefaction is completed; then indeed the fevers and heat subside.
But with a node there is no heat, nor fever, nor pains, and it contains them in sacs sifachin, which is their own sac, and it is according to the color of the body: and the beginning of them is like a chickpea, and it becomes like a date stone, larger and smaller.
And they are according to two kinds: either fatty; or containing moisture. And the colors of the moisture are many, according to what I said in the division.
Therefore it is necessary that when you arrive at the treatment of a node, you should examine it and inquire into it first with an instrument called an intromissor, according to what its form will come to be in the chapter which is after this.
And the method of inquiry for abscesses and all nodes is to take this instrument and insert it into the damper place which you find in the abscess; and turn your fingers with it gradually until you know that the instrument has already penetrated the skin. Then finally measure your hand according to the measure of the size of the abscess.
Then draw out the intromissor and consider that which comes forth in its trace.
If therefore moisture flows out, whatever the color may be; then dry it with a superficial incision, according to what I said in the other abscesses.
And if the moisture does not flow out in the track of the Intromissi, then know that it is fat; therefore cut upon it a section in the shape of a cross, according to this form as I have taught you: and suspend it with hooks, and flay the skin from every part with ease; and keep a kistin if you can over it, so as to draw out the entire one healthy with a knot.
But if the kistin is broken at the operation and it is not possible to extract it whole, for this often happens, then draw it out in pieces until nothing remains of it.
For if a little or much of it remains, the Knot returns most often.
And if it overcomes you and a little remains from it: then fill the wound upon your completion with certain corrosive sharp powders; and constrict the wound and put upon it something that sits hot poultice, and care for it with the rest of the cure until it heals.
But if the Knot is large, then cut its two lips, and care for it as it incorporates.
But if a vein contrary to you pulsates or does not pulsate, then hurry and fill the place with triturated zegi, and leave it constricted for two days or three until the wound putrefies and the ebullition of blood subsides; then return to cutting what remains from the Knot.
Chapter XLVI.
Concerning external instruments for incision and perforation.
Of these are the forms of Intromissis and they are of three species.
For among them is a large one, and among them is a medium one, and among them is a small one.
Let them be made of sharp square-ended iron so they quickly enter the abscess.
And these are the forms of Experimenters, or rather, perforators, and one of them is called a beris, that is, a perforator, and they are of three species.
For from them there is a large one, a medium one, and a small one.
It is agreed that they should be investigated through them abscesses, and fistulas, and buboes from that which is between them of bones.
And others are made round, smooth, and polished, like large needles with which cushions are sewn, namely from citron, or from bronze, or iron, or silver.
And the best of them are those that are made from citron.
And experience is also made from black lead, which are suitable for experimenting with fistulas, whose concavity has reflections, so that they are reflected with their smoothness with those reflections.
And they are again of three kinds. Because from them are long ones, medium ones, and short ones, according to the amount of the width of the fistula and its tightness.
Form of Hooks, and they are of many kinds.
For from them are simple ones, namely those which have a single curve: and they are of three kinds as you see, Large, Medium, and Small.
And from them are blind hooks: and they are of three kinds.
And from them are hooks having two curves, and they are of three kinds: and from them are hooks having three curves, and they are of three kinds: and all these are necessary in their place.
And these are the forms of Knives, with which nodes and abscesses are cut and scraped: and they are of three kinds: because from them there is a large one, a medium one, and a small one.
Their tips with which the cutting is done are sharp, other tips are not sharp: and they are not put thus unless so that with them there is relief in the scraping of nodes when there is fear of cutting a vein or nerve: and so that the weak are healed through them and find a little tranquility from the burning they experience at the scraping of an abscess.
They are made of bronze, and they are similar to a razor, with which alcohol is made: and at the wide end there is a hidden spatulated point: and through it runs inwards, and outwards when you wish, as you see.
The spatulated forms which are concealed between the fingers at the perforation of abscesses: and the sick do not perceive them: and they are of three kinds, large, medium, and small.
Forms for drawing out wind with which the flow of blood is severed: and they are of three kinds, large, medium, and small.
Let them be made of bronze, or brass, round, to a length a little as you see: and let them be fine.
And it is necessary that these kinds of wind-drawing instruments be large and small with you: so that you may sever blood quickly when necessary, and then when medicine is not present to you.
However, do not use them in the cutting of blood in every place in the body, and do not use them except in fleshy places: such as the calves of the leg, and the hip, and the calves of the arms, and the breasts, and the belly, and the groin, and similar places to these, from the fleshy calves, moist.
And sometimes they are made into other small instruments similar to hazelnut shells, according to their shape: with which blood is cut off when it flows from the place of phlebotomy, or at an incision of a vein, or artery: and sometimes they are made round according to this shape.
Chapter 47.
On the treatment of the breasts of men which resemble the breasts of women.
Sometimes the nipples of certain men swell up during sleep (that is, when they begin to be excited in sleep) until they resemble women's breasts: and they remain fetid abscesses.
Therefore, whoever shuns that, must cut above the nipples with a lunar section, according to this form from line b to line g. Then remove all the fat and skin. Then fill the wound with flesh-colored medicine. Then join the two lips of the wound with suture and care for it until it heals.
But if the nipple descends downwards and softens because of its size, as happens to women: then it must be divided on its two upper sides with two similar lunar incisions, each of which is joined with the other at the ends of each, until there is a larger line containing the smaller one, according to this form from line b to line g. Then remove the skin that is between the two lips and take away the fat and administer what we said about suture and powders, which are necessary for healing, until it heals.
But if you cannot complete the incision as it ought to be made, because of the restlessness of the patient or because of bleeding: then it is necessary to fill the wound with cotton soaked in a corrosive ointment and leave it until it corrodes what remains of the fat. Then care for it until it heals.
On puncturing an abscess which occurs under the nipple.
Chapter XLVIII.
These abscesses which occur under the nipple are of a kind of hard Strophulus, which contains cysts and some that contain humors.
Therefore those which are of the kind that contain humors must be cut with a lunar incision according to this form from line b to line g. And when all that is in the abscess is evacuated as pus, then fill it with old cotton and leave it until the next day.
Then remove the cotton and treat it with a suitable ointment until it heals.
If the abscess is of the kind of knots called Strophulas, then split it open above it, as we said in the section on Strophulas equally.
But if the wound extends so as to discharge pus, then apply a cautery to it, according to what we said previously.
Chapter XLIX.
On the incision of an abscess which occurs because of an artery and vein.
When an artery is ruptured and the skin above it becomes consolidated, an abscess often results; similarly, a vein may also develop an abscess and swelling.
The signs by which it is recognized that an abscess and swelling are due to an artery or vein are these: when the abscess is due to an artery, it is long, aggregated deep within the body; and when you press the abscess with your fingers, you feel as if there is a grating sensation.
And that which is due to a vein is round and evident on the surface of the body.
To make an incision on this abscess is an error, especially when it is under the armpits, groins, neck, and in many parts of the body; and it is very large. Therefore, it is necessary that its treatment be performed with fire, and what arises from them also at the extremities or in the head must be separated.
On that which arises from the swelling of an artery opening, make an incision in the skin along its length. Then open the incision with hooks, and then expose the artery; free it from its surrounding tissues until it is exposed.
Then insert a needle under it and make it penetrate to the other side, and tie off the artery with two threads in two places, according to what I taught you in the extraction of the two arteries which are in the temples.
Then puncture the area between the two ligatures with a scalpel until all the blood in it has exited and the abscess is resolved.
Then apply a treatment that produces pus until the ligaments fall away; afterwards treat it with suitable ointments until it is healed.
If it is an abscess due to a vein being cut, then you must hold with your hand as much as possible from the abscess together with the skin. Then insert a needle under the place which you held with your hand and make it penetrate; and let there be in it a double thread until you draw it out from the other side. Then tie the abscess together with a good ligature, as I told you in regard to a ligature for a vein, from two parts with two ends of thread.
But if you fear that the thread will be pulled out, then insert another needle with another thread under the entire abscess at the needle incision; press and tighten your threads in four places.
Then cut the abscess in its middle until what is in it comes out; then incise the excess skin and let what is tied be released from it.
Then place upon it a poultice which you have soaked in wine and oil. Then apply a treatment made with lint and ointments until it is healed.
Chapter One.
Concerning an abscess which occurs from twisting of nerves.
Just as an abscess occurs in an artery and vein, similarly an abscess occurs in nerves; which happens to them by a blow or from excessive labor and the like; and it most often occurs in places of support, such as the heel, and in every place where joints are moved; and it is a serious abscess.
Its color resembles the rest of the body, and it is in many positions without pain; and when it is squeezed with force, a weak person feels a sensation similar to numbness.
And it is not a collected abscess deep within the body; rather, it is under the skin, and it moves to every part, and it does not extend forward or back.
Therefore, what is among them at the joints should not be invaded with iron. For perhaps contraction may occur.
But in that which is among them on the head, or on the forehead, find the skin with a spatula. If therefore there is a small abscess, then hold it with forceps, and cut it from the root.
And if it is large, then suspend it with a hook, and flay it: then remove it, and bring the wound together with suture: and care for it until it heals.
Chapter LI.
Concerning the incision of an abscess, which occurs in the abdomen.
It happens to some men many times in their abdomens, and in the other bodies of theirs, boils, which are called fungal, by similarity of them with fungi.
Whose root is delicate, and their head is thick, and their lips are inverted. And some of them are small: and some of them grow very large.
And I indeed already saw a man, and in his abdomen were two boils, which were more similar to fungi than other things: there was no difference between them, white, small-rooted, and their lips were inverted, and they split open, and moisture always flowed from them.
I therefore cut into them, and found in the weight of one almost ten and eight ounces and in the other almost six ounces. And the operation in cutting them is that you consider then, if he is weak, moist, and the color of the boil is white, moist, delicate-rooted: then cut it with a broad spatula, and have a cautery in your presence in fire.
Very often much blood is expelled at its incision: hasten therefore if blood overcomes you, then cauterize it.
But if you see a weak and timid one from the incision with iron, then take a thread of lead well made, and constrict it with it: whose method is what we said, and let it go for two days.
Then add it to the mold of lead. Therefore do not cease to press the lead, whenever it prints on the thelil, until it is cut off and falls by itself without effort.
But if the thelil should be of a thick root, then there is some fear and dread in its incision; especially that which is in the belly.
Nevertheless it is possible to cut into half of it. Then cauterize it so that it does not grow back.
Then care for it until the wound is healed.
And be careful not to presume to cut into the thelil which is of a dark color, little feeling, foul appearance; for it is indeed a cancerous swelling; and I shall come with a remembrance of Cancer after this.
Chapter 52.
On the treatment of umbilical hernia.
An umbilical hernia occurs from many causes. Or from fissures in the diaphragm which is above the belly; and from it exits fat or intestine, according to what happens in other ruptures. Or from blood flowing from a Vein or an Artery, according to what was previously stated. Or from wind constricted within it.
But if it is because of a fissure in the diaphragm and the exit of fat; then its color is similar to the color of the body; and it is soft, without pain; and appears in various locations. And if it is because of the exit of intestine, then its location, with that which we have narrated, is of more vehement diversity. Therefore when you press it with your fingers, it disappears; then it returns, and perhaps there is a sound with it; and it swells greatly at the entrance of the bath, and there is vehement labor.
But if it is because of moisture, it is soft, then it does not disappear when you press it with your hand, nor does it contract or increase.
If it is due to blood, then with these signs an abscess appears darkening from the cleavage.
But if it is due to flesh: then the abscess is grave, thick, hard, and is fixed according to a single quantity.
But if it is due to windiness, then its touch is soft.
And the operation on that is, that you then consider if the prominence of the umbilicus is due to blood from an Artery or Vein, or windiness: then it is necessary that you prohibit its cure. For there is fear and dread, as I taught you in the chapter, in which I said abscesses due to artery and vein.
But if the prominence of the umbilicus is due to intestine or mesentery: then it is necessary that you advise the sick person, so that he may hold his breath and stand extended. Then mark with a cautery all around the umbilicus. Afterwards instruct him to lie down between your hands on his back. Then encircle with a broad spatula all around the umbilicus above the place which you marked with a cautery. Afterwards extend the middle of the abscess upwards, with a large hook. Then tie it tightly around with thread or cord from silk with a firm bandage: and let the knot of the bandage be distant.
Then open the middle of the extended abscess above the bandage. If therefore you find it already caught by the bandage: then extract the distant one and insert your index finger into it, and push the intestine towards the interior of the belly: and if you find a mesentery, then take hold of it with a hook and cut off its excess.
But if contrary to this it is an artery or vein: then encircle it greatly. Then return to your operation. And take two needles, and insert into them two strong threads; and insert the two needles into the circle which you made around the apostema in the shape of a cross, making them penetrate.
Then constrict the apostema in four places over the needles; and if you wish, remove the needles, and leave the place until the extended flesh putrifies; and falls off by itself; or cut it yourself when it softens and putrifies.
Then treat it with what is necessary from ointments until it heals.
But if the prominence of the umbilicus is from raw flesh in it, or from moisture; then it is necessary to make concave apostemas, as we said. And you should draw out the blood, or moisture, which you find in it.
Then treat it with what strengthens the place.
Chapter 53.
Concerning the treatment of CANCER.
We have already spoken in the division of the species of Cancer, and how the way to its cure is with medicines; and we cautioned against it being treated with iron so as not to ulcerate it; and we spoke of Cancer generated in the womb, and the caution against its treatment.
And the ancients said that sometimes Cancer is in a place where its eradication is possible; such as Cancer which is in the breasts, or in the thigh, and those like it, from the limbs suited to extracting all of it; especially when it is beginning and small.
But when it grows old, and is large, it is not necessary to approach it.
For I myself have not been able to heal anyone from it; nor have I seen another before me who attained that.
And the operation for it, when it is located as we said, is to precede and loosen the weak part from black bile several times. Then if you find the phlebotomy itself with manifest fullness.
Then prepare the sick person with preparation, in which he will be placed during the operation.
Then project into the Cancer hooks that are suitable for him. Then scoop it out from every side with the skin, according to examination, until nothing remains of its roots; and let the blood run, and do not cut it off quickly, rather press the places, and wipe away all thick blood with your hand, or with whatever instrument is possible there.
But if the thick flow of blood from the incision of an artery or vein opposes you, then cauterize the vein until the blood is severed. Then care for it with the remaining treatment until it is healed.
Concerning the Treatment of Hydropsy.
Chapter Fifty-Four. We have already narrated in the division of species of Hydropsy, and how the aggregation of water is made; and the signs of every species, and its cure with medicine.
And that which is cured with iron is none other than the ascites species alone; and it does not approach the species tympanitis nor the hyposarcae species at all, for that is fatal.
When you have already treated this kind of dropsy of the ear drum with medicine, and your medication has not been successful, then consider. For if the weak patient has already acquired debility, or if there is another illness with him besides dropsy, such as if he has a cough with it, or loosening of the bowels, and similar things to these, then beware lest you treat him with iron, for there is fear of that.
But if you see the patient to be exuberant in strength, and there is no illness with him besides dropsy alone, and he is neither a child nor an old man: then the method of operation on him is that you raise the patient standing between your hands, and a minister behind him shall press his belly with his hand, driving the water to the lower part of the chest.
Then take a thorny spatulate, according to this form. Let it be of two sharp points, long, with a sharp extremity like a spatula. However, let there be some slight shortness in it, so that you do not pierce through to the intestine with it during the operation, and injure it.
Then consider. For if the dropsy is a result of the intestines, then it is necessary that you lengthen the incision from the navel by the length of three fingers, towards the lower part in its direction above the chest.
But if the dropsy arises from a liver ailment, then let your incision be on the left, from the navel by three fingers.
And if its origin is from the spleen, then let the incision be on the right side; and it should not also be necessary that the incision be on the side on which the patient wishes to lie, so that the superfluities do not flow to that weak place.
Then pierce through the whole skin with the instrument. Then insert it into the incision and lift your hand with the spatula between the skin and flesh, as if you were flaying it; and let the amount that is flayed be the size of a nail, or thereabouts.
Then pierce the bladder until the trocar reaches the empty place: and that place is for water. Then withdraw it, and introduce into the opening an instrument which has this shape. And it is like a cannula; made of silver, or brass, or orichalcum, smooth and polished: to the lower part of which there is a small hole, and on its sides are three holes, two on one side and one on the other: and the face of its extremity is cut, according to the manner of cutting a reed pen, as you see: in its upper extremity there is a ring.
For when the instrument reaches the water, then it descends immediately upon the instrument, therefore it evacuates a moderate amount of water in an hour.
For if you evacuate from it more than is necessary, the sick man perhaps dies in an hour through dissolution of his animal spirits: or he suffers a swoon which approaches death.
But evacuate from it according to the amount of his strength, and according to what the sick man's dispositions signify to you from the power of his pulse, and from his good condition.
Then withdraw the instrument, and retain the water.
This is because it is retained immediately on account of the skin which retains the opening which is above the bladder: of which I have spoken, so that you pierce it according to that method.
Then repeat the instrument on the next day, if you see the sick man tolerate it; and also evacuate a small amount from the water.
Do this for several days without fear, and guard yourself against error: until there does not remain from the water except a little.
But if you are afraid for the sick man and dismiss a great deal from the water: then care for him with burial in warm sand, and much sweat in a bath and the sun, and expect thirst upon him; and care for him with drying medicines until he is healed: and cauterize over the stomach, liver, and spleen after the extraction of the water, according to what I have spoken in that which precedes.
On the treatment of infants who are born with their urine openings not pierced.
Chapter Fifty-Five.
When any of the infants exits from the belly of its mother and the urine opening of its head is not pierced, or the hole is narrow, or it is in a wrong place.
It is therefore necessary to help with its piercing immediately when it is born with a fine Spatumili according to this form.
Then place a thin lead plumb bob into the hole and tie it; and hold it for three days or four.
When it therefore wants to emit urine, remove it and let it mingle. Then put it back in.
And if you do not place lead, it is possible that the urine which flows over the place will not allow it to close.
He whose hole is narrow is treated with lead as we said for many days until it widens.
Those in whom the hole is in a wrong place are injured. For it is so that from those who are born, the hole is at the end of the head bone; therefore it cannot urinate forward until it raises its bone with its own hand to a higher place. Nor is there generation for him, because the sperm cannot reach the womb according to straightness; and it is a very foul disease.
And the method of operation on him is that the sick person is laid on their back. Then extend the head bone with your left hand using strong extension, and cut the top of the head bone from the place near the penis with a sharp point or a fine Spatumili: as is the incision of a reed or as you shave something so that its middle becomes like the head bone; and so that the hole falls in the middle as it should be.
And beware of the flow of blood during your operation. This often happens. Therefore oppose it with that which cuts blood, and care for the wound until it heals.
Concerning swellings that occur in the foreskin and head of the penis, and putrefaction, blackness, and adhesion.
Chapter 56.
These swellings often occur in the penis and there is a prominence of foul flesh.
And some are malignant from it and some are not malignant.
Therefore, a non-malignant one ought to be suspended with a fine hook and cut off until it is cleansed entirely. Then place upon it cotton dipped in Egyptian ointment. Then care for it afterwards with dyafinicon ointment until it heals.
But if the swelling is malignant and foul-colored, then you ought to apply a cautery to it after its incision and its scraping.
But if the swelling is in the foreskin of a captive, uncircumcised man, and part of the swelling is between the foreskin and part of it is outside, then you ought to remove the swelling which is inside first, until when it consolidates, then care for that which is outside; because when you care for both together, the foreskin will not be secured lest it be pierced.
And they also happen sometimes in the testicles and foreskin, blackness and putrefaction. Therefore, you ought to carve away everything that has already blackened and which is about to putrefy, or which has already putrefied.
Then smear it afterwards with honey that has been mixed with flour of pomegranate grains and crushed herbs sifted through a sieve.
Then care for it with the remaining treatment until it heals.
But if bleeding occurs, then apply a cautery with a lunar cautery, according to this form.
For a cautery is helpful for two things at once, namely, for the flow of blood, and a wound when it is corrupted.
And if the head of the rod is corroded, and it is all destroyed in an hour: then it is necessary that you insert into the urethra of the penis a cannula made of lead, so that the weak one may urinate upon it.
But in the adhesion of the foreskin with the head of the penis, and this indeed adhesion does not happen except in him whose foreskin is healthy, and circumcision is not required upon it: and sometimes adhesion happens because of a wound or an abscess: it is therefore necessary that you scrape it off with a blunt spatula until the ligament is loosened and the head of the penis is freed from every part.
And if it is difficult to distinguish these things exquisitely, then it is necessary that you scrape off something from the head of the penis and from the foreskin. For this is because when the foreskin is delicate it may perhaps be perforated quickly because of its delicacy.
Then separate between the foreskin and the head of the penis with a fine linen cloth which has already been moistened in cold water so that they do not adhere again.
Then treat it with astringent wine until it consolidates.
Chapter 57.
On the cleansing of infants and their treatment in case of an accident.
Circumcision is not a solution of continuity, like other wounds: nevertheless since it is from our operation, by our will, and its use is proper in boys, it is necessary that we indicate in it a better operation and an easier way leading to health.
Therefore I say that the ancients did not mention circumcision in any of their books: because they do not use it in their laws and it is only what we have acquired through experience.
For this is because I have found the greater part of surgeons and phlebotomists to use cleansing, circumcising with a lancet and with forceps: and they use alum, and ligature with thread and incision with a scalpel.
And I myself have now tested all these methods and found none better than cleansing with forceps and ligating with thread.
For in cleansing with a scalpel, the skin often escapes. For the skin of the foreskin has two coats; therefore perhaps the upper skin is cut and the lower coat remains: thus it is necessary to make an incision to cut the other, and there will be future pain. And with cleansing which is done by an alfect, that is, a finger, which tailors use, the incision of the extremity of the male member is not secured; for perhaps it enters into its opening.
But in cleansing with a nail, the iron perhaps goes astray and ruins your operation; and indeed the skin of a child is short in nature, and very often they are born thus, so that they do not need cleansing; and I have already seen this.
But cleansing with forceps and ligation with thread has shown me a good experiment; because the forceps are proportional to the incision; therefore, because the sharpness which is above is like the sharpness which is below. Therefore when you press your hand forward, the two sharpnesses are proportioned, and they fall according to a single operation at one time; and there is compression of a similar shape, containing the skin of the rod from every side, with which there is no error at all.
And the mode of the proper operation is to make you estimate, especially a boy if he is one of those who understand a little, that you do not do anything except bind the thread on his rod only; and let him go until another day.
Then make him rejoice and be glad in every way possible for you, because he receives his own intelligence. Then make him stand between your hands in a prepared posture and let him not be sitting; and hide the forceps in your sleeve or under your foot so that the boy does not see them at all, nor anything of the instruments.
Then insert your hand into its groove and blow upon the skin, and draw it upwards until the head of the groover emerges. Then cleanse it from whatever filth may collect in it.
Then bind the marked place with a double thread. Afterwards, bind a little lower a second ligature. Then hold with your index finger and thumb the place of the lower ligature with a good grip, and cut between the two ligatures. Then quickly raise the skin upwards and extract the head of the groover. Then allow the blood to flow gradually (for it is better) and put powder over it; and upon the powder a cloth in which has been spread yolk of egg previously cooked in rose water, mixed with good recent oil of roses; and let it remain upon it until another day; and treat it with further care until it heals.
But the form of the forceps for cleansing is this.
Let the two ends be blunt and converging so that the branch of the clavicle does not slip out of them; and let the length of the two points be equal to the length of two handles.
But an error which may occur in cleansing is this: that perchance the whole inner skin or a part of it may escape at the incision.
Therefore, it is necessary that you extend it from your hour with your nail before applying the poultice; and cut into it according to equality.
But if you cannot hold it with your nail, then extract it with a hook and cut into it.
But if three days pass and what is under the groover remains swollen and inflamed as an abscess, then let it be so until the abscess becomes settled and hot; and flay it easily, and cut into it according to what is prepared for you; and beware of the head of the groover. For it will not harm him.
Therefore care for it with that which consolidates a wound from powders, which we have related in the treatise on powders; and if it falls from the skin above the measurement, and reaches upward, it will not harm him even then.
Therefore care for it with what we have said until it is healed.
Chapter LVIII.
Concerning the Extraction of Retained Urine in the Bladder.
The retention of urine in the bladder is caused by obstruction made from a stone, or congealed blood, or pus, or flesh grown there, and similar things from those which we have said from the methods of treatment in the division.
And if the urine is not discharged, and you see that its retention is because of a stone which is already in the neck of the bladder, then it is necessary for him to be weak, curved over his knees. Then let his feet be placed upon his back, and let him extend his belly from the upper part of his back, and let the weak one hold his breath as much as possible for him. Thus the neck of the bladder is forced toward the expulsion of the stone to the back; therefore the urine is discharged.
But if it is not discharged with what we have said, and the matter is severe upon the weak one; then it is necessary that you administer his extraction with an instrument which is called a syringe, of which this is the form: Let it be made of silver, and let it be slender, smooth, concave like a bird's quill in the subtlety of its radius, long in the likeness of one and a half palms; to which let there be a slender cannula at its head.
And the manner of the exit of urine through it is, that you take a doubled thread and tie wool or cotton at its end with a good ligature, and insert the end of the thread into the lower part of the syringe; and cut off with forceps anything which overflows from the wool, but insert it into the cannula forcefully, like raising.
Then anoint the syringe with oil, or butter, and egg white; and make the weak person sit upon a seat, and embrocate the bladder, and the penis of him with oily moist substances, and olive oil, and warm water.
Then introduce the syringe into the penis with ease, until it reaches the root of the penis.
Then double it upward from the part above the navel.
Then press the syringe within it until it reaches the bladder, and let the weak person feel that he has already reached the empty thing; and this is not done according to this order unless because the passage in which it goes is one in which there is a winding.
Then extend the thread with wool gradually. For urine will follow the wool.
Then draw it out, and make the urine run through the way of urine; and repeat the syringe, and do not cease to do that until the bladder is emptied, and the weak person finds relief.
On the Quality of Bladder Irrigation with a Clepshydra.
Chapter LIX.
When an ulcer occurs in the bladder, or blood congeals in it, or pus constricts in it, and one wishes to distill waters and medicines into it; then let it be done with an instrument which is called a Clepshydra, and this is its form.
For it is deaf, that is, closed. And in it are three openings, two on one side, and one on the other side, as you see.
And the concave place, in which is that which presses, let it be according to the quantity which it will fill itself without addition, until when it is drawn with it something, from moisture it is drawn: and when it is pressed, with it it is expelled to a distance; according to what the clepshydra does, with which naphtha is projected, that is, Greek fire in battles of war.
Therefore, when you wish to inject moisture into the bladder, insert the extremity of the syringe into the moisture and draw with it that which pushes upwards, for moisture is drawn into the concavity of the syringe.
Then insert the extremity of it into the rod, according as we have described concerning the syringe. Then force the moisture with that which forces it; for the moisture reaches the bladder immediately, so that the weak one feels it.
Form of a slender clyster by means of which the bladder is injected.
Let it be made of silver, or brass; of which the head is higher and similar to a small mortar, and beneath it a groove into which a ligature may fall.
Then take the bladder of a ram, and put into it the moisture with which you wish to inject the bladder. Then bind it between the two grooves with a strong ligature using a double thread; and warm that moisture slightly over fire.
Then insert the extremity of the clyster into the rod; afterwards constrict your hand above the bladder with moisture with a decent constriction, until the weak one feels that the moisture has already reached the bladder.
But if you do not have a bladder present, then take a piece of parchment and make a circle from it, and pierce it through all its roundness. Then insert a strong thread into those holes, as they are turned. Then gather the circle, as a bandage is gathered; after you have put into it that which you wish from moisture, and oils, and waters.
Then bind it into the instrument; and do with it as you did with the bladder from expression with your hand, until the moisture reaches the bladder.
Chapter Sixty.
Concerning the Extraction of a Stone.
We have already said in the division, the kinds of stone, and its treatment with medicines; and I have said the difference between the stone generated in the kidneys, and the bladder, and the signs of all of it; and I said the stone in which operation falls with the hand and sectioning; and there is a stone generated properly in the bladder; and that which is attached to the rod; and I am narrating that with manifest exposition and abbreviation.
Therefore, I say that a stone generated in the bladder most often occurs in children.
And among its signs is that the urine coming forth from the bladder resembles water in its subtlety; and there appears in it white sand, and a weak man rubs his staff with it, and labors with it, and very often hangs. Then it is erected, and the anus goes out with it in many of them.
And the cure of children is easy from that time until they reach fourteen years. And it is difficult in old men. In youths however it is medium, in that which is between those two; and the cure of him whose stone is larger is easier; and if it is small, the care for it is greater.
Therefore, when we come to the cure, it is necessary first of all that we purify the sick man with an enema which extracts all the dung which is in his bowels; for it sometimes prevents the discovery of the stone in the examination.
Then let the sick man be taken with his feet, and shaken, and moved downwards so that the stone descends to the depths of the bladder, or jumps from a high place several times.
Then make him sit between your hands prepared, and let his hands be under his hips so that the whole bladder becomes sloping downwards.
Then examine him and touch him externally.
If therefore you feel the stone in a space, then hasten immediately with an incision over it.
But if it does not fall at all under your touch, then you must wipe the index finger with oil of the left hand, if the sick man is a boy; or the middle finger if he is a full-grown youth; and insert it into his anus, and inquire for the stone until it stands under your finger: and gradually turn it towards the neck of the bladder.
Then press upon it with your finger and drive it outward toward the place whose incision you desire; and order your assistant to press the bladder with his hand; and order another assistant to extend the testicles upward with his right hand, and with his other hand remove the skin which is under the testicles in the part where the incision is to be made.
Then introduce the speculum, which has this form. And find in it what is between the anus and the testicles, and not in the middle, but to the side near the left buttock; and let the incision be over the stone, and your finger be in the anus; and let the incision be transverse, so that the outer part of the incision is wide and the inner part is narrow, according to the quantity by which the egress of the stone from it will not be greater.
For perhaps the finger, which is in the anus, compresses the stone at the incision and it exits without difficulty.
And know that a stone with angles and edges is difficult to extract because of them.
And there is one which is smooth, similar to a gland, and round; and its exit is easy.
Therefore, for one which has angles and edges, add an incision into the fissure.
But if it does not exit this way, then you must ingeniously work on it; or tie a ligature over it with a crescent-shaped end, which will constrict the stone and not slip from it; or insert a fine instrument under it with a curved end.
But if you cannot work on it above, then enlarge the opening a little. If something overcomes you from blood, cut it off with a scalpel.
But if there is more than one stone, then first drive the largest one towards the bladder opening. Then make an incision over it. Then drive a small one after that; and do the same if there are more than two.
If it is very large, ignorance is to make a large incision above it. For it happens to the weak one of two things, either that he dies, or there occurs to him a continuous distillation of urine: therefore, because the place is not consolidated at all.
But administer its expulsion until it goes out: or ingeniously break it with forceps until you pull it out piecemeal.
Therefore when you have completed your operation, then fill the wound with frankincense, and aloe, and dragon's blood: and bind it up, and place upon it a cloth infused with oil and wine, or rose oil, and cold water: so that a hot swelling is seated.
Then place the weak one supine on his neck, and do not loosen the bandage until the third day. When therefore you loosen it, embrocate the place with water and much oil.
Then care for it with dyafinicon ointment, and basilicum ointment until it is healed.
But if a further hot swelling, and corrosion, and things similar to those occur in the wound: such as if the blood congeals in the bladder, and urine is prohibited from it: and that is recognized from the issuing of blood with the urine. Therefore insert your finger into the wound, and extract that blood. For if it remains, it brings about corruption of the bladder, and its putrefaction.
Then wash the wound with vinegar, and water, and salt: and oppose to it every kind of thing suitable for its healing until it is healed.
And it is necessary during the whole time of treatment that you bind two thighs and hipbones together, because of the medicines which are placed upon the place.
But if a stone is small, and is in the urethra of the penis, and is fixed in it and prohibits the passage of urine: then care for it with that which I have related before you come to incision. For very often you are excused from incision by this treatment, for I have already proven it.
And take a perforator of iron, with a triangular point, sharp, fixed in wood.
Then take a thread and bind it to a stick under a stone so that it does not return to the bladder.
Then insert the perforating iron into the stick easily until the perforator reaches the stone itself; and revolve the perforator with your hand on the stone gradually; and try to make a perforation in it until you make it penetrate from the other side.
For urine is immediately discharged.
Then press your hand on that which remains of the stone from the outer part of the stick. For it is broken and comes out with the urine, and the sick person is healed.
But if this treatment is not prepared for you because of something that prevents it: then bind a thread under the stone and another thread above the stone. Then cut above the stone on the stick itself between the two ligatures. Then extract it. Then loosen the bandage and cleanse the congealed blood that forms in the wound.
And it is not necessary to bind the thread under the stone unless so that it does not return to the bladder; and the other thread above unless so that when the thread is loosened after the exit of the stone, the skin returns to its place and heals the wound; and because of this it is necessary for you when binding the upper thread to lift the skin upwards so that it comes back to its completion and heals the wound, as we have said.
Chapter LXl.
On the Extraction of Stones from Women.
Stones rarely form in women.
But if it should happen to one of them a stone, then the cure is difficult and is forbidden because of many ways.
One of them is: that a woman might possibly be a virgin.
And the second is, because you do not find a woman who will reveal herself to a doctor when she is chaste, or from those having husbands.
The third is, because you do not find a woman who well knows this art, especially the operation with the hand.
And the fourth is the distance from the place of the stone: wherefore it needs deep incision, and there is fear in that.
But if necessity provokes to that, then it is necessary that you take a woman doctor, well knowing: and few are found.
But if you deprive yourself of those things then seek a chaste doctor, subtle and present a female midwife well taught in the matter of women, or a woman who inclines to this art, a part of the art; therefore make her present, and especially to her, that she do all that you command her from inquiry upon the stone.
First of all which is, that she look to see if the woman is a virgin: then it is necessary that she insert a finger into her anus and seek the stone.
If therefore she finds it, she will constrict it under her finger, and then command the midwife to insert her finger into the vulva of the weak woman, and inquire about the stone, after she has placed her left hand upon the bladder, and compresses it with a good compression.
If therefore she finds the stone then it is necessary that gradually she move it from the orifice of the bladder to the lower parts with all her strength until it arrives with it at the root of the thigh.
Then cut upon it at the opposition of the middle of the vulva at the root of the thigh, from whichever part she prepares for it and feels the stone in that part: and her finger is not removed from the stone, constricting under it: and let the incision be small at first. Then he should introduce a probe over that incision.
Then he should introduce a probe over that incision. If therefore he feels a stone, then let him add to the incision according to the proportional amount by which the stone exits from it.
And you should know that there are many kinds of stones. For among these there is a small one and a large one, a smooth one and a rough one, a long one, and a round one having branches.
Therefore know its kind so that it may be made clear to you by this regarding what you see.
If therefore blood overcomes you, then add pounded lint in its place and hold it for one hour until the blood is stemmed.
Then return to your operation until the stone comes out; and do that you prepare for yourself from the instruments which I mentioned in the extraction of a stone in men so that you may be helped through them in your operation.
But if blood flow overcomes you and you know that the expulsion of blood is from the artery which has been cut then put powder over the place and bind it with powders in a decent bandage and do not change it; and leave the stone and do not extract it. For perhaps the weak woman may die. Then attend to the wound.
When therefore the sharpness of the blood subsides after days and the place festers, then return to your operation until the stone comes out.
Chapter Sixty-Two.
On Incising for Aqueous Hernia.
An aqueous hernia is nothing but a collection of moisture in the white sac which is under the skin containing the testicle; and it is called a sac.
And sometimes it is in its own groin which nature prepared for it in that part until it swells; which is another egg and it is the skin of the testicle between the white sac which we mentioned; and this does not happen except rarely.
And indeed the generation of these branches is from a weakness that occurs in the testicles; therefore this matter is poured forth to them. And sometimes it happens from a blow upon the testicles.
And this humidity has many colors. For its color is either inclined toward yellowness, or it is blood-red, or fecal, black, or it is watery white, and that which is more.
And the signs by which it is known where the aggregation of water is are these. For if it is in the white sifac which we have said, then the abscess is round in length a little like the shape of an "o," and the testicle does not appear because the humidity contains it from every side.
And if the humidity is in its own scrotum, then the abscess is round with a part of an "o," and because of this a man thinks that it is another egg.
And if the humidity is between the skin of the testicles and the white sifac, then it falls under the sense.
When therefore you wish to know the color of the humidity, then experience the color of the humidity in the abscess with a square instrument, whose shape precedes. According to that therefore which comes out in the trace of the instrument, judge concerning that which is between it.
When therefore we come to the treatment with iron, it is necessary that you advise the sick person that he should be bled if it is possible for him; and you see his body full.
Then he is laid on his back on a high thing a little, and many cloths are put under him.
Then you sit on his left side and direct the attendant to sit on his right side who should extend his instrument towards one of two sides of the skin of the testicles and towards the part marveling at the belly.
Then take a broad scalpel and cut the skin of the testicles from the middle through its length, up to near the comb; and let the incision be made according to the straightness of an equidistant line which divides the skin of the testicles until it reaches the white sifac comprehending it; and flay it out and observe, or beware from this, that you cut it; and let your flaying be from the part which hangs with the egg more; and carefully scrutinize with the flaying according to the quantity that is possible for you.
Then pierce the full bladder with a wide perforation and extract all the water.
Then separate between the two lips of the incision with hooks and extend the bladder upward, and do not touch the skin encompassing the testicles; and incise the bladder however it is possible for you to incise it, either with its entirety or in pieces, especially its thinner side. For if you do not diligently examine it, there will be no safety in its incision, lest the water return.
But if the external testicle proceeds from its skin during your operation: then when you have completed the incision of the bladder, replace it. Then join the two lips of the skin of the testicles with suture.
Then care for it like the other wounds until it heals.
But if you find a corrupted testicle from blackness or other signs, then it is necessary to tie the vessels which are suspensive, fearing the flow of blood.
Then cut off the testicle with its suspensory ligament and extract the testicle; afterwards care for it as we have said.
But if the water is collected in two parts, then know that there are two branches; therefore cut the other part according to what you did in the other equally.
And if it seems fitting to you that the operation be one, then do so; then wipe away the blood. And insert into the incisions wool steeped in oil, or in rose oil; and place outside other wool than that which you have steeped in wine and oil, and spread it over the two testicles and the belly; and put double cloths on top, they are bandages; and tie them on top with a bandage having six strands according to this form.
Then take two cloths and have them taken and filled with a cooked poultice according to this similarity: and let their ends be pressed from the elbow or wet wool; and they are gathered together with a bandage on the upper arms: and one of the ends takes hold above the anus up to the back, and the second takes hold at the side of the breastbone: and two ends on top of the two thighs below and the other two ends on top of the two groins, and let the entire bandage be gathered on the upper arms.
And sometimes this membrane is artificially treated with a cautery in the place of incision with iron; and it is as you should take a sharp knife-cautery and cut the skin of the testicles with it; and ignite it according to what we have said: until when a white sifac appears containing water, then take a cautery of this shape.
Greek letters are assimilated.
Then pierce it with that cautery and ignite it until all the moisture comes out.
Then extend the sifac with hooks and flay it with a thin ignited cautery and cut into it as much as you are able until you root out all of it.
And be careful that the fire does not touch the testicle.
And know that this operation is safer and further from hemorrhage and better than the operation with incision.
But if it presents opposition to you in the hour of incision, or in the hour of cautery an inflamed abscess, or hemorrhage, or anything else: then it is necessary that you treat what opposition it presents to you from it with the treatment which suits it according to what has been said previously.
But if he is weak and fearful, and does not await this operation because of its horror: then administer a perforation according to what I relate it: and it is.
When you are assured at your place by that which we said about the signs, that the membrane is watery: then it is necessary that you make the weak person sit upon a raised seat.
Then pierce the abscess in the lower part of it with a broad trocar; and place the piercing according to the length of the body; and let the opening be wide until all the water runs out.
Then place a compress on the spot and bind it, and let it be so that the remainder of the water runs out; then treat it until the wound is healed.
But if there is an opposition to the water escaping at the hour you pierce it; and this is not except because the white fatty layer makes opposition at the wound's opening and prevents the water from going out: then it is necessary that you introduce a pen into the wound, or an instrument with which water of dropsy patients is extracted, whose shape precedes; or add a little in the operation of the wound.
