And know that the water returns and collects again after six months, or thereabouts. Therefore when it collects, then it is returned to according to the same way, and the sick man will pass his days.
Chapter Sixty-Three.
Concerning the care of fleshy branches.
Know that in the section on this branch, there is fear leading to destruction in many dispositions. Therefore it seems to me that its dismissal is saving from it.
And I am remembering the operation on it, and the kind of abscess.
I say therefore that many abscesses occur in bodies, from which the testicles are composed; and this happens from many causes, namely.
Either from acute or sharp superabundance, which is poured onto the testicles. Or from a blow.
And the color of the abscess is according to the color of the body and pain is not found in it: and it is a heavy abscess.
And perhaps it is a stony abscess, whose color is dark brown, and there is no sensation to it.
And sometimes it happens from the knotting of arteries and their inflation, as I have already mentioned its remembrance; or from the inflation of veins.
Which therefore is from them from the inflation of arteries, it is recognized by the disposition of the abscess when you compress it with your fingers. And it is not necessary for you to touch it altogether.
That which is done by the inflation of veins, nothing is dispersed from it upon compression with the fingers.
And now it is possible that a section be made on this species, and on the other flesh; and it is to cut the skin of the testicles. Then extend the testicle upward and extract it with white silk; and free the spermatic cord from the vessels, and tie the vessels; and incise the spermatic cord after you have freed it from every part of the parts of the testicle.
If therefore the testicle is consolidated with those flesh born together, then it is necessary that you draw out the testicle and incise it.
And if there is adhesion between something of the spermatic cord or in that which is between the vessels, then it is necessary that you free all that adhesion and incise it with a round incision.
But if there is a birth of flesh in the place of adhesion which is behind: then it is necessary that you incise all of it and extract the testicle as we have said.
When therefore your operation is complete then fill the wound with lint steeped in rose oil and wine; then care for it with the remaining care until it heals.
Chapter LXIIII
Concerning the Care of Branches, which are with Vines.
Hydrocele is a swelling enveloped with a certain involution, like a dropsy, with the softening of the testicles; and it makes difficult walking and exercise.
And in the treatment of this ailment there is fear because of that of which mention was made before.
But it is necessary for us to say that an operation be done on it according to what the ancients did.
I therefore say that it is necessary that you make the patient sit upon a raised seat. Then press the spermatic cord of the testicles downward. Then hold the skin of the testicles with your fingers, along with the veins that are near the penis; and let another attendant hold them, and extend them with a vigorous extension.
Then cut with a wide, sharp scalpel in a transverse section in the direction of the veins, until the veins are exposed.
Then skin from every part, as we told you in the extraction of arteries which are in the temples.
Then place a needle with a double thread into them, and tie it in the first of the places where the veins occur; and tie it again at the last of them. Then cut them in a straight middle section along the length of the body, and extract what collects from humid, corrupted excretions.
Then care for the wound with the care of the other wounds, in which you wish to generate pus, so that the veins which nourish one of the testicles may fall away. For it is not bad through that.
But if it should happen to a vein with all the veins, then it is necessary that they be extracted because of this testicles with the veins: so that the testicle is not deprived of nourishment because of the incision, and wastes away, or dries up, and there is no benefit with them.
Chapter LXV.
Concerning the care of Intestinal Branches.
The outcome of this Intestinal Branch is from a section, which occurs in an extended colon over the belly in the groin part from the belly button.
Therefore the intestines descend from that rupture to one testicle. And this rupture is either from the colon, or its extension.
And these two species occur from many causes: either from percussion, or a jump, or a shout, or the lifting of a heavy thing, and similar things.
And the sign when it is from the extension of the colon It happens gradually over a long time and does not happen suddenly; and it is an abscess equal towards the depth because the suture compresses the intestine.
And its sign is when it is from the section of a suture, because it causes to happen from its beginning intense pain suddenly; and it is a separate abscess appearing under the nearby skin; and that is because of the exit of the intestine and its course to the outside of the suture.
And sometimes it issues forth with intestine a swelling; and this is called an intestinal Ramex and a swelling; and sometimes it happens with flatulence.
And sometimes feces runs into the intestine and is retained there; and it is with this deadly to the sick person because it causes severe pain to happen, and especially when it is pressed.
And in the treatment of the species of this sickness with iron there is fear; therefore it is necessary that one should beware in this matter.
And the manner of the operation is that you direct the sick person to return the intestine with his hand to the interior of its cavity if it obeys the return. Then he is laid back on his neck between your hands; and raise his legs.
Then extend the skin which follows the skin upward and cut all the skin of the testicles through its length. Then fix hooks in the two lips of the incision according to as you judge to open the incision with them; and let the incision be according to the quantity as much as possible so that an egg may exit from it.
Then scrape the sutures and what are under the skin of the testicles until when the white suture, hard on all sides is uncovered; then insert your index finger into that which follows the testicle into that which is between the white suture, which is under the skin of the egg, and between the second suture, and loosen with it the adhesion which is in the back part of the egg.
Then move with your right hand to the inner skin of the testicles and with this extend the white linen upwards with your left hand, and lift the testicle with the linen to the part to be cut, and order the assistant to extend the testicle upwards, and you will loosen the adhesion which is behind after complete detachment.
And search with your fingers so that nothing from the intestine remains entangled in the hard white linen. But if you find something from it, then push it down towards the lower abdomen.
Then take a needle with a thick thread made of licorice from ten threads and insert it at the last linen, that is, the tunic which is under the skin of the testicles following the incision, then cut off the ends which protrude from the thread until there are four threads. Then arrange some of them over some in the shape of a cross and tie them with the linen bandage we spoke of, because it is under the skin of the testicles with a strong ligature, until nothing from the vessels which nourish them can send anything to them so that hot abscess does not occur there. And place a second ligature outside the first ligature, distant from it by less than two fingers. And after these two ligatures let out from the linen which is under the skin of the testicles a quantity the size of a finger and cut off the rest according to its roundness, and remove the testicle from it.
Then cauterize the lower part of the skin of the testicles with the incision from which blood and pus flow, as we have narrated in what has been said before.
Then administer wool soaked in oil and place it on the wound and administer the bandage we have spoken of. And sometimes the white linen that we mentioned is cauterized after its incision fearing bleeding. Very often this happens. And release the ligature until it falls by itself.
But if its falling is delayed, then embrocate it with warm water and place upon it that which we have said, until it falls.
Then care for the wound with the remaining care of wounds, until it is healed.
Chapter Sixty-Six.
Concerning a windy branch.
In this branch I have not seen anyone who dared to perform surgery upon it with iron.
And first of all they said that it is done as we have narrated in the branch which is with a vine. Which is, that you bind the vessels after section above them with ease below; then cut in the middle, and finally care for it with that which opens it up, until the vessels fall away; and care for the wound, according to what we said, until it is healed.
Chapter Sixty-Seven.
Concerning a rupture which occurs in the groins.
When a rupture occurs in the groins, as we have said, and the place protrudes, and nothing from the intestines descends to the testicles; and if it does descend, it is a small amount, and it returns in all hours: but if time prolongs with it, a fissure is added to the inguinal canal, until the intestines descend into the inguinal canal.
And this occurs from the extension of the inguinal canal which is in the groins, as we have said. Which is because the inguinal canal is extended; then it is softened and the place becomes inflamed and protrudes.
And its care is with a cautery, as its preceding narration has shown.
And sometimes it is cured with iron, according to this method: and it is that the patient lies on his back between your hands. Then cut the inflamed and protruding place of the rupture, by a section across its width, according to the amount of three fingers.
Then bind the ligature and what is under the skin until a white vein is revealed, which follows it beneath the skin.
Then take a ray and place it upon the prominent spot from the vein and press it deep into the abdomen.
Then choose two prominent spots above the end of the ray from the vein and join them together with a suture; then extract the end of the ray.
And do not cut into the vein at all, nor pull the egg, nor anything else, as I have taught you in the treatment of intestinal branches.
Then treat it with wound care. When therefore you cut the threads clean it and restore the wound until it is consolidated.
For the vein contracts and no prominence of a rupture is added.
And cautery is better in this place; because it is closer to safety.
Chapter LXVIII.
On the softening of the skin of the testicles.
Often the skin of the testicles is softened in some men, because of which the flesh between them is softened and its appearance is spoiled.
Therefore it is necessary for him who is asked in its treatment, to make the sick man lie on his back and cut away all the softened skin onto the natural skin.
Then join the two lips with a suture; and if you wish, use at first three or four sutures on the excess softened skin and firm the suture.
Then cut between the sutures.
Then treat it with the care of other wounds until it is healed and the threads fall out.
Chapter LXIX.
On castration.
Know that castration is excommunicated in our law and because of this it would be permissible for me not to remember it in this book of mine; and there is no need to remember it except for two reasons.
One of which is, that it be in the knowledge of medicine when asked about it: and that he know its cure to whom it happens.
And another way is, that we very often need the castration of certain animals, for our benefit through it, such as rams, kids, and cats, and similar animals.
I say therefore, that castration is done according to two ways. Or by abrasion. Or by fissure, and section.
But in that which is done by abrasion, the way of the operation is: that we make the animal sit in warm water, until its testicles are softened and relaxed, and hang down: then rub them with your hand, until they are resolved and do not appear under touch.
But in castration by fissure and section, it is necessary that you hold the animal, and press the skin of its testicles with your left hand.
Then ligature the spermatic cord, and cut above all the egg with one incision, until when two eggs proceed: then incise them after you have skinned both, and do not leave anything above them except a subtle wick, which is above the vessels.
And this way of castration is better than that which is done by abrasion: because abrasion perhaps leaves something of the testicles, wherefore the animal desires intercourse.
Then care for the wound until it heals.
Chapter Seventy.
Concerning the care of hermaphrodites in two ways.
A hermaphrodite is from males according to two ways. One of them is because it appears in him that follows a space, or in the skin of the testicles, in that which is between the two testicles, a figure like that of a woman's vulva, in which there are hairs: and sometimes urine runs from that which is in the skin of the testicles.
In women, however, there is one species, and it is the vulva of a woman above the pubic bone, just like the testicles of a man, very small, prominent outwardly: one of which is like the penis of a man, and two like two testicles.
And the treatment of the three species, two from men and one species from women, is: because it is necessary that you cut away the added flesh until their impression escapes. Then treat them with the care of other wounds.
But in the second species of men, from which urine flows, which is in the skin of the testicles, there is no operation, nor is there any treatment at all.
Chapter LXXI.
On the incision of a tenting and prominent flesh in the vulva of women.
A tenting may perhaps be added upon the natural part until its appearance becomes unsightly and foul: and sometimes it magnifies in certain women so much that it expands like in men, and reaches even to intercourse.
Therefore it is necessary that you grasp the excess tenting with your hand or a hook and make an incision; and do not stop in the incision, especially deep at the root: so that bleeding does not occur.
Then treat it with the care of other wounds until it heals.
But the flesh that arises is flesh that is born in the opening of the womb until it fills it; and perhaps it protrudes outward according to the likeness of a tail; and on account of this some ancients call the ailment tailed. Therefore it is necessary that we cut it like we cut the tenting, and treat it until it heals.
On the care of an alratic vulva, which is a vulva not perforated.
Chapter LXXII.
An alratic vulva is one that is not perforated, or has a small opening. And it is either natural or accidental.
Accidental is from a preceding illness and it itself is made either from flesh added that was born, or thin pus, or thick; and it is made either in the depth of the womb, or on its sides, or in its upper part, or lower part; and it prohibits coitus and impregnation and childbirth, and perhaps prohibits menstruation.
And the knowledge of it is made by the midwife's view, if evident swelling is nearby.
But if it is not evident, then seek it with your fingers, or with a rod.
But if the swelling is from thin pus and is near by two lips then hurry and break it open.
And it is as if you put poultices on two lips.
Then place the index fingers of both your hands together, and let the woman be on her back with her legs spread. Then extend the two lips with strength until the thin pus is broken and the swelling is opened.
Then take wool and soak it in oil and place it upon the spot; and let the woman come every day so that the place does not solidify with other fluid.
But if the pus is thick and dense then it is necessary that you cut it with a wide spatula similar to a myrtle leaf.
But if the swelling is from flesh that was born then suspend it with hooks and cut into it; and let there be medicines with you which stop the flow of blood without biting, such as acacia and dragon's blood and alum aggregate with egg white.
Then administer a wide lead tube so that the wound does not heal quickly, hold it for several days; and administer a bandage of dry linen, then let the rest be cured until it is healed.
And sometimes there is born a nativity of other flesh within the womb; therefore it is necessary that they be cut according to this manner: but if there is no cancerous swelling for a cancerous swelling in the womb should not be opposed with iron at all.
Chapter Seventy-Three.
On the treatment of hemorrhoids, athehelil, and bothor rubeae in the vulvas of women.
Hemorrhoids are indeed the opening of the veins' orifices, until much blood constantly flows from them.
When therefore hemorrhoids become ancient, they become athehelil; and I have already said in the division of their species and signs: and I will say here which ones receive healing from them, and which ones do not.
I therefore say that hemorrhoids which are in the orifice of the matrix, and do not appear to the senses: then there is no healing for them with iron.
But those from them which are in the orifice of the matrix on which sensation falls are those that are healed.
It is therefore necessary that you introduce the woman into a cold house. Then extend the athehelil with forceps, or with a rough cloth, and cut them from their roots.
Then sprinkle upon them at the flow of blood from one of the cutting powders without modification: such as acacia, and dragon's blood, and thus, and those similar to them.
Then press her feet against the wall for an hour. Afterwards place her in cold water.
But if the flow of blood persists: then make her sit in a decoction of sumacs, and pomegranate rinds, and galls, and those similar to them.
Then take barley flour, and mix it with honey and vinegar, and make a plaster with it upon her back.
If blood is therefore cut off with it, it is good: and if not, make the windswept ones adhere to the back, and nipples, without scarification.
Then take wool and submerge it in the juice of tamarind, or the juice of arugula, or the juice of twigs of alang-alang, that is, aglantus, Pontic wine, and make it adhere to the place.
Therefore, when the abscess is quiet, care for the place with ointments until it heals.
However, a red ulcer resembles the heads of alfa-alfa, and it has a rough appearance.
Therefore, it is necessary to cut out what appears from it, as I said in the chapter equally; and care for it until it heals.
Chapter Seventy-Four.
On Perforation of the Uterus which Occurs in the Womb.
Many kinds of abscesses occur in the womb, such as cancer, and stony abscesses, and ulcers, and adubelati, and alekilati, that is herpes, aestiomemus, and fistulas, and hemorrhoids, and fissures, and thelil, and hot abscesses.
And I have indeed already said all these diseases and their kinds, and the signs of them, and their cures in the division: it is necessary that I say in this treatise a hot abscess which occurs in the womb, when it is from abscesses which collect pus, and you wish to know how its opening is made with iron.
Therefore, it is necessary for you to consider. Then if the pain of the abscess in its beginning is acute with percussion, and heat, and fever; and you see it to be red; if numbness falls upon it, then do not hurry with its perforation; and care for it with that which helps with its maturation, until when the sharpness of the occurrences becomes quiet.
Then it is necessary that you make the woman sit upon a seat which beats two seats, and recline, or lean upon her back, and gather her feet to the lower part of her belly; and let her hips be spread, and let her put her arms under her knees, and bind each with a suitable bandage to it.
Then let the midwife sit from the right side, and administer the instrument by which the orifice of the womb is opened.
And it is necessary when you wish to administer this instrument, that you fix it with the Speculum deep in the matrix of the woman: so that what enters from the instrument is not more than the depth of the matrix, and does not injure the weak parts.
But if the instrument should be longer than deep, then it is necessary that you place lint on the covering of the deep matrix: so that entry of the whole instrument into the depth of the matrix is prohibited: and it is necessary that you place the handle, which runs in the instrument, on the upper side: and that you hold the instrument, and that the assistant revolves the handle until the depth of the matrix is opened.
Therefore when the Exterior appears and is touched by the hand and is soft and pliable: then it is necessary that you cut it at its head with a broad spatula.
Therefore when the blood is evacuated, it is necessary that you place a linen cloth soaked in rose oil or green oil, in which there is a certain astringency: and place the linen cloth and draw out the incision into the depth of the matrix.
And place externally on the matrix, and above the comb clean wool soaked in water, in which apples have been boiled.
Then let it be treated after two days with an ointment suitable for it until it is healed.
And sometimes the matrix and the wound are washed so that it is cleansed with water and honey; then with water in which the root of lily or long birthwort has been boiled; then let it return to the ointment.
But if the wound should be hidden inside the matrix, then it is necessary that you refrain from treating it with iron, but rather care for it with what we said in the division.
Chapter LXXV.
Doctrine of Obstetrics: How living embryos are treated, according to an unnatural figure having exited.
It is necessary for an obstetrician to know first the figure of natural childbirth.
Therefore, from its signs, it is that when you see a woman compressed toward the lower parts, and desires to smell air, and finds relief above her about childbirth, and hastens the exit of the embryo: then you know that this childbirth is according to the natural figure, and its exit is upon its head, and following with it: or suspended, or hanging from the navel.
Therefore when you see these signs, it is necessary that her belly be compressed so that the embryo descends quickly.
For when it descends upon its head, the following parts descend with it, and are cleansed from those superfluities by good cleansing.
But that which is besides this childbirth is unnatural, undesirable.
For sometimes the fetus goes forth upon its feet, and its body goes forth before its head, and its feet, and its hands together, or one of its feet; or its head goes forth and its hands; or its belly goes forth; and perhaps it turns upon its neck, and similar things from undesirable figures.
Therefore it is necessary for an obstetrician to be prudent, subtle in all these things, and beware of error and deception: and I am showing every figure from them, and how its ingenuity is in it, and may be signified by it, and stand upon it.
When the fetus goes forth upon its head by its natural exit, and it becomes vehement upon the woman, and childbirth becomes difficult for her, and you see her strength already weakened: then make her sit upon a seat, or let women hold her; and vaporize her vulva with decocted fenugreek, and with moist oils.
Then let the obstetrician introduce a small spatula between her fingers, and grasp the following part with it, and cut it with her fingernail until what is in it runs out from moisture; and press the belly of the woman until the fetus descends.
If it does not descend then it is necessary that the woman be given an enema of fenugreek mucilage with sesame oil.
Then order her after the enema to compress it; and make her cough when compressed, and hold her mouth and nose for one hour. For the fetus will immediately come out.
But if two hands of the fetus emerge first, it is necessary that you return them gradually with ease. But if it does not obey upon entering, then place the woman over a seat and raise her feet upwards; then strike the seat on the ground; and let the woman be held so that she does not fall during the impact.
But if the hands do not emerge and the fetus dies, then cut them and extract the remainder of the fetus; and tie its hand with cloth and extract it evenly for it will come out.
The exit of the embryo upon its feet. When the fetus emerges on its feet then it is necessary that you push them upwards; then turn the fetus gradually; afterwards equalize it.
Then take one of its feet and extend it gradually.
When they emerge as far as the hips, then order her to compress it and make her cough when compressed; for it will come out if God wills.
And if it does not come out with what we have described, then return the fetus gradually until you place it according to its natural form. For it will then come out with an easy exit.
But if it is hindered with all that we have described, then take fenugreek mucilage and sesame oil and dissolved gum; and strike the whole in a mortar with good percussion; then smear the woman's vulva and lower part of her belly with it. Then have her sit in warm water until her lower abdomen follows suit.
Therefore, when you see that its lower parts are already heated, then make for it a poultice of myrrh and apply it. When therefore the poultice has remained for one hour, then make it sit upon a seat. Then make it snore and compress its mouth and nose and press the lower part of its belly with gentle pressure. For the pus will come out immediately.
The expulsion of the fetus is upon the knees and hands. When it comes out according to this form, then he will begin gently introducing his hands. Then the woman is laid on her back upon her neck and her feet hang down to the lower part, and she lies upon a bed and entwines her hands. Then press upon her belly gradually. Then draw out what is under her feet from the cushions until she is prepared in the lower part. And if the fetus does not come out then take hold of her feet and move them with a vigorous motion. Then press what is above the hypochondria gradually until the fetus ascends to the upper parts. Then let him introduce his hands and equalize the fetus gradually and order the woman to compress herself until the fetus comes out.
The expulsion of the fetus is horizontal, hanging from the hands. He will begin by reducing his hand. But if he cannot reduce it then raise the woman and make her walk.
But if she cannot walk then make her lie upon a bed and strike her legs with a vigorous concussion.
Then administer the poultice which you have prepared from mucilages.
But if she receives childbirth then make her sit upon a seat. Then strive in reducing the hand and equalize the fetus according to its natural form and make the woman snore and order her to compress herself until it comes out.
The exit of the fetus over its neck, with hands spread out, and its face towards the back of its mother. The midwife must hold the hand of the fetus, then turn it gradually, and equalize it with ease.
When therefore it is equalized, then place upon its vulva what we have described from oil and gums.
Then instruct her to move gradually. When therefore you have done that, then make her sit upon a seat, and instruct her to incline towards her right side. Then instruct her to compress, and make her snort when it is pressed.
The exit of the prepared fetus over its side. When it descends to the orifice of the matrix according to this method with the secundina, then split the secundina with your nail until it is evacuated from moisture.
If therefore it is suspended over the face of the fetus and its neck, then cut it from the umbilicus so that it is not retained and dies.
Then press the fetus towards the interior until you place it according to what is necessary, and instruct the woman to compress herself several times for it will exit by an easy passage.
The Exit of Twins or Many Fetuses. Know that twins are very often born, and sometimes three, and four are born. However that is extraordinary and rarely happens.
Five however is against nature and they do not live entirely and it contains them although they are multiplied, one secundina and they are separated by a sifach distinguishing the ligature in the umbilicus of each of them.
And sometimes they exit just as one fetus exits by a natural, easy passage and they exit by a difficult passage, as we have described.
And if some of them are difficult at childbirth and they arrive at what we said from unnatural figures then take care of them as we have described and let there be a subtle midwife, and she does what she does with ease and slowness and devises according to every figure with what is prepared for her from ingenuity leading to health.
Remember what is formed in the womb from embryos, and are aborted.
Sometimes one is formed in the womb, and two, and three, and four, and five, and six, and seven, and more than ten; and it has already been verified with me that a woman aborting emitted seven, and another fifteen, all formed.
Which was done by the measurement of the glorious wise man.
Therefore all this ought to be in the knowledge of Obstetrics. Perhaps it may happen to her like that one; therefore let her oppose him with what is necessary.
Chapter LXXVI.
On the extraction of a dead fetus.
When you care for a dead fetus as I said in the division from medication, and it does not exit, and you are forced to use surgery with your hand, then it is necessary that you consider.
If therefore the woman is of sound health, and is not with any of the accidents from which her loss is feared: then it is necessary that the woman be laid on her back on a bed, and her head inclined downwards, and her legs elevated; and let attendants hold her from two sides, or she be tied to the bed so that her body is not pulled out at the extraction of the fetus.
Then moisten the mouth of the uterus with oily substances with mucilage of marshmallow, fenugreek, and flaxseed.
Then let the midwife anoint her hand with these oils and mucilages; then let her insert it into the uterus with ease. Then she should seek with it a place in which to fix hooks from the fetus, and places suitable for that.
Consider then, if the fetus is prepared to descend upon its head: then fix hooks into its eyes, or into its neck, or into its mouth, or into its palate, or under its chin, or into its clavicle, or into places near the ribs, and under the hypochondria.
But if the descent is upon his feet, then into the breastbone and his middle ribs.
And it is necessary that he hold the hook with his right hand and put the curve of it into what is between the fingers of his left hand; and introduce it with his hand easily, and fix the hook in some one of those places which we have mentioned, until he reaches the empty part.
Then fix opposite to it another hook, or a third if necessary, so that the contraction is equal and does not incline to one side.
Then let it be extended with equal extension, and let there not be extension according to straightness only, but rather moved to every part so that its outlet is relieved; just as is done with a tooth in its eradication.
And it is necessary in what is between that, that the extension be softened. And if a part is retained from it, then it is necessary that the midwife anoint some fingers with oil, and introduce them from the side, so that she may revolve what is retained with them.
If therefore a part of the fetus goes out, then let the hooks be changed to another place which is higher; do this until the whole fetus goes out.
If therefore the hand goes out before other things, and it is not possible to reduce it because of its constriction, then it is necessary that a cloth be wrapped over it. Then draw it out until the whole of it goes out, cut it either at the joint of the sole or at the elbow; and thus it is necessary that you do in one hand and in both feet.
But if the head of the fetus is large, or constriction happens to it in the outlet, or there is water collected in its head; then it is necessary that he insert between his fingers a Spatulum spinosum, and pierce with it the head, and draw out the water; or break it up itself with an instrument which is called Almisdac; the form of which comes with the forms of the other instruments in the chapter which is after this.
Similarly he should do if the fetus is of a large head, then it is necessary that you make an incision above the shoulder: or extract it with forceps, as we said. Then draw out the bones with forceps.
But if the head emerges and is constricted at the fork: then cut it, until the moisture which is in the chest is poured out.
But if it does not become entangled, nor yield to coming out: then cut the head into pieces, as much as is possible for you.
But if below the abdomen there is an abscess or dropsy: then it is necessary that you perforate it, until the moisture runs out of it.
But if the fetus is on its feet, then its extraction is easy and its direction to the mouth of the womb is slight.
But if it is constricted at the belly or chest: then it is necessary that you extract it with a cloth which you have wound around your hand: and cut the belly or chest until what is inside them runs out.
But if the remaining limbs are removed, and the head returns: then let the midwife introduce her left hand. If therefore the mouth of the womb is open, then let her introduce her hand deep into the womb, and seek the head with it, and draw it up to the mouth of the womb with her fingers: then let her introduce one hook or two hooks into it and draw it out with them.
But if the mouth of the womb constricts because of a hot abscess which happens to it: then it is not necessary that she labors upon it, but rather it is necessary at that time to administer a poultice of humid things, fatty things: and apply plasters; and let her sit in waters which soften and moisten.
But if the fetus is on its side, then it is possible that it will become equal: then administer what we said for a living fetus.
If that is not possible, then it should be cut into pieces and removed.
And you must not leave anything from the second layer inside at all.
Therefore, when you have completed your operation, then administer what we said for hot abscesses that occur in the matrices.
But if there should be a flow of blood, then let it descend into astringent waters, and administer the remaining care.
And I myself have already seen a woman in whose belly a fetus had died; then she became pregnant again with another, afterwards that fetus also died. Therefore, after a long time, an abscess occurred in her navel, and it swelled up until it opened, and it began to discharge pus.
I was therefore summoned to treat her, and I treated her for a long time; but the wound did not heal.
Therefore, I placed upon it some plasters of strong attraction; and an opening came out of the place. Then several days passed, and another opening appeared. I was therefore amazed there, since there is no opening in the belly.
Therefore I thought that they were from the bones of the dead fetuses.
I therefore examined the wound and extracted many bones, with the woman being in her better conditions; and she now lives thus for a long time, and she discharges a little pus from the place.
And I did not affirm this as something foreign, except because there is knowledge in it, and assistance to that which the craftsman physician administers with his hand from healing.
Chapter Seventy-Seven.
Concerning the Forms of Instruments Necessary in the Extraction of a Fetus.
The form of the screw by which the mold is opened.
Below here is the form of a Strictor, as it is made equal in the books, to which two vortexes are equally set at the two ends of two pieces of wood. But this screw must be made thinner than a ligature and to do so it should be made of ebony or boxwood.
And the width of all the wood should be about two fingers and the edge of each of them is about one finger, and the length of each of them is a palm and a half; and in the middle of the two pieces of wood are two additions of the same kind of wood, which are already fixed on both of them, whose length is half a palm and a little more or less; and the width of each is about two fingers, or a little more or less.
And these two additions are inserted into the opening of the mold so that it opens with them when you turn the two screws.
Another form similar to that one, but thinner and lighter.
Let it be made of ebony wood or boxwood according to the likeness of forceps. But at the end of it there should be two additions as you see here above; and the length of each addition from them is about a palm; and the width of each is two fingers.
Therefore when you wish to open the mold with it, then make the woman sit on a bed with her legs hanging down, opening what is between her thighs. Then insert these two joined additions into the mold; and you are holding the lower end of the instrument between her thighs. Then open your hand with the instrument as you do with forceps, equally according to the amount that you wish from the opening of the mold's mouth until the midwife does what she wants.
The form of another screw which the ancients called the Form of Impelling, by which the fetus is impelled.
The form of Almisdac, by which a large head is crushed; and it is similar to forceps, which has teeth at the end as you see.
And sometimes it is oblong, like forceps according to this shape, as you see, which has teeth like the teeth of a saw, with which one cuts and crushes.
The form of Uncini having one point.
The form of Uncini having two points.
The form of Spatula having two ends for cutting off the head.
The form of two Spatulas also wide.
All these instruments are used, and the more their kinds are multiplied and they are prepared by the craftsman, the quicker they are for their operation, and higher among men, because of their quantity.
Therefore do not despise any instrument from them, but let it be prepared by you. For the necessity of them is inexcusable.
Chapter Seventy-Eight.
Concerning the Extraction of the Afterbirth.
When the afterbirth is retained at childbirth, then it is necessary that you order the weak woman to retain her breath. Then have her lie down and strain as if she were constipated; and compress your hand over her mouth and her nostrils. If therefore it comes out through this, it is good.
If not: then take a jar and make a hole in its lid; and put into it herbs that open the mouth of the womb, such as calamint, rue, anise, chamomile, dill, cinnamon cassia, and centaury; take all these herbs or some of them, and submerge them with water, and put them over fire.
Then place a reed tube over the hole of the jar's lid and the other end into the mouth of the womb; and let her hold it until the vapor reaches the cavity of the womb.
Then make her breathe deeply, as we have said. For then it will exit easily.
But if it remains after this treatment and does not exit: then order the midwife to immerse her hand in oil of sesame, or hollyhock mucilage. Then let her introduce it into the vulva and search for the secundina with it. And when she has found it, then let her take hold of it and gradually draw it out.
But if it is attached deep within the uterus, then let her insert her hand, as we have described, until she finds the secundina and gradually draw it out according to its straightness so that the uterus does not fall due to a strong pull; indeed, it is necessary for it to be moved with ease to the right and left sides; then add to the amount of traction. For then it obeys and is preserved and comes loose from its adhesion.
But if the mouth of the uterus is constricted, as we have already described: treat it with sneezing and a medicine from a pot with herbs.
But if it does not exit with all that we have described, then be careful not to tire yourself out by repeatedly attempting the extraction.
Rather, it is necessary that what comes out at the mouth of the uterus be tied to the woman's thigh. Then give her an enema with the tetrapharmacon ointment. For it will putrefy it after a few days and dissolve it so that it exits.
However, when it putrifies, a foul odor rises from it to the stomach and head and harms a weak one. Therefore, suitable fumigations must be administered for that.
Some of the ancients who are already experienced have used the fumigation of watercress and dried figs.
The form of the instrument with which a woman is smoked at the retention of the secundines and menses.
Let it be made of glass like an alembic or of pewter; and let the wide end be placed over the fire; and let the smoking be set upon coals; let her hold it until that smoking is consumed; and repeat another.
Chapter LXXIX.
Concerning the cure of an unperforated anus.
Many boys are born, and their anuses are not perforated, which a subtle suture has already closed. Therefore, it is necessary that the midwife perforate that suture with her finger; and if not, let her perforate it with a sharp spatulim; and let her beware lest the laceration touch it.
Then place upon it wool steeped in wine and oil. Then cure it with ointments until it is healed.
But if you fear that it will close, then place a lead cannula in the foramen for many days, and remove it when the boy wants to defecate.
And now it happens that closure of the anus also occurs from consolidation of a wound or an abscess. Therefore, let that consolidation be cut, then let it be cured with what we said from the cure and lead.
Chapter LXXX.
Concerning the Cure of Fistulas Which Occur in the Lower Part.
Fistulas which occur in the lower part are nodulations and swellings which occur near the anus externally; or in the space of one part; and there is one fistula or several.
When that nodulation becomes old, it opens, and a watery moisture, white, or subtle pus flows from it.
And sometimes these fistulas are penetrating. Therefore penetrating ones are recognized by this, that it flows out from defecation and flatulence, when the sick person uses defecation; and perhaps a worm flows out from them.
And they are fistulas when they are penetrating into space as far as the bladder and as far as the urethra. And they are penetrating as far as the junction of the hip, and as far as the node of the tailbone.
From that which is known of a penetrating fistula to the anus, even from one that is not penetrating, it is, that you insert your index finger into the anus and insert a slender probe into the fistula from metal or iron when there is no tortuosity.
But if there is tortuosity in it, then insert into it a slender probe from lead or a hair from horse hairs until you feel the probe or hair on your finger.
But if you do not feel it deeply, nor does anything proceed from the opening by way of excretion, nor flatulence, nor worm as I said: then know that it is non-penetrating; hasten therefore to treatment.
And when a fistula is penetrating as far as the bladder or as far as the urethra: then its indication is the discharge of urine from it and the prohibition of it from this so that the place may be consolidated with medicine.
But if it is penetrating as far as the junction of the hip or as far as the acetabulum, then its sign is that a probe reaches there if there is no tortuosity in it up to the opening and the discovery of pain in the surrounding area and that pus always issues from it: and that medication is ineffective in it nor does it consolidate with ointment.
And in all these penetrating fistulas there is no cure at all: and the treatment of them is labor and vanity for a foolish physician upon whom it is inflicted.
But in that which is not penetrating and not ancient, healing is hoped for with a knife according to what I tell you and I have already proven: and it is, that the sick person lies between your hands on his back and raise his legs up above his hips and let his hips be inclined towards his belly. Then introduce a lead probe, or one of air, if there is twisting in the fistula, until you know where the probe reaches.
But if he feels it weak around the anus, then it is necessary that you insert your index finger into the anus.
If therefore you feel the probe already penetrated through itself, beyond which you feel between your finger and it, or flesh: then you know for sure that it is penetrating: do not therefore labor on it, because it is not its healing, as we said.
They however said, that the flesh is healed in some men, and from the medication for which he hopes for benefit, it is, to ignite a subtle cautery according to the width of the fistula, as precedes, and to introduce it ignited into the fistula, until it reaches around the anus. Then repeat that twice, or three times until you know, that all those added filtered flesh, which are similar to a bird's quill pen, have now been burned.
Then care for it with linen cloths wrapped in butter, until those burned flesh depart: then care for it with ointments generating flesh and consolidating.
If therefore it heals, it is good.
And if not, then it never heals with any other treatment.
And if the probe exits and does not reach your finger which is in the anus, and there is a thick membrane of flesh or sifac between it and the probe: and you see the fistula following the surface of the skin, then cut the skin from the beginning of the fistula, and you will cut with the probe what is in the fistula, until the section reaches where the extremity of the probe arrives, and the probe is freed and falls.
Then clean those filtered flesh, which are assimilated to a bird's quill pen, so that nothing remains from them.
If, however, blood overcomes you and dissolves between you and your operation so that you sever those flesh: then it is better, and quicker relief, to cauterize it with fire or with a sharp medicine.
Because a cautery with fire gathers good qualities, it adds burning of those added flesh and cutting off blood and drying up moistures.
Then care for it with linen wrapped in butter or chopped stalks until the place emits pus and those burnt flesh go out into the pus.
Then care for then the wound with hardening ointments, those that produce hard flesh: and there are medicines in which are astringency and drying.
For when they are restored, then they already heal: and you do not fear repetition.
But if the fistula has already reached to the depth of the anus and is elongated from excess of the body: then insert your finger into the anus and inquire with it.
If therefore you feel a probe and between you and it there is a membrane of fat or flesh and it is near penetration: then there is no operation on it except according to the way of trust and hope.
Which is because it is administered in it one of three ways: either that you cauterize it itself as we said or that you cut it until you reach its cavity so that it is possible for you to insert linen and care from next. And do not proceed much with the cutting so that you do not fall into the sphincter containing the anus and it happens to the weak a discharge of excretion without will. Then care for it with what we said.
Perhaps it will heal or you lengthen the membrane itself and cleanse it after you dry it up to near the anus as we said either with a probe or with another instrument of sharp extremity. Then cleanse those filtered flesh that are in the cutting up to where you can with examination.
Then try to restore the whole wound with a section and an upper opening where it is consolidated, and there will remain an open fistula in the inner part of the anus, and it will be easier for the sick person.
And sometimes a fistula follows in this way: and it is when you insert a probe into the fistula: and it is on the side of the anus around the surface of the body with skin and the margin of the anus; then take a probe with a perforated end, which is like an awl of aleskef, that is, prepared leather, according to this form. And insert into it a thread wrapped from five threads, and around it. Then insert the probe with the thread into the fistula until it reaches its cavity.
Therefore, if it is penetrating into the inner margin of the anus nearby, then pull the thread out of that hole: so that you insert your finger into the anus and pull out the end of the thread, and join the two ends together: and tighten both, and let it go for one or two days; and the more the thread falls into the flesh, tighten it well, until those pieces of flesh that are between the two ends of the thread fall off; then care for the wound until it is consolidated and healed.
But if the opening of the fistula is not penetrating, then make it penetrate in any way that is prepared for you, unless it is very deep. For it is not fitting for you to do that because of a tear, lest you fall into it.
Then do as we said with it until it is healed.
The shape of a spathulated spine with which the fistula is divided, whose curvature is very sharp, and the other part is not sharp, so that it does not fall into what is not necessary, as you see.
Chapter LXXXI
On the stitching of hemorrhoids, from which blood runs from their incision: and the care of fissures.
There are hemorrhoids according to two types. For they are either within the anus, similar to bladders of coals or prunes; or as if they themselves are grains of a bean. And from them are small ones and large ones: and blood runs from them always, and it is one, and there are many. And they are outside the anus, and at its extremities.
But these which are outside the anus are according to most few in moisture, from which runs a yellow water, or little blood with a constant flow; and they are according to the color of the body.
And the treatment of that which is within the anus is, that you command the sick person to sit: and it should be pressed until the anus emerges, and the hemorrhoids appear to you. Therefore suspend them with hooks; or hold them with your fingernail. Then cut into them at their roots.
But if hooks are not retained in them because of their moisture and softness: then take them with a rough cloth, or with a swab made from double wool, and draw them out with your fingers. Then cut into them, and sprinkle over them after the incision certain hot powders, so that they stand in place of a cautery. Or cauterize them according to what preceded in the chapter on cauterization. Then treat them with the remaining treatment until they are healed.
But if the anus does not yield to protrusion, then give the sick person an enema in which there is a little mordant, so that it washes with what is in the anus, and yields to protrusion quickly when the sick person strains.
But if the hemorrhoids are external from the anus, then their matter is light, easy: and it is like you should take them with your fingernail or take them with a hook, and cut into them. Then care for them with what we have said until they are healed.
And whoever recoils from incision with a knife, then it is necessary that you administer their suture in this manner. Take an unraveled thread and insert it into the eye of a needle. Then draw the thread upward and make it penetrate with the needle at its root on one side; and entwine the two ends of the thread in the lower part of the needle, so that they are parallel; and tighten the thread with a strong knot. Then tie the thread and withdraw the needle.
Do this with all the threads, and leave one out of them; do not draw it tight so that excess blood may run from it.
Then place a cloth soaked in rose oil or carrot juice on top of it and put it over the wound; and instruct the patient to rest. Then leave him until it falls off.
When therefore the threads fall off, care for them with ointments and the remaining treatment until they are healed.
Concerning the Care of Fisures. For they often happen from the drying up of feces and constriction of nature.
When therefore they become hardened and medicine does not work in them; then it is necessary that you scrape them with a spatula point or with your fingernail until they become moist and the upper layer which prevents them from consolidating is removed. Then care for them until they consolidate as needed.
But if they do not consolidate; then repeat them with a more vigorous scraping than the first, until they bleed much and are flayed, then care for them until they are healed.
Concerning the Treatment of Hemorrhoids Inverted and Not Inverted; and Threads Dry and Ants.
Chapter 82.
Know that a hemorrhoid is nothing but a round knot according to the color of the body, similar to the head of a nail; and it happens throughout the body, especially in the lower parts of the feet and fingers; and pain arises from them upon walking.
Therefore it is necessary that what is in the circumference of the clavicle be cut and be held with forceps, or with a hook, and be eradicated from its root.
Then take care of that; and if you wish, cauterize it according to what preceded in its chapter.
But from the dried athel one is a small eminence, rough, filtered, round above the surface of the body. And they often occur in the hands of boys.
And its treatment is, that you extend the athel, and cut it; or you tie it with silk thread; or with a hair until it falls off. And if you wish, cauterize it according to what was previously mentioned with fire, or with a sharp medicine; and now inverted athel are eradicated, and those not inverted with the instrument that I shall say.
Furthermore, a formica is also a small filtered eminence, thick, above the surface of the body, going deep; and when it finds cold, it feels like a ant bite and spreads throughout the body; and most of it occurs in the hands.
And its treatment is that you take a cannula from a goose feather, or a strong vulture feather; and place the cannula over the formica until the circle of the cannula contains its parts. Then revolve your hand with the cannula until you cut what is in the circumference of the formica; and place it deep into the flesh, and eradicate the formica from its root.
And if you wish, do that with a cannula of air, or iron, according to this form.
Let the upper part of the cannula be drawn upward and covered, so that the apprehension on it is relieved and its rotation itself.
And if you wish, cauterize the formica according to what was previously mentioned in the cauterization of the athel in the chapter on cauterization.
Chapter Eighty-Three.
Of the forms of instruments which are employed for fistulas in diseases of the anus, and opening of the abdomen, and colic.
Sometimes an enema is made of silver, or of brass, or of cast bronze, or hammered.
And sometimes it is made of these instruments small and large, according to those in which they are administered.
Therefore that which is administered in the healing of very young children should be small; and those whose anuses are weak, or painful, so too their enemas.
A large enema.
The cane of it, that is, the upper head in which the bladder is tied, should be wide according to this manner; and let there be a distinction so that the bladder is tied as you see, and the lower extremity which is introduced into the anus should be deaf, that is, not open, smooth, very drawing towards subtlety: and on one side of it let there be two holes, and on the other side one hole.
And let the width of the hole be according to the thickness of the rays, and a little thicker; and let the bladder in which the medicines are placed be from an animal's bladder: or from sheepskin parchment, which should be according to the form of a small wineskin: whose root is one palm.
And let it be perforated with many holes as it is turned, and the space which is between every hole should be the thickness of a finger. Then insert into those holes a strong thread from raw silk, which should be from ten threads or thereabouts, and join it with a bladder like a wineskin. When therefore the bladder is placed in it, tie it tightly in the head of the enema between two distinct constrictions. Then administer the enema with it.
And this is the shape of the circle bladders here depicted and their holes.
The quality of giving an enema with it is: to fill a syringe or bladder from medicine which is warmed according to the amount tolerated by the sick person; and be careful that it is not very hot or very cold.
Then the sick person is laid on their back and raises their legs upwards; and put underneath them a mattress, that is, skin on which people lie, or a sheet.
Then smear the end of the enema with oil, or with egg white, or with fenugreek mucilage, or similar things. Then insert it into the anus easily. Then let the attendant grasp the syringe with both hands together and squeeze it forcefully until they have emptied everything that is in the enema into the intestines.
Then withdraw the enema, and let the sick person raise their legs to the wall.
And if the medicine compels them to defecate, then they should hold it as long as they can. And if they sleep with it all night, it is better for healing.
But if you give an enema to them in illnesses of abdominal dropsy, and their defecation is hurried; then repeat the enema until they are healed.
But if there are hemorrhoids or painful abscesses in the anus, then let the enema be very subtle and gentle, from the enemas with which children are given enemas.
And this is the form of a subtle enema for when the skin is tight.
Chapter 84.
I have already said in the division on the treatment of wounds what was appropriate for the book, and I will say here their treatment with the hand and with medicines according to supplementation.
Therefore I say that wounds are diversified according to what they are done by and according to the place on which the wound falls.
Therefore, the things by which wounds are often made are like the throwing of a stone: or a cut with a sword, or a knife; or a blow with a spear, or wood, or an arrow; and similar things from many objects.
But wounds which are made according to the parts of the body are like a wound that falls on the head, or neck, or chest, or belly, or thigh; and similar ones from those limbs.
And I am relating the treatments of some wounds so that I may put this reasoning and rule over the remaining wounds.
And I will begin with simple head wounds; because the recollection of compound ones will come at the beginning of the third chapter.
Therefore, I say. When a simple wound occurs on the head and there is no fracture of the bone; then consider if it is from the throw of a stone, or things similar to it, and has already broken only the skin; and the wound is large, and you fear for a bad outcome of hot abscess; then hurry and perform a cephalic phlebotomy immediately; and do not delay it; and draw out blood from him according to the quantity of his strength: if there is nothing forbidding phlebotomy, especially if the blood that comes out from the wound is little. And let the phlebotomy be from the opposite side of the wounded part.
And let the phlebotomy be from the opposite side of the wounded part. And let the sick person beware for himself against repletion, and wine, and coarse foods.
But if something forbids reduction, then let him reduce from food, and not approach meats, nor wines.
And put on the wound if a hot abscess happens to him, cotton infused in rose oil alone, or with wine in which there is astringency.
But if you are safe from a hot abscess, then put on the wound, if it is fresh with its own blood and has not been changed by air, the powder which I will relate a little later: and if it has already been changed by air, then put on it certain ointments until it heals.
But if the wound is large, or is from a cut by a sword, and similar weapons: and the lips thereof do not come together by stitches, then join it with suture, according to what I shall relate about wounds of the belly and their suturing.
But if the wound has already been torn from the bone, and hangs down, and there is no retention of it except in a small suspensory, then cut it. Then treat the wound with medicines, and restore hard flesh in it in place of the skin, and instruct the sick person to nourish themselves with foods which have substance, such as heads, and cooked wheat, until they are plump; and those similar.
And if you find putrefaction in the skin, and it does not adhere to the bone: then cut out all the putrefaction, and throw it away; then treat that [area].
But if in these wounds there is an artery, or vein from which blood flows: and it is not severed by medicines, then inquire above the artery. If therefore you find it not cut, then cut it with a Spatula, or tie it off. But if necessity compels when that which we said does not apply, then cauterize it until the blood is severed.
But if these wounds are small, simple, then their matter is easy, light: so that you sprinkle on them this powder: because it will consolidate them before they change in the air.
Method of Making Incarnated Powder. Take one part of frankincense, and two parts of dragon's blood, and three parts of unquenched lime: mix the whole well, and sift it; and fill the wound from it, and constrict it with a good constriction until it adheres well upon it, and coagulates with the blood.
Then constrict it from above with stitches, and leave it alone; and do not loosen it until it remains, so that it does not discharge. For it is not eradicated by medicine until it is healed.
And sometimes lime alone does this when you do not have frankincense at hand, and dragon's blood also makes the same operation in many large wounds, when it is suitable to put it on them and bind them up, and they are recent wounds with their own blood which has not been changed by air.
But if the air has already changed it somewhat, especially when it is in the summer season, then the application of this powder is not in order; therefore care for it with what we have said, so that you put on it certain digestive ointments: or put on it poultices made from barley flour, made with water and honey, until they discharge pus. Then care for the rest with further treatment until they are healed.
But if it should happen that a fracture occurs in the bone with the wound, and it is small, then extract it with forceps; and I will already say the treatment of skull fractures in what is to come.
Of wounds of the neck.
But if a wound should happen in the neck, then there is no difference in its treatment and that of a head wound when it is simple.
But if a nerve or artery of the neck has already been cut: then if it is a nerve, there is no remedy for it except that its treatment be joined with that which produces strong astringency, such as alum and saffron, and those similar to them. For they injure nerves and contract them.
And do not put anything cold at all on the wound; for the substance of nerves is cold, and its continuation is with the brain, which is more sublime than other members; and let that with which it is treated be from mild medicines, such as lime slaked with fresh water repeatedly infused with oil, or with rose oil, and tutty when washed also with fresh water: and the remaining mineral stones in this manner, and soft ointments.
If, however, the wound is large, then apply a suture; or bring its lips together with lint. Then care for it until it heals.
But if the wound has depth, or a sinus occurs in its lower part, in which pus already collects, then perforate it in a more depressed place within it.
But if an artery has already been incised in the wound and its flow of blood follows: then separate or cauterize it, and bind it; or cauterize it, if necessity compels it to be done so.
But if the spondyles of the throat have already fallen, or the whole thing, and the alhaudig are saved: then join the lips of the skin with a suture over the trachea, and do not touch the throat, rather equalize it, and return it to its natural form. Then constrict it with a decent ligature, and leave it for several days. Then care for it with ointments which have astringency and desiccation, until it heals.
But if the wound has already made an impression on the bone, then explore it: then if there are fragments of bone in it, extract them all, or as many as you can from them; and what is not prepared, leave it until the wound putrifies, and separates and relieves its exit.
But if the wound is recent, then sprinkle over it the powder which I have described. But if bone appears to be seen in the wound and a fistula is formed: then treat it with what its recollection comes to in its chapter.
On a Wound on the Fingers.
If there is a strike from a spear or knife and you see its depth, then consider it. But if windiness comes out from the wound, when a weak person breathes from it, then know that the wound is mortal.
If it is not deep, and fresh with its own blood, then do not put powder on it from the first hour, nor bind it up, so that the blood may not be retained in its depth, and return to the heart, and kill the sick man; but apply an attractive ointment to it, and dissolve it twice a day.
But if you do not have ointment at hand, then put an old rag in the opening of the wound, so that it may draw out what comes forth from it of moistures; and make the sick man sleep upon the wound, so that what is gathered in it may flow.
But if three days or more have already passed since the wound, and there does not happen to the sick man spasm, nor evil trembling of the heart, nor constriction in breathing, and you see the remaining dispositions of his are good: then know that the wound is well. Therefore take care of it with dressings, and other treatment, until it heals.
But if its healing is excused, and it is now always open, then know that a fistula has already been made. Therefore take care of it from its own chapter.
And if the wound is from a cut of a sword, or knife, and it is simple on the surface of the chest, or back: then take care of it with what preceded from suture, if it is large; or with powder, if it is small.
But if it has already made an impression on the bone, and chips come out of it: then examine the wound, and hurry to extract those chips, if they are separate. But if they are not separate, then leave them until the wound putrifies, for their extraction is alleviated then.
But judgment in the treatment of other accidents of wounds in the remaining limbs is the judgment that we have said.
Concerning wounds of the belly, and the exiting of intestines, and their issue.
Disruption which occurs in the abdomen is great, medium, and small.
From a great disruption, therefore, sometimes intestines or a number of intestines protrude.
Therefore their introduction is made, and their suture more vehement and difficult.
And even a small disruption is difficult in another way. It therefore follows that their reduction is more difficult if you do not hasten in introducing them to their place immediately; because it swells, opens, and enlarges; therefore its entry becomes difficult.
Because of this, therefore, a medium disruption is better; because with it the reduction of the intestine is not difficult as it becomes difficult in these two species.
Know that the suture of a vein is according to four methods.
Two sutures are common and apply to the suture of the abdomen, and to the suture of other wounds of the body; and two other sutures specifically pertain to the remaining wounds. However, they are more appropriate with the suture of the abdomen; and I will explain each one to you with description and explanation.
I say, therefore, that when a wound is small, and something protrudes from it out of the intestine, and it is difficult to reduce it; then this is due to one of two methods. Either because of the smallness of the disruption, as we have said; or because there is inflation in the intestine because of the cold air.
When therefore it is so, then you must warm it, so that you submerge a sponge or cloth moistened in lukewarm water alone. Or you boil in water fennel, papyrus, and saffron and embrace the intestine with it until the inflation is resolved.
Sometimes it makes wine in which there is astringency; and it is better than water alone in resolving inflation.
When therefore you see the swelling already resolved, then moisten the intestine with water in which marshmallow or mullein has been boiled. For through that its entrance is eased with a small thing.
But if its return is excused after this treatment, then make a small incision in the disruption with the instrument with which hemorrhoids are cut.
And the first treatment is better when it is prepared than a section; and compulsion is not made to cut after necessity and lack of instrument.
Let one part of it be twisted and sharp, and the other part not sharp, and let the subtle end not be with the subtlety of a spatula, rather let it be somewhat duller: and it is that which resembles a chisel, as you see.
When therefore the wound is enlarged and enters the intestine, then it is necessary that there be a reduction of it according to its own natural form in its proper place, if possible above it: for it is better.
When, however, the disruption is wide in the lower part of the belly: then it is necessary that you make the sick person lie on his back and put his legs higher than his head. And if it is in the upper part of the belly, then put his head and chest higher than his lower parts.
And similarly if the disruption is in one of the two parts of the belly, then put your intention and your consideration always on this, that the part in which the wound is be higher than the other part.
And thus it is necessary that you administer it in large wounds and in middle wounds.
But in small wounds, put it according to what is prepared for you.
Then make a pious minister be present between your hands who will hold the whole disruption with his hand and bring together its lips. Then uncover from it for him who follows a suture something after something; and this is the way of a common single suture.
And take a needle, or a sharp-pointed instrument, according to the size of the wound. Then let fall from the extremity of the disruption the width of a ring finger, that is, of a physician; and fix the needle one span beyond what you are about to introduce the thread into it in two places at the skin, with two extremities of sifac which is under the skin from within, until you make it penetrate from the other side, and you have now joined the two extremities of the skin and the two extremities of sifac; and there become four rows.
Then tighten with a double thread around the needle several times on both sides at once, until the two lips of the wound are joined in a decent manner.
Then let fall the width of a finger also, and fix another needle into them; then draw it tight with thread, just as you did with the first needle. Therefore do not cease doing that with as many needles as you need until you complete the constriction of the whole wound.
And let the needles be of a medium size between thickness and thinness; for thin needles pierce the flesh very quickly, and thick ones are difficult to insert into the skin; therefore it is necessary that they should be medium in thinness and thickness.
And it is not necessary that the needle be fixed in the two extremities of the skin very close together; lest the flesh be pierced quickly and the wound opened before its consolidation, and lest they also be elongated with the suture so as not to prevent the wound from consolidating.
Then cut off the ends of the needles so that they do not injure the sick man in his sleep; and put little tufts of cloth on all sides to hold the ends of the needles; and leave them until you know that the wound is already consolidated.
And this indeed, kind of suture with needles thus is more suitable for small wounds. For it suffices in the suture of those, one needle, or two, or about that number.
The second suture is common, so that the aggregates with the suture join the four ends: two ends of the skin and two ends of the sifac in one stitch, with a needle in which is wrapped a thread equal in subtlety and thickness.
Then when you have penetrated with the needle these four ends, draw back the needle from the part where you began, so that the drawn thread falls from the upper part of the wound; so that the suture is like the suture of cloth by which cloths are tied, and place between every suture an amount of thickness less than a finger.
But men know this suture.
And with this suture I healed a wound that had happened to a man in his belly, who had been wounded with a knife, and the disruption of the wound was greater than one palm: and about two palms had come out from the intestines from the middle intestine, and the disruption was in the middle of the belly.
I therefore returned it after his bowels had stood outside the wound for twenty-four hours.
The wound was therefore consolidated in a space of about fifteen days; and I took care of it, and it was healed; and he lived after that for many years, converted to his own affairs.
And the physicians had judged that it would not be healed.
And from a miracle it was, that I did not care for it with ointment. For I was in a place where ointment was not found, nor anything of medicine. Therefore I put old linen over the wound twice a day, and I diligently washed it with honey water until it was healed.
But I shall tell two kinds of proper sutures according to the account of speech.
And it is, that you begin with a sharp suture; and you introduce the needle from the outer to the inner part.
When therefore the needle penetrates into the skin, and into the muscle going according to the length and breadth of the whole body: let the end of the sifac fall in the side in which you introduced the needle, and make the other end penetrate from the inner part to the outer part in the other end marvel.
When therefore you have made it to penetrate, then make it to penetrate thus in this same extremity, from the exterior part to the interior: and let go the extremity of the sifac, which is on this side, and make the point to penetrate into the other extremity from the interior part to the exterior; and make it to penetrate with your penetration that you make it to penetrate into the sifac extremity, which is in its part, until it penetrates into the whole.
Then begin also from this same side, and draw it up with the extremity which is from the sifac on the other side, and extract the point from the skin which it has penetrated.
Then return it to that skin, and draw the sifac extremity which is on the other side with this mirach extremity; and extract it from the skin which is from its part, and do that vice versa, one after another: and do that vice versa, until you have drawn out all its extrinsic part according to that example.
And the sum of this suture is that its suture should be like the sutures of skinners, so that its sifac vice versa on one side with one extremity of skin; and that you should let it go vice versa on the other side, until you complete it.
The second kind of proper suture, which Galen mentioned.
His speech is narrated so that its suture should be according to the similarity which some physicians have, in such a way that they bring together all the part to its fitting counterpart by nature: and they apply the sifac extremity to its other extremity, and the mirach extremity to its other extremity. And that is done according to what I tell you.
It is necessary that you fix the point into the mirach extremity nearest to you from the exterior part, and make it penetrate to the interior in that alone; and let go the two sifac extremities. Then turn the point, and make it penetrate from the exterior part to the interior into the two sifac extremities, namely on both sides.
Then turn both, and also make both penetrate from the inside to the outside, into the two extremities of the other side.
And this method of suture is better than the common easy suture, and it is the suture of which a reminder preceded.
This is therefore also the speech of Galen.
And some said. The suture of the belly is corrected with two needles. Which is, to insert one thread into both of them; and begin with the insertion of the needle, from what is at your side, and make it penetrate up to the other side, according to the suture of those who are skins equally.
And know that when the disruption is in the middle of the belly, then its suture is more difficult than that of the other places of the belly.
But the judgment of the medication of a wound, is the judgment of other wounds.
Which is, because when you obtain a fresh wound with its own blood, before the air changes it; and you reduce the intestine, and its own, and fit it; then place powder of incarnating on top.
But if the air has already changed the wound: then place some ointments, which are present to you, on top of it until they make pus; and the threads fall off, and the sifac and mirach become consolidated. Then take care of it as is the care of other wounds, until it is healed.
But if medicines are not present to you, then immediately place on it, from which it begins to emit pus, old cot; and change it twice a day, as I taught you, until it is healed.
For you do not need other medication in many dispositions, when the wounds are simple.
But if you fear that the wound communicates to the principal members in injury, then it is necessary for you to immerse soft wool in oil of tempered heat, or in rose oil; and place it in the circumference of the places which are between the groins, and under the armpits.
If he feels pain, or pricking, or putrefaction in his bowels (for this often happens) then give him an enema with dark, thick, warm wine; especially if the putrefaction has already reached the intestine and is about to penetrate to its cavity.
And know that what is from the large intestine is easier to heal; and what is from the small intestines is harder to heal.
But the intestine which is called Jejunum does not admit healing from a wound that happens in it at all; and this is because of its multitude, which is of veins, and their size, and because of the subtlety of its body, and the proximity of its nature to nerves.
But if what proceeds from the wound is a protuberance, and you obtain it fresh; reduce it according to your reduction, by which you reduce intestines equally.
But if a space has passed for it, and it has already become green, or black; then it is necessary that you tie it with thread above the place which has been darkened from it, so that flow of blood does not occur. For in a protuberance are veins and arteries.
Then cut what is beyond that ligament, and put two ends of the thread hanging from the lower part of the wound outside it; so that relief comes upon you from its attraction, and extraction of itself in case of the protuberance, and emission of pus from the wound.
Recollection of a Wound That Happens in the Intestine.
When a rupture occurs in the intestine and it is small; then it is possible that it may be restored in some people: therefore, because I have seen one who was wounded in his belly with a thrust of a spear, and the wound was from the right of the stomach, and the wound was old; and a fistula was made, and there issued from it discharge, and flatulence.
Therefore I applied treatment until I was no longer anxious about its healing; nor did I cease to dress it carefully until it was healed and the place consolidated.
After I saw the place was already consolidated, I feared for the sick man, lest some evil accident should befall him in his belly; and no such evil accident befell him.
