And he remained in a better state of health, eating and drinking, and sleeping soundly, and he entered the bath, and exercised himself in his daily routines.
And certain experimenters have now said that when a wound occurs in the intestine and is small, then it ought to be ligated in this manner. And it is, that you should take ants of large heads. Then bring together the two lips of the wound and place one ant with an open mouth upon the two lips of the wound. When its head is thus upon them and it clenches its jaws, cut off its head; for it adheres and will not be loosened.
Then place another ant near the first and do not cease to do this with ant after ant according to the total extent of the wound.
Then bind it up and its wound. For those heads will remain attached within the intestine until the intestine is nourished and healed, and no harm will befall the sick man inwardly.
And it is now possible that the intestine may also be ligated with a fine thread; which has been drawn out from the intestine of an animal and attached to it after it has been introduced into a needle.
And it is, that you should take the end of this thread from the intestine and it should be scraped off very much. Then tie a fine linen thread around its end. Then insert that thread into a needle and sew the intestine with it. Then bind it up in its dressing.
And this method of suture with thread and gut is not except according to the way of trust and hope.
Know that a wound which is large, wide, especially if it is in one of the slender intestines: then there is no skill except refinement, and there is no healing of it at all.
On the treatment of fistulae and ulcers.
Know that every wound or abscess when it grows old and becomes chronic, and becomes an ulcer, and does not consolidate, and emits pus always, and is not cut off: then it is called universally wherever it may be a fistula, and we call it a zulchemen.
And a fistula indeed is according to truth a knotted filtration, hard, white: with which there is no pain, which has the concavity of a bird's feather: and on account of that some call it risata, that is, a feather.
And it is in certain hours moist, which emits pus. And perhaps the moisture is split in certain hours. And sometimes this moisture is much, and is little: and it is thick, and it is subtle.
And I already stated previously in the division, then when I said wounds, that every ulcer which does not heal, nor is flesh born in it, it is not except because of one of nine new causes.
Of which one is, either because of the scarcity of blood in the body; or because of its malice; or because between it and on its lips there is hard flesh, prohibiting the birth of flesh; or because it is full of much filth and dirtiness; or because the ulcer in itself is putrid, and the material which is sent to it is of bad quality; or because the medicine is unsuitable in its treatment; or because corruption falling into the region is a pestilential kind; or through a property in the region, as happens in Caesaraugusta: in which digestion of illnesses becomes difficult, and healing of abscesses is delayed by nature; or because there is one bone, or a number of bones in it.
And I have already shown all these causes and their signs, and their treatments with medicines in the division; and it is necessary here for me to make you know the treatment of Fistula and Zuken, which is done by way of operation with the hand.
Therefore know that Fistula occurs in all the members of the body. For from them are Fistulas which reach the large veins and arteries, or nerves, or sinews; or to the intestine, or to the bladder, or to one of the ribs: or to one of the vertebrae of the back, or to one of the joints composed of composition; such as the hand, and foot: and from them are those which go to the proximity of the principal member, and similar ones.
Which therefore of these Fistulas is in this way, is from illnesses difficult of healing, and does not receive treatment, and occupation with it is toil and ignorance.
But if it is from Fistulas some of whose depth does not reach these places which I said: and you hope, and trust in its treatment, and wish to arrive at the knowledge of it, then take a probe of bronze, or iron, if the Fistula tends according to straightness, and explore it with it.
But if there is crookedness in the Fistula, then investigate it with a probe of thin lead. For lead because of the gentleness of its body is relieved, and curves around the crookedness.
But if the Fistula has many openings, it is not possible for you to indicate over it with a probe. Therefore cleanse from them an opening out of its openings.
For the moisture with which you cleanse it, goes towards the other openings, and runs from them.
Then carefully inquire according to what way it is possible for you: that you may know that there is a bone, or nerve, and the Fistula's concavity is distant, or near: or that the Fistula is single which has many openings, and stand upon all of it, with your utmost strength: with inquiry on your part, by which you question the sick person, and discovery of pain at pressure with your hand upon the place, and similar ones from signs and causes happening to a boil.
When therefore you have stood upon all these true stations, then go to the cure according to confidence: and it is, that you consider then, if there is a fistula nearby apparent, or in a safe place far from the joint, or from a nerve, or artery, or vein, or one of the places which I told you: then cut the fistula according to what has been said in my narration, and remove what is in it from filtration, and corrupt flesh, which is born in its orifice from flesh, or thelul, and similar things: and care for it until it heals.
But if the fistula is of distant depth, and is according to straightness: then it is necessary that you cut it deeply as much as is possible for you. Then cleanse it from all its corrupt flesh.
Then administer linen wrapped in sharp medicines: and insert it up to the concavity of the fistula which you comprehended with iron: do this repeatedly with it until the sharp medicine corrodes all that remains in the concavity of the fistula from corruption.
Then restore it with ointments which generate healthy flesh until it heals.
But if it does not heal through that, then cauterize it according to what we have narrated.
Therefore if the cause of the fistula is a bone, and it is made certain to you: then inquire into it and cut it according to what I have narrated to you: if nothing prevents you from a vein, or nerve, or main member as we said.
If therefore the bone is revealed to you, and there is something of corruption and blackness in it: then scrape it until all its corruption is removed. Then care for it with that which makes flesh upon it until it heals.
But if it does not heal and emits pus as it was doing: then know that you have not reached the total eradication of the corruption.
Therefore reveal it again, and make a careful examination of it, and cleanse it with your ultimate strength.
Then restore it yourself. Therefore if it heals, it is good; and if not, then you will know that corruption is deep within the body, which you have not reached. Therefore there is no skill for you in it except to leave it to nature.
But if the mouth of a young child is corrupted and it is possible for you, draw it out with fine forceps suited to it.
But if there are many bones, then make a careful drawing out of them all, and do not leave anything behind with your strength.
But if something opposes you from the humors and there is no fear or mistake in cutting them: then cut into those humors and flesh if they are there and free the bones.
But if you do not have preparation for taking out the bones at your time, then place over the wound what will cause it to decay itself; and leave it until that which is around those bones decays from flesh and humors.
And see that the wound does not solidify. And let the incision which you made in your operation be tightened. Rather place over it, if you fear, a cloth steeped in sulfur ground with oil; or a cloth steeped in Egyptian ointment, or some emerald ointments.
For when those humors decay and separate from the bones, removal and their drawing out are eased.
But if there is one large bone, such as the bone of the leg or the hip bone and similar to these, and what is corrupted from it is a large part; then scrape it with the last resort until that blackness and corruption are removed. Then restore the wound.
But if what is corrupted in it is a large part and the corruption has already reached the marrow of the bone, then it is necessary to saw it and cut it all the way to the place where the corruption has reached; then care for it until it heals.
And I tell you about a man who had a wound on his leg: so that you may take it as an example and aid for your operation.
There was a young man of about thirty years old, to whom pain occurred in his leg from a cause which had moved upon him within the body, that is, from within; until the matter reached his leg and there formed a large abscess: and he did not have an external cause.
Time was extended with him in error of the physicians until the abscess was opened and much matter flowed from it. And his treatment was postponed until his leg became fistulated and many openings were made in it; which all emitted pus and the body's humors.
Many physicians treated him for about two years, and there was no one wise in the art of the hand among them until I was called upon and saw his leg and the materials which were flowing from those openings with a great flow: indeed, the whole body of the man had already wasted away, and his color was citron.
Therefore I introduced a probe into one of the openings and it reached the bone.
Then I examined all the openings and found that they reached each other from all parts of the leg.
I hastened then and cut above one of those openings until I exposed a part of the bone; and I found it corrupted, which had already been corroded and blackened, and putrefied, and was perforated until it penetrated to the marrow.
I sawed off what was revealed to me and prepared it from the corrupted bone.
And I determined that there was nothing in the bone except that corruption which I had cut off and sawed. And now I had eradicated it.
Then I placed the restoration of the wound with consolidating medicines over a long space. And it did not consolidate.
Then I probed and found above the second opening, above the first discovery, a continuous corruption with bone; and I sawed what appeared to me also from that corruption.
Then I tried its restoration. And it was not restored.
Therefore I discovered above it again and did not cease to cut the bone into pieces and try its restoration; and it was not restored until I cut about a palm from the bone and extracted it with its marrow.
Then I restored it with medications and it quickly consolidated and healed; and this iteration was not necessary in its operation and section except for the weakness of the sick person's constitution, and his lack of tolerance, and my fear for him regarding death: because he suffered from evil syncopes in all hours from excess evacuation.
He was therefore healed with complete healing; and firm flesh grew in place of the bone, and a good arrangement was made in his body, and his strength returned; and he returned to his former conditions; and it did him no harm at all in walking.
But if the protruding bone in the body is already broken, then it is necessary to saw it according to this method; and it is: to take a ligament and tie it at the end of the protruding bone; and instruct someone to extend it upward; and put another ligament made of coarse wool after the first ligament. Then tie it over the flesh which is under the bone and extend its extremity so that you draw the flesh downwards; and you expose the flesh above the place which you wish to saw so that the saw does not injure the flesh.
Place wood or a board under the bone below decently; because when you do this, it is not prevented from cutting the corrupted flesh.
And it is necessary that the saw be over the corrupted place for a short time so that there is no corruption in the hollow of the bone; and it does not appear to the sense in its appearance and compels it to the vice of its own juice.
If the mouth is corrupted and is not prominent but continues with its part, and the corruption is in its middle or in a part of it, then expose all the flesh from all sides. Then place wood underneath.
Then saw it from the first part, where the corruption is, until the saw continues on the other side; and let the saw be according to the length of the corruption a little further, as we have said.
But if the corruption is at the junction, cut the corrupt junction itself, and saw the mouth where it continues on the other side.
But if the corruption is in the continuation of two junctions, then there is no artifice in it except abrasion.
But if the corruption is in the comb of the hand or in the comb of the foot, then the matter is very difficult. But it is necessary for you to inquire how the corruption appears to you. And abrade it and cleanse it according to whatever arrangement is possible for you, and with whatever artifice is rectified for you: when it does not cause opposition to you by vein or nerve.
And know that there are many cutting and sawing instruments for cutting these bones, according to the position of the bones, and their preparation, and their thickness, and subtlety, and magnitude, and smallness, and hardness, and rarity. Therefore it is necessary that you prepare an instrument suitable for that operation of every species.
And know that these operations signify to you above the kind of instrument with which you are in need when you have a long study. And the knowledge of the methods of this art is a testimony to the methods of illnesses; for then you will already find by yourself what suits it from the instruments for every infirmity.
And I affirm to you in the last of this chapter a number of instruments which you put examples on which you cut and a test by which you try on others.

And it is not fitting except that the heads of joins be rubbed with it when they are corrupted or when a wide and large mouth is present.

On the incision of extremities and the Locking of Bones.
Extremities putrefy either from an external or internal cause.
When therefore you have treated that corruption with medical care, and the treatment does not help; and you see the corruption fall upon a limb which it does not affect: then it is necessary that you cut into that limb until you reach the corruption, so that the sick person may be delivered from death, or from the evil which is greater by the loss of the limb.
And the sign of this happening is that the limb becomes dark, until it seems as though fire has burned it, or it rots after the blackness: until that putrefaction is cut down to what follows that limb, and crawls into the whole body.
Therefore hasten with the incision of it, and similarly if the cause of the corruption is from the sting of certain venomous creatures: such as a sea scorpion, and viper, and asp, and the like.
If therefore the corruption, or sting is in the extremity of a finger, then cut into the finger, and do not allow space for the corruption to advance as far as the rest of the hand.
And similarly if it happens in the hand, then cut into it from the root of the forearm, and do not allow space for the corruption until it reaches the forearm.
And if it invades that, then cut into the arm at the elbow joint.
But if the corruption passes through, and you see it around the elbow: then death is upon the sick person. And besides that from treatment, the quantity of strength is to be administered.
And do similarly with the foot, when corruption invades a toe, then cut it at one of the joints: and if it seizes the arch of the foot, then cut into the whole foot. But if it ascends to the knee, then cut the leg at the knee joint.
And if the corruption has already reached above the knee, then there is no wisdom in it except dismissal, and to leave the sick person to death.
The manner of cutting a limb, or sewing it up, is to tie a ligature below the part you wish to cut and tie another ligature above the place. Let one assistant extend one ligature downwards, and let another assistant extend another ligature upwards. And you separate the flesh which is between the two ligatures with a broad Spatula, until all the flesh is exposed. Then it may be cut, or sewn up.
And you must place linen cloths on all sides, and let the saw not touch the healthy part; and it should happen that a weak additional injury is inflicted, and a warm poultice applied.
But if internal bleeding should happen while you are operating, then cauterize the place quickly: or put some powders that staunch blood upon it. Then return to your treatment until it is completed.
Then bind the wounded limb with a suitable bandage for it, and care for it until it heals.
And I tell you that it happened to a man in his foot, the same accident that I am telling you about.
Which is because a blackness occurred in his foot, with a burn that resembles fire's combustion; and that corruption first happened in his toe, until it began to affect the whole foot.
Therefore the man hastened from himself, after he saw the corruption cutting into his limb with its violence, which he found out from the pain and the burning of it; and he cut it at the joint, and was healed.
And after a long time had passed for him, that same corruption happened to him in the index finger of his hand.
He therefore came to me, and I tried to recover that excess with what I put on his hand from medicines after the cleansing of his body; and the excess was not repelled, and it began to advance into another finger: until the corruption began in the hand.
He therefore called upon me to cut his hand; and I refused to do so, hoping that excess superfluity might pass from him on its own: and I also feared for him, lest he should die at the incision of his hand. For the man's strength was beyond the case.
When therefore he despaired of me, he returned to his own land; and it was reported to me concerning him that, hastening, he fell upon his whole hand, and he was healed.
And I have not related this narrative except that it may be a help concerning what falls upon the nature of this illness; and that it may be a sign by which it is signified, and according to which it is acted.
On the Treatment of Sinuses, and the Quality of Clysters with Medicines.
When an abscess occurs in certain fleshy members and the space of the abscess extends until it collects pus; then it bursts or is perforated, and what is in it issues forth as pus, and a vacant place remains as if it were a vessel: and the skin that is over it is like a cloth that has already been attenuated: and the corruption does not proceed to its final stage, nor does it press into the mouth, nor into a nerve, nor into a ligament; it deserves to be called a Sinus and not a Fistula. However, if the space of it extends until the corruption presses into any one of these members, then it is called a Fistula or a zuken.
And its treatment is to cut all that skin as it revolves; and especially when it has become attenuated and made like a cloth; and you trust because it does not adhere to the place on account of its corruption.
But if you hope that the skin will adhere and the last corruption does not follow from it; and if there is thickness in the skin from flesh, then treat it with a clyster; and it is, that you consider the Sinus.
Then if it is large, and the pus that is emitted from it is of a foul odor: then cleanse it with Egyptian medicine, which is vinegar, and oil, and ziniar, taking equal parts, mix them in a vessel; and decoct them over fire until the medicine reddens; and comes to the thickness of honey.
Then take from it as much as you need; and dissolve it with water and honey, and cleanse the Sinus with it; and constrict its opening, and let the medicine remain in it for the space of two hours.
Then extract it with pressure; do this for as many days as necessary until the Sinus is cleansed and the foul odor is removed.
And sometimes cleanse with ash water, when you do not have this medicine present; and it is such that you should take ash from vine wood, or ash from holly wood; and pour water over it. Then strain it, and cleanse with it until you are confident that the Sinus is already cleansed.
And if the weak person cannot tolerate Egyptian medicine nor ash water, then cleanse it with water and honey, to which some crushed ziniar has already been mixed. Or cleanse it with honey and wine mixed together, since honey has the property of cleansing and washing; and wine makes the Sinus adhere, especially when there is an excess of astringency and dryness in the wine.
When you have done this several times, and are confident that the Sinus corruption has already been removed; then cleanse it with something that promotes flesh growth in it.
And it is such that you should take some dyafinicon plaster; and dissolve it with rose oil and astringent wine; or cleanse it with certain medicines, and other ointments, the properties of which I have written in the treatise on ointments.
And if the opening of the Sinus is narrow, in which the enema cannula does not fit: then widen it a little with iron; or place in it a licorice root wrapped in Egyptian ointment, or basil ointment until it widens.
And similarly if the orifice is also wide, then bring together its lips with suture: and let out from it a quantity into which an enema may enter without addition.
And similarly if the orifice from which pus flows is raised upwards: then dry it up in a lower place which is in it so that pus may flow from it downwards: because pus when constricted in the depth of the sinus prevents flesh from being generated in it.
And if it is not possible for you to dry up the sinus approximately below it according to your wish: then try, so that you prepare the limb with preparation so that pus may flow from it easily according to how it is prepared for you. Try so that nothing of the pus is retained in it at all: and take consolidating medicines which are put upon a sign, suitable to what you wish. And extend them upon linen cloth. And let the cloth be of such quantity that communication of the whole sinus is made. Then cut with forceps a hole straight in the orifice of the sinus, a little wider than the orifice of the sinus. Then extend from soft ointments upon another cloth according to the quantity of the hole: and put it upon that one in a position distant from the orifice of the sinus so that the flow of pus from it may be relieved: and do not remove the large cloth except after several days at once.
But the one which is upon the orifice of the sinus is the one which you remove every hour so that what collects in it from pus may exit: and so that you may know whether what flows from it is much or little: or digested or undigested.
And with this again consider the place of the sinus itself whether the master thereof feels pain in it or there is no pain in it: and whether there is an abscess in it or not.
When therefore your medication proceeds thus, and you see the matter which flows out upon the orifice of the sinus: then you will know that the sinus is already adhering or approaching adhesion: then therefore dissolve it in the morning or on the third day: and when you dissolve the wound then change another cloth and renew the ointment.
But if after a long time a subtle virus comes out of the sinus, then do not despair of its adherence, for its healing is approaching, since there are some people who delay the generation of flesh in their wounds, and there are others who act contrary to that.
But if after many days purulent matter undigested comes out of the sinus, then you will know that the sinus does not adhere.
Therefore if the adherence of the sinus is delayed and its matter prolonged: then add to its drying medicines, so that they may be humid in their substance and dry in their power, such as diaphicinum ointment, when an excess of addition from zegi is added to it.
And of the last things by which it is cured, is to take diaphicinum ointment and dissolve it with rose oil, and pour over it old wine equal in its substance.
Then mix it with that and use it.
And honey, when you boil it until it thickens, and use it, now does a similar action to this, when diaphicinum ointment is not present to you. Or take myrrh and aloe and frankincense: grind the whole thing and sprinkle it over honey while it is over the fire. Then put a cloth over it and bind it over the sinus: and smear with the honey that you have boiled until it has thickened: and sprinkle spices over it, and put a cloth over it and bind it.
And sometimes long aristolochia is administered in it, and the roots of the lily and there is a lily: and herb flour, and centaury. Use them simply or combined, grind them, and sift them through a sieve: and sprinkle over the honey that I have described, and use it.
But if the matter of the sinus is prolonged and does not heal by this treatment, then know that there is corruption in its depths: or it is already making an impression on the bones, or on the remaining hard members, or ligaments: then cure it with the treatment for fistulas, according to what has been previously stated.
On the treatment of whitlow and a crushed nail, and cutting into an added finger, and sectioning.
Whitlow is an abscess that arises beneath the nail of the thumb of the hand or foot; and perhaps it arises in the remaining fingers.
Therefore when the matter thereof prolongs, and its care is neglected, and it becomes inflamed with a hot abscess; or it corrupts, and emits pus, until it corrodes the root of the nail; and perhaps it corrupts it altogether, and perhaps the corruption reaches the bone, until it has a foul odor; and the extremity of the finger becomes wide, and its color becomes dark.
When therefore you treat it, as we said in the division, and your treatment does not succeed; then it is necessary that you cut with an iron the whole superfluity which remained from the nail. Then cauterize the wound after that. For the cautery is very helpful.
But if the bone is sound, and the nail is also sound, and the empty additions of the nail already compress the flesh inwards, and begin to prick it, and injure it: then it is necessary that you place a thin Radium under an angle of the nail which pricks the flesh, and push it upwards; and cut into the flesh with ease, and place upon what remains of the flesh from burning medicines, corrosive ones, until all of it is removed. Then treat it with ointments until it heals.
But if the corruption already presses into the bone, then it is necessary that you cut into the bone, and extract it; for it never heals while a corrupted bone remains in it.
And if you see the erosion and corruption advancing into the finger, then cut it at one node, according to what has been previously mentioned about it. Then treat it until it heals.
If, however, attrition or percussion should befall a nail, and there should be intense pain in it, then it is necessary first to let blood from a weak person. Then dry the nail with a sharp spatulum and make a transverse cut upwards.
And be careful that the cut does not reach the flesh which is under the nail, for you will cause intense pain through it to a weak person, and it will be the cause of added growth of flesh in that place.
Then treat the place with something that relieves pain.
An added finger that is born in some men's hands, perhaps it is all fleshy; and perhaps there are bones in some of them; and perhaps there is a nail on it; and some of them are born at the root of a joint, or its birth is in some knots of the finger. Therefore, the one that is born in some knots of the finger does not move; and the one that is born at a joint of the finger perhaps moves.
Therefore, the section of the fleshy one of these is easy. Which is to say, you should cut it off at its root with a broad spatulum.
But the treatment of the one whose birth is at the root of a joint is difficult. Therefore abstain from cutting it.
But in the one that is born in a finger at the last knot, then you must first cut its flesh with a round incision down to the bone. Then saw the bone with one of those saws suitable for that purpose. Then treat it until it heals.
Consolidation that happens between the fingers often happens either from the birth of a man, that is, he is born with it, or from consolidation of a wound, or from burning by fire and similar things.
Therefore, it is necessary to separate that consolidation until the fingers return to their natural form. Then place cloths between them, or cloths soaked in rose oil, so that they do not join quickly and separate them; or put a thin sheet of lead between them until the place is consolidated as needed.
And similarly if a consolidation happens to some fingers with the palm, then cut that consolidation according to what is prepared and suits the shape of the limb.
Concerning the Incision of a Blemish.
Varicose veins are large, full veins affected by excess melancholic humors which occur in many parts of the body; and their occurrence is most common in the legs, especially in the legs of travelers, workers, and carriers.
Therefore, it is necessary that you administer a strong flogging with a black whip several times.
Then phlebotomize the patient from those veins.
The cure of them with iron is according to two methods. One of them is that they are cut and black blood is extracted. And the other method is that the vein is raised and entirely removed.
The section of it is according to this method. First embrocate the leg with plenty of hot water until the thick feculent blood is resolved. Then bind the infirm's leg from the upper part of their hip to the lower part of the knee with a bandage. Then cut the vein in one place, or two, or three, with a wide incision.
Then raise it, that is, draw the blood into your hand from the lower part of the leg up to the top, and the phlebotomist should first phlebotomize the infirm person at the top. Then cut the vein further down until a quantity of blood comes out which you see is sufficient and which the strength of the infirm person can tolerate.
Then bind it; and order him to abstain from foods that produce black bile; and repeat evacuation and bloodletting when the veins are full, and this is harmful to the weak one.
Its elevation however is in this way. Shave the leg of the sick person, if there are many hairs on it. Then introduce it into a bath; or embrocate it on his leg with hot water, until it reddens and the veins swell; or exercise it with strenuous exercise, if a bath is not present for him, until the limb heats up.
Then dry the skin directly at the vein section lengthwise; or at its last part near the knee, or in its lower part near the heel.
Then open the skin with hooks, and expose the vein from all sides, until it appears to the senses; and at its appearance, you see it reddish towards a dark decline. When therefore it comes out of the skin, you see it white as if it were a chord.
Then insert a trocar beneath it until it is raised and comes out of the skin; suspend it with a blunt hook, gentle one.
Then make another incision near that incision with three fingers. Then expose the skin from the upper part of the vein, until it appears. Then raise it with trocars as you did, and suspend it with another hook, as you did at first.
Then make another incision, or several more incisions, if it seems necessary to you. Then raise it and cut it with the last incision near the heel.
Then extract it to the incision that is above it.
And do this until you extract it from the incision that is above, or higher incisions altogether, until it comes out completely; then cut it.
But if it does not obey you to be drawn out and raised: then introduce a needle with a strong double thread, and tie it, and draw it out; or introduce a radium under it, and turn your hand in every direction until it comes out. And beware that it does not fall in. For if it falls in, it becomes difficult for you to raise it greatly, and harm comes upon the weak one from it.
When therefore you have drawn it all out, then place wool soaked in wine and rose oil, or oil over the place of the wound, and care for it until it is healed.
But if the thorn is contracted, having a twist with an involution to the parts, and is not as we said according to straightness: then it is necessary that you split it above it on every part of its twist, and the places of its involution.
Then suspend it with hooks until you raise it all; and beware at your section above it that you do not fall upon a vein, or wound it; for it will become difficult for you to raise, or draw it out. Therefore preserve your prudence.
The Form of a Falling Thorn by Which It Is Healed.
The form of a blind thorn.
It does not have a curvature like the other thorns, nor is it of a sharp point, so that it does not wound a vein; and it is of a thick, gentle flexuousness; for if it were slender, it would fall upon a vein by its slenderness: but rather let it be excessively thick as we said.
On the Extraction of a Vein of the Leg.
This vein is generated in the legs in hot lands, such as Al-Andalus and the land of the Arabs, and in dry, hot lands of little tenderness; that is, those in which grass, nor trees are born.
And perhaps it arises in other places of the body besides the legs.
And its generation indeed is from putrefaction which occurs under the skin, just as snakes and ascaris worms occur within bodies, and worms generated between the skin and flesh.
And the sign of the beginning of this event of this Vein is, that a vehement inflammation occurs in the leg. Then the place blisters. Then the Vein begins to come out from the place of that blistering, as if it were the root of a plant, or an animal.
Therefore when its extremity appears from it, it is necessary that you wrap upon it a piece of lead, whose weight is from one drachma to two drachmas, and bind it firmly: and let the lead hang suspended from the leg.
Whenever therefore anything comes out from it to the outside, wrap that in lead and bind it. And if it is prolonged much, then cut a part of it, and wrap the remainder. And do not cut it entirely before it all comes out. For if you cut it, it contracts and enters into the flesh: and it causes an abscess to occur, and putrefaction in the place, and a bad ulcer.
Therefore it is necessary that it be handled gently and be drawn out gradually until it comes out from its last part, and nothing remains from it in the body: and from this indeed Vein there comes out in some men what is in its length five palms and ten. And it has already come to me that it came out of a man twenty palms long.
But if it happens to you in the hour of your medication: then insert a ray into its foramen, and pierce it with a long foramen, along with its body, until all that is in it from matter is evacuated: and administer putrefaction to the place with medicines for several days. Then take care of it with the treatment of abscesses.
And indeed this vein sometimes has many branches, especially when it appears at the junction of the foot, or in the foot itself; and it has many openings, and a branch goes out from every opening.
Therefore care for it as we said in the division, and with that which was stated before.
Concerning the cutting out of worms generated under the skin, and it is called bovine disease.
This disease is called Bovine Disease in a certain land among us; because it very often happens to oxen, and it is a small worm that is born between the skin and flesh.
And it wanders throughout the whole body, ascending and descending; and it is shown to the senses by its movement from limb to limb, until it bursts wherever it bursts in the skin and comes out.
And its generation is from the putrefaction of certain humors. Just as worms and snakes happen, that is, earthworms and roundworms in the belly.
And it is feared because of its harmfulness. Because when it wanders through the body and rises to the head and reaches a place, then perhaps it opens there and comes out and destroys the eye; and that happens very often.
When therefore you wish to cure it and extract it, then it is not done except when it wanders and appears to the senses.
Therefore it is necessary that you tie what is above it and what is below it with a strong ligature constriction. Then cut above it and extract it.
But if it burrows into the flesh and you do not find it: then place a cautery with fire upon the spot until it dies.
And the greater thing that is feared is its corruption, by which it corrupts the eye as we said.
If however you see that it has already reached the head near the eye, then bind a good ligature under it above the eyebrow. Then cut above it and extract it.
And it is necessary that the sick person use cleansing of his body with medicines dissolving foul and bad humors.
And let him beware of foods generating putrefaction.
Concerning incision for the ailment Nakir.
This ailment is called in our land Nakir, and it is a pain which occurs in certain members, then it is transformed from member to member.
And I myself have indeed already seen it, according to what I relate to you. I was summoned to an infirm woman in certain ulcers. She exposed her arm, and I saw a small swelling in the vein of the arm cord.
And after I remained for an hour, I saw one swelling advance with a burning sensation, just as a worm advances, ascending to her shoulder faster than it is possible: so that it is like living silver, when it runs from place to place.
Therefore the pain withdrew from that place and was fixed in the shoulder.
Then I sat for an hour and it ran through the rest of the body until it reached the other arm.
Then she narrated to me that it revolved throughout her whole body, according to what I saw.
Therefore I marveled at the swiftness of its transformation from member to member. And indeed before that, I had not seen this ailment with my own eyes in such a manner. However, I saw many who found pain to be transformed from member to member, and I did not see it with my own eyes as I saw it in the woman.
And I do not think that it is anything but because the woman was from those who are of dry bodies with exposed veins, therefore that swiftness appears to the senses.
And it is necessary that they not appear according to this method in those who are delicate and have moist bodies, hidden veins.
Therefore when you wish its cure and the patient feels that pain: then if it appears to you by eye as we said, then bind above it and below it quickly: and cut above it until that wind shall go out constricted: and cauterize the place.
But if you do not see it with your own eyes: then treat it with shaking of the body and with that which expels winds and drives them out with foul pills and pills of Serapina and similar ones.
Concerning the Extraction of Arrows.
Arrows are diversified according to their kinds and according to the places in which they fall from the body.
Their diversity is this: since some of them have large points and small ones: and concave ones and deaf ones. And some of them have three angles. And some of them have tongues. And some of them have stars.
Those which are according to the limbs in which they fall are according to two ways. For either the limbs are from the principal concave limbs such as the brain and heart and liver and lung and kidneys and intestines and bladder and those similar to them. Therefore when it falls into any of these limbs and the signs of death appear to you which I will tell you after then it is necessary that you avoid the extraction of that arrow from them for death follows him in whom it is in many dispositions. And when these evil signs do not appear to you and the arrow is not hidden in the depth of the limb then draw it out and care for the wound.
For the signs of the brain when an arrow falls into it and penetrates the mouth and exits through the nasal passage which is above the brain are: because vehement spasms and dimness of vision and vertigo and redness in the eyes and swelling and redness of the tongue and spasm and confusion of mind and vomiting with cholera. And perhaps blood will issue from the nostrils, or ears. And perhaps speech is cut off, and the voice is destroyed. And there issues from the place of the wound a white moisture like pus. And there comes forth from it something like the wateriness of flesh.
If therefore these signs appear to you, then abstain from treatment, and extract the arrow if it does not come forth.
The signs of an arrow when it falls into the heart are that it is near the left nipple; and he feels as if it were fixed in a hard thing, not in a hollow thing. And perhaps the arrow has a motion similar to the motion of the pulse; and black blood flows from the wound. And following that is coldness of the extremities, and cold sweat, and fainting. Know therefore that death descends without doubt.
And the sign of an arrow when it has wounded the lung is the exit of frothy blood from the wound; and the vessels which follow the neck become inflamed, and the color of the sick person changes. And he breathes out a great, deep sigh; and he seeks the attraction of cold air.
But if the arrow falls into the membrane which is in the chest, then it is near the small ribs; and there is a great panting with violent pain, with a sigh; and all the limbs of the shoulders are moved.
And if the arrow falls into the stomach, then perhaps something undigested from food will issue from the wound.
And if the arrow falls into the belly and is fixed there, something like dung will issue from the wound, or phlegm, or intestine which has already been broken. Therefore there is no hope in its treatment, nor in the extraction of the arrow.
But if the arrow falls into the bladder, and urine comes forth; and something proceeds from it to the outside, and the pain is stronger upon the sick person: then know that it is mortal.
The remaining limbs, such as the face, neck, throat, shoulder blade, armpit, vertebrae of the back, collarbone, hip, thigh, and similar limbs are saved according to multiple arrangements: when the arrow does not find an artery or nerve; and the arrow is not poisonous.
And I tell you what I have seen about these arrows so that you may understand through it concerning your treatment.
Which is this, because an arrow fell to a certain man in the tear duct of his eye, at the root of the nose. Therefore I extracted it from him on the other side, under the fat of his ear; and he was healed, and no harm befell his eye.
And I extracted another arrow from a Jew, which had fallen to him in the fat of his eye under the lower eyelid; and the arrow was already concealed, and I did not obtain from it except a small part of it with which it adheres to its wood; and it was a large arrow from the arrows of Turkish bows, with soft iron, in which there are no ears.
Therefore the Jew was healed, and no bad accident befell his eye.
And I extracted another arrow from the throat of a Christian.
And it was an Arabic arrow, and it is the one which has ears. I therefore made a cut above it between two folds; and it was already submerged in his throat. Therefore I proceeded delicately with it until I extracted it, and the Christian was healed.
And I extracted another arrow from a man which had fallen into his belly; and we thought that he would die from it.
After thirty days had passed him, and nothing changed in his condition, I made a cut above the arrow; and I devised something about it, and extracted it; and he was healed, and no bad accident befell him.
And I saw a man to whom an arrow had fallen on his back, and the wound had healed over it. Seven years afterward, the arrow emerged from the root of his hip.
And I saw a woman to whom an arrow had fallen on her abdomen, and the wound healed over it, and the arrow remained inside her: and nothing changed in her disposition, and the woman did not find harm from it in any of her natural operations.
And I saw another man to whom an arrow had fallen on his face: and the wound healed, and it remained, not finding much pain from it: and similar things to these many.
And I extracted an arrow from a man of the King's princes, which had fallen to him in the middle of his nose, and was now inclined toward his right side a little: and the arrow was entirely concealed.
I was therefore summoned for his treatment after the fall of the arrow up to three days. I found the wound of the arrow very tight.
I therefore inquired for it with a subtle probe, and did not feel it; and he himself indeed found a puncture and pain under his right ear. I therefore hoped that this puncture was from the extremity of the arrow.
I therefore plastered the place with a plaster in which there was attractive and digestive power, trusting within myself that the place would become inflamed and the sign of the arrow would appear to me, so that I might cut above it: and nothing happened in the place indicating that the arrow had reached the location.
I therefore persisted with the plaster upon him for many days: and nothing happened, and the wound meanwhile healed: and he remained weak about its extraction for a space of days, until he felt the arrow one day within his nose. And he related this to me.
And he related this to me. I therefore placed sharp, corrosive medicine upon the wound for many days, until it opened; and when I probed it, I felt the slender extremity of the arrow adhering to the wood.
Then I added to the opening of the wound through that sharp medicine until the extremity of the arrow appeared visibly to us; and it took me about four months with this.
Then after the wound was enlarged and it was possible for me to insert forceps into it and move it, and it did not yield to coming out: then I did not cease to refine and contrive upon it with various ingenious methods until on a certain day I began upon it with forceps skillfully made, according to how their form will come at the end of the chapter, until I extracted it. Then I restored the wound.
And physicians indeed judge that the cartilage of the nose is not restored. Therefore I restored it and the wound became consolidated and he was healed with perfect health: and nothing harmed him at all.
And I relate to you the quality of extracting certain arrows so that you may establish a rule and meaning upon that which I have not said.
Because the parts of this art and their distinctions are not comprehended with narration; nor does this book comprehend it, and a prudent craftsman compares a little with much; and with that which is present, with that which is absent; and he finds a new operation and a new instrument among the descents of these foreign matters of this art when they happen.
I say therefore that arrows do not come out from the limbs in which they are fixed except according to two ways. Either by attraction from the place from which it entered. Or contrary to the other part.
And that which comes out from where it entered is either an arrow proceeding into a fleshy place and it is attracted and comes out.
And if it does not yield to coming out at the hour in which it falls: then it is necessary that you let it alone for several days until the flesh which is around it putrefies and its attraction is relieved and its exit.
And likewise if it is fixed in bone, and does not yield to you for removal, then leave it also for several days: and repeat it with attraction and motion every day, until it comes out.
But if it does not yield to you for removal after several days, then it is necessary that you perforate in the circumference of the arrow in the bone itself from every part with a fine awl, until the arrow is enlarged. Then draw it out and extract it.
But if the arrow is already concealed in a place in the body and hidden from sensation: then inquire for it with your hand. If you feel it, then draw it out with certain instruments which are suitable for drawing it.
But if you cannot get hold of it because of the constriction of the wound and because of the length of the arrow deep inside: and there is neither bone nor nerve nor vein there: then probe over it until the wound is enlarged and it is possible to extract the arrow.
But if there are ears by which it is held, then free the flesh fixed in them from every part with all your skill: and apply yourself, if it is not possible to get hold of the flesh in the fracture of the ears and their twisting until it comes out.
And when you perform the extraction of an arrow, wherever it is, then use twisting of your hand with forceps on all sides, until you make it come out; and with subtle finesse, so that the arrow is not broken, and its attraction and extraction become difficult for you.
And if you are not able to get it out within one hour, then leave it for several days, until that which is around it becomes putrid. Then repeat it; for then the tension will be eased. But if the flow of blood opposes you, then administer what we said about care in its chapter; and guard your strength from cutting a vein, or nerve, or cord.
And administer ingenuity in every way possible to you for the evasion of the arrow; and let it be with ease, and tranquility, or slowness, and firmness, as I have told you.
And it is necessary that you make, when you draw out the arrow, it be weak according to the shape according to which it was at the time of the arrow's case itself. For it is more beneficial. But if that is not possible for you, then administer what is possible for you from shapes.
But an arrow which comes out from the opposite side of the other, or already proceeds something from it to the outside; or makes a hump at the end of the arrow by feeling from the upper part of the nearby skin and you see it prominent: then cut above it, and let the incision be according to the amount that your forceps can grasp in it.
Then draw it out; for it itself becomes easy for exit.
But if it is held fast in bone, then twist your hand according to the roundness, until the arrow presses into the bone and enlarges itself; then draw it out.
And if not: leave it for several days; then repeat it until it comes out.
If the wood of the arrow is in it, then drive it out with that.
But if the wood has fallen out and you wish to apply pressure: then insert a concave instrument into it so that it enters the cavity of its tail; then drive it out with it.
But if the arrow is concave, then drive it out with an instrument entering into it. For the arrow is relieved by that.
But if the arrow is poisoned, then it is necessary that you scoop out all the flesh in which the poison is, if it is possible for you to do so. Then treat it with what is suitable for that.
But if an arrow falls into the chest, or the belly, or the bladder, or the side: and it is near from what you touch with your hand, and it is possible for you to cut above it: then cut, and be cautious of cutting a vein, or a nerve, and extract it.
Then sew the wound, if you see it necessary, and treat it until it heals.
And this is the form of forceps with which arrows are extracted.
Let the extremities be like a bird's beak, which are made like iron: when they grasp onto an arrow, they do not release it.
And many kinds are made from them, large and small and medium, according to the size, slenderness, and width of the wound and its tightness.
The form of a concave pusher.
And the form of a deaf pusher.
This is so that the extremity of the deaf one is like a spoke, so that its entry into the concave arrow is eased and it can be driven out with it.
Concerning Phlebotomy of Veins.
Veins suitable for phlebotomy, of which the custom is to flow in the body, are thirty.
Of these, there are sixteen in the head: two pulsating veins, which are behind the ears, called two Herbae; and two arteries, which are apparent in the two temples; and two veins which are in the tear ducts of the two eyes, which are called Aspicientes; and a prepared vein in the middle of the forehead; and a vein which is at the end of the nose. And two Guidegi, which are in the neck. And two veins which are in the upper lip of the mouth. And two veins which are in the lower lip. And these are the veins which are called Algecherich. And two veins which are under the tongue.
Veins which are phlebotomized in the arms are five veins in each arm.
One of them is the Cephalic, and it is on the sylvan side; and the common people call it the vein of the head. And the Fusca. And it is the Middle vein, and it is composed of a branch of the Basilar and a branch of the Cephalic; and the common people call it the vein of the body. And the Basilar, and it is located on the domestic side; and it is also called the Titillcaris. And the common people call it the vein of the belly. And the Brachial cord, and it is located on top of the fossa; and it is that in which one phlebotomizes, and it is that which appears above the thumb with a clear appearance.
And the Saluatella; and it is a vein that is between the annular and the little finger; and it has two branches.
And in the leg and foot there are three veins. One of them is which is under the curvature of the thumb from the outer side. And the second is the Saphena and its place is at the heel from the inner side. And the vein Nesse: and its place is at the heel from the outer side.
And in the other leg there are three veins similar to those.
Concerning phlebotomy of two veins after the ears.
But phlebotomy of the two veins which are behind the ears is beneficial for old catarrhs and hemicrania and apoplexy and bad ulcers of the head.
And the quality of this phlebotomy is according to what I relate. And it is, that the head of the sick person be shaved and his occiput be rubbed in the place of the two veins with a rough cloth with good rubbing. Then let the sick person constrict his neck with a band until two veins appear. And their places are behind the ears in two hollow places of the head. Therefore seek them with your finger and where you feel their pulse under your finger mark there with a hot iron.
Then take a phlebotomy knife which is called the Incidens. Then insert it under the vein into the skin until the phlebotomy knife reaches the bone. Then lift your hand with the vein and skin upwards and cut into the vein and skin with an incidental incision. And let the length of the incision be the quantity of two joined fingers or about that and make as much blood come out as you wish.
Then constrict it with lint and let it go until it heals.
And now at the beginning of the book incision of them and their branding preceded.
The benefit of phlebotomy of two arteries which are in both temples is for an old headache; and for habitual dropsy; and for persistent ophthalmia; and for the course of acute, overflowing fluxes to the eyes.
And the quality of phlebotomy in these cases is as I tell you. Let the weak patient severely constrict his neck until two veins appear clearly to the senses; and let their pulse be shown beneath your finger. Then mark them with a cautery.
Then lift the skin above the vein upward with your index finger and insert the phlebotomist's instrument cutting beneath it, and elevate the vein upward. And cut into it as you have done with the other two veins; and let the blood flow out according to the amount of your necessity.
Then release the constriction of the weak patient and place your finger upon the vein for one hour. Afterwards place a compress and cotton wadding upon it and constrict it tightly from above; and release it until it heals.
And the preceding recollection of them, and their incision, and their extraction have already been mentioned at the beginning of the book.
The benefit of phlebotomy of the vein of the forehead after phlebotomy of the cephalic vein is for old facial ailments; and for headache; and redness; and foul ulcers.
And the quality of this phlebotomy is as I tell you. Let the weak patient constrict his neck with a towel until a vein appears. Then take the instrument which is called a Foissor.
Place the projecting point which is on the head of the Fossa over the very vein, and strike it with a comb, or with some other thing in like manner: and let the blood run according to the quantity which you wish. Then loosen the perforation of the sick person, and bind it until it heals.
And sometimes it is opened with a wide phlebotomy. But it is not necessary that it be of an acute extremity, as are the other phlebotomies, but rather of a wide extremity. And let the phlebotomy be made across it, because the mouth is nearby; and perchance it will be broken in it when it is slender.
The benefit of the two veins which are in both lacrimals of both eyes is for the eye from scab and redness; and swelling in the eye, and illnesses of the face.
And the quality of their phlebotomy is that the sick person should bind his own neck with a bandage. Then phlebotomize them; and you should be standing over his head.
And let the phlebotomy be made across it a little in length, with a small phlebotom, a little wide, for there is not much flesh in the place. For if the phlebotom is of a slender extremity, it may perchance be broken.
Then let as much blood run out as you need. And put a pledget over them, and bind them one night, and loosen them.
The phlebotomy of the veins of the nose is helpful for acute fever, and vehement soda, and illnesses of the face, such as hemicrania, and redness which occurs in the face; especially when it is old.
And the quality of its phlebotomy is such that the weak neck constricts. Then hold its nose with your left hand and take a slender, long phlebotome; and fix it in the middle of the hare itself, between that which divides the nose according to straightness. Because the vein does not appear to the sense there and because the blood begins to flow immediately.
And it is necessary that you keep your hand with the phlebotome for a little while and make the blood run according to your need. Then bind it with one ligature at night. For it restores quickly.
Indeed the benefit of two phlebotomies of a Guideg is for the constriction of asthma and the inception of leprosy and melancholic illnesses and impetigo and bad ulcers and corroding ones, that is, summer herpes.
And the quality of their phlebotomy is such that it constricts the weak one under both in his neck with a ligature; and the artisan stands above the head of the weak one and the weak one sits on a seat.
Then, with the phlebotomy, widen the vein to a length for a little while. Then extract from the blood a moderate quantity, or according to that which you see according to the necessity which is for it. Then do similarly with the other vein.
Afterwards loosen the ligature and constrict the two veins in the middle so that the weak one is not startled. And leave it until morning. For the wound heals.
Concerning the phlebotomy of the veins of an Algeberich. The benefit of the phlebotomy of the veins of an Algeberich is because it contributes to alcohol in the mouth and corruption of the gum; and bad ulcers; and fissures of the lips, and bad ulcers even those which are in the nose and around it.
And the nature of their phlebotomy is such that you should make the weak person sit before you and bind their neck with a band. Then turn back their lip and look at the two veins: one is from the right side of the lip and the second from the left: and the demonstration of both is by the blackness of both. And this is true because around them are small, black veins. Therefore, cut both with an incident incision.
But if there is doubt upon you and you do not know which of them it is, then attend to the incision of the larger ones and the more manifest.
And similarly do with the two veins that are in the upper lip; and indeed, custom most often runs with this; and cut the two veins which are in the lower lip.
Concerning phlebotomy of the Vein under the Tongue after Phlebotomy of the Cephalic Vein. Indeed, the benefit of phlebotomy of the two veins under the tongue is that phlebotomy of the Cephalic vein is for premonitions which are in the throat and pain of the uvula and pains of the mouth.
And the quality of their phlebotomy is such that the weak person should sit between your hands before the sun and lift up their tongue, and look under the tongue from one side for a vein and from their other side a vein; and the color of both inclining towards blackness.
Therefore phlebotomize both: and take care not to insert your hand in phlebotomizing them. For under them are arteries and perhaps it may happen that a flow of blood comes from those arteries.
The three veins which are phlebotomized in the elbow are those in whom phlebotomy runs with custom in all people.
And phlebotomy of them is done according to two modes. Either by puncturing with a wide myrtle phlebotome or an olive-shaped one inclined to subtlety; or by cutting with a knife phlebotome, and it is called Alnesil. And this is its form.
And of these there are wide and subtle kinds according to the width of the veins and their firmness. The myrtle phlebotome's form is wide as you see. And it is suitable for opening concave veins, full, proceeding, apparent, large, and those which contain thick, cloudy blood.
And this phlebotome is of lesser width and more subtle extremity.
And it suits the phlebotomy of subtle veins which contain thin, yellow blood.
But the Basiliac, which is one of these three veins, is a help for phlebotomy. Because it draws blood from illnesses which are under the throat and neck, from that which follows the chest and the belly; and it suits the phlebotomist with a phlebotome to approach it carefully and be fearful of it because under it is an artery. If therefore he errs and adds to the puncture of the phlebotomy, he will strike that artery, and there will be a flow of blood. Therefore it is necessary not to phlebotomize it with a puncturing phlebotome but rather to cut it with Alnesil.
But if the Basiliac does not appear by a manifest appearance, then it is necessary that someone else find it and change to another vein or seek some branch of it; or a phlebotome in place of it on the Brachium. For it itself is manifest, and cut it with the phlebotome Alnesil, as we said.
But if you wish to phlebotomize the same, then it is necessary before the constriction of the arm, to touch the place until you know the place of the pulse. Then mark the spot with a cautery, afterwards the arm, and cut the vein with a concave incision using an Alnesil lancet as we have said. And take care that the percussion does not fall through the space from the place of the artery.
And when you see, as you tighten the ligature, swelling in the place which you marked with a cautery, then that swelling is swelling of the artery; therefore avoid it.
And if you see the blood rise at the phlebotomy like the urine of a child and it is subtle, reddish blood; then you know that it is from arterial blood. Then hasten, and place your finger upon the space longways, then remove your finger. If therefore the blood is cut off (for it is often cut off) then tighten the arm and release it; and let the weak one beware of its movement; and let there be fear upon him.
And let him not move it for several days until it heals.
But if the blood is not cut off and overcomes you and you do not have medicine present to you at that moment, then cut into the artery if it appears to you. For its extremities are contracted and the blood is cut off.
Or take the bark of a pistachio nut and shred it; take one half and tighten it upon the place of the vein with a decent constriction, with a ligature and poultices up to another day.
Then if the blood is cut off, it is good. And if not: then treat it with that of which a previous remembrance proceeded from the place of powders cutting off the flow of blood; and the incision of its blood is not difficult in many constitutions because of the small place of the wound and the possibility of a ligature from the arm.
The veins of the temples are beneficial for phlebotomy, because they draw blood from the upper part of the head and the lower part of the body; therefore, because it is composed of a branch of the Basilary vein and a branch of the Cephalic vein, as we have said.
And it is fitting for the phlebotomist to make an incision on it above fear of cutting during phlebotomy, for beneath it is a nerve. Therefore if he adds to the puncture during phlebotomy and finds a nerve, there occurs in it numbness and healing becomes difficult and perhaps it does not heal completely.
And this nerve often appears to the senses. But if it is hidden in some people and is subtle and does not appear, then it is necessary that you place your phlebotomy so that the phlebotomist makes a section with Alnesil and avoids nerves with your strength.
If the vein is between two nerves then find the vein in length.
Concerning the Cephalic Vein. The Cephalic vein is beneficial for phlebotomy because it draws blood from the head and carries it to illnesses of the eyes.
And it is necessary in this vein especially if you wish, for the phlebotomist to pierce it with an olive phlebotome or a myrtle phlebotome; because it is the safest of all veins because there is neither an artery nor a nerve beneath it.
However it is fitting for you during phlebotomy to avoid the head of the lizard with the phlebotome and seek a gentler place; and it does not harm it if it is not found in the first percussion to go over it several times with the phlebotome.
However perhaps it may become inflamed in some people when it is not pierced in the first percussion but that inflammation does not harm it at all.
Concerning the Utility of Phlebotomy and Precautions to be Observed Therein. The quality and incidents of phlebotomy are derived from it; you must consider its correction.
Therefore, first of all, you must know that phlebotomy is not administered except for the preservation of health and its perseverance, and precaution against the advent of diseases.
And so that phlebotomy may be performed in one of the three veins which are in the elbow, namely, the Cephalic, and the Fusca, and the Basilica. And so that phlebotomy may be performed at the beginning of Spring on a fast, in summer and autumn; and winter after a meal, when the signs of repletion appear.
And let phlebotomy be performed on the first or third day after three hours have passed.
But phlebotomy which is administered in diseases does not have a determined hour; but when necessity and convenience call for it, by night or day, at any hour and time.
And children should not be subjected to phlebotomy until they reach fourteen years of age. Nor should a pregnant woman be subjected to phlebotomy except from the fourth to the seventh month. Nor should old men who have already passed sixty years be subjected to phlebotomy.
Therefore, when someone desires to undergo phlebotomy for whatever reason it may be, they must purify their bowels before phlebotomy with a mild enema if there is much retained feces in them so that the veins do not draw off foul and harmful superfluities from the intestines during phlebotomy.
Nor should a patient be subjected to phlebotomy when they are full, that is, experiencing nausea; nor intoxicated, nor recovering from drunkenness until that has been removed from them. Nor one weak with dropsy.
And beware of phlebotomy again after a colic attack, vomiting, diarrhea, increased intercourse, labor, exertion, wakefulness, fasting, and anything that dissolves virtue from the bodily or animal substance.
Then consider the subtleties of the humors before that, if the blood is thick with foods and drinks, and medicines: if it is possible to thin it.
Then he should enter a bath, if nothing forbids it: or he should exercise with some exertion, so that the blood becomes thinner.
And place his phlebotomy on his chest, around the third hour of the day, as we said: and try, so that his chest is evacuated that day from all animal evils: such as anxiety, and anger, and fear: and from all bodily evils, such as labor, and pain, and excessive intercourse, and those similar.
And let the things with which he is accustomed to use be present in his seat: from sweetness and smell and joy and those similar as much as possible.
Then let the phlebotomist sit on a cushion which is higher than the cushion of the one being bled: then let him draw out his arm and rub it with his hand twice or three times. Then let him tie a ligature with strips of linen and wrap it twice.
And let the constriction be moderate, because when the constriction is unequal or by excess in the constriction, then it prevents the flow of blood: or in relaxation, then it also prevents the flow of blood.
Then after the constriction it is necessary that the one being bled rub his hands together until the veins swell and make themselves known to the sense.
Then let the phlebotomist clean the one being bled with a little oil, preferably old.
Then let him place the index finger of his left hand on the very vein under the place where he wishes to perform the phlebotomy, a little: so that the vein does not flee and percussion occurs. Because from the veins there are some like a fleeing chord at phlebotomy: and among them are those which are full of windiness: and when you place the lancet on it, it contracts under the lancet and deceives the phlebotomist, and does not open the vein: and if it opens it, it is constricted: therefore for this reason it is necessary that the phlebotomist be steady and attentive to all these things.
Then let him lay down the phlebotomist. Therefore if a vein is opened from one side, then it is good. And if not, then repeat that on another side a little below or above it with speed: if the place does not become inflamed.
But if it becomes inflamed or weak and swells, then let him go for a day or two days: and let him not tighten the ligature. For perhaps it will draw in a hot inflammation. And let him not enter a bath.
Then let him repeat the bloodletting if he wishes.
But if the opening is small or the blood flows subtly: and you fear that the quantity of blood will not come out as you wish: then repeat the phlebotomy in the same hole according to straightness, and add a little in the operation.
And do that with speed before the place becomes inflamed, for an inflamed place is in many men at a small operation.
Therefore if you see it already inflamed, then do not repeat it on top of it at all, for it will excuse nothing to you. And put something from the dregs of oil on top of it: because it relieves the course of blood. And it is better in this place than the oil itself and than other oils.
And similarly administer the dregs of oil in all your bloodletting, by which phlebotomists prohibit the course of blood at veins: and they do this with tyriaca, aloes, and sagapenum when one of them is put on the place.
For blood becomes thinner and dissolves when it is thick.
But if a large inflammation should happen in the place of bloodletting, for it often happens, especially to him who is not a phlebotomist except from that side, or the opening of the vein is small, then hurry and put a sponge infused in water and salt, warmed a little, on the place: and bind it for an hour; because it will be resolved.
And it is necessary that you do this after the blood has exited from the vein itself, with its complement, or from another vein.
If anything of blackness or greenness remains in the place after a few days, it does not harm; and if you wish, put something dissolved of aloe or myrrh upon it; or something from the juice of mint, that is, calamint or something similar to it.
And very often there occur abscesses and swellings at the phlebotomy of the basilica.
Place your hand upon it, then if you find softness under your compression upon it, then that prominence is bad. Therefore be careful to place something from what we have said upon it. For perhaps arterial blood may flow from it.
But make a plaster for it with that in which there is astringency so that the place hardens. Then care for it with the rest of the treatment until it heals.
And it is necessary that you draw from every man blood according to the amount of his strength. And what appears from the prevailing color over the blood.
For if the blood is black, then let it flow out until it reddens; and similarly if you see it thick, then let it flow out until it becomes refined; and similarly if it is sharp, until its sharpness is removed.
And it is necessary for him who is full, strong and needs bloodletting with one ounce, that the phlebotomy of the vein be enlarged, and the phlebotomy be wide; and in him who is weak, let it be the opposite of that.
And it is necessary that you draw it out in stages, and that the opening be narrow; and what is best administered in the phlebotomy of a vein is that there be a transverse section, not a puncture.
And this method of phlebotomy is safe from the flow of blood and from the incision of a nerve; and it is more praiseworthy and safer than phlebotomy by width and length.
And for whom fainting is customary during bloodletting, it is necessary that he eats before the bloodletting a small amount of bread soaked in pomegranate juice; or seneca root, if it is warmed; and you should extract from him three or four times an amount of blood.
And if he has a cold complexion, then let him take before bloodletting bread soaked in syrup of pomegranate juice, or in syrup of honey spiced with aromatic herbs, or in fragrantly spiced syrup.
And if fainting should occur during bloodletting, and the cause of it is the excessive loss of blood; then it is necessary that he takes in a drink meat broth and refined fragrant syrup; and uses aromatics with agarwood, and lines his chest with them; and uses the other things we mentioned in the division on fainting from evacuation.
But whoever wishes to spare his arm, and to shed his blood on a second day; it is necessary for him whose bloodletting is for much evacuation, and whose strength is weak, that he sheds blood gradually by the amount of his strength on consecutive days.
But he who wishes to spare his arm and shed his blood on a second day, and whose body is strong; then let him do it after seven or nine hours from his first bloodletting.
But whoever wishes to draw blood from his body to the opposite side from which it inclines; it is necessary that it be done to him on the second or third day.
In whom, however, the blood is already much and hot, and sharpens, and causes fever to occur; it is necessary that a large amount of blood goes out from him at once, and that a great quantity be extracted from him; and the opening should be widened until fainting occurs: after you have observed all the conditions of bloodletting: and so that you place your hand upon his pulse near the course of the blood, lest death occur in place of fainting. Very often it happens that this occurs when the phlebotomist is ignorant and negligence occurs.
And it is not right when you wish to loosen the arm and loosen blood secondarily, when the opening of the vein has already been closed and the exit of blood becomes difficult, to press down on it forcefully, and to bend quickly. For that is very bad, rather let it alone until it is bled a second time.
Or remove with the sharpness of the phlebotomy that which has become clotted from blood in the opening of the vein and place something dissolved salt on it. And put some tyriacal aloes or sage on it and put it under gentle compression until the blood comes out.
But if the vein has already become inflamed, then let it be and do not press on it until the inflammation subsides.
But if necessity compels to loosen the blood a second time and there is no excuse then either let the phlebotomist perform it above that place or in the other arm in another vein.
The funis of the arm is phlebotomized in the place of Fuscae and Basilicae when they are not found and are hidden because it is composed from them.
And the quality of its phlebotomy is such that the weak person introduces their hand into warm water until the pulse reddens and the vein appears manifestly.
Then it is bound on it with a ligament with little constriction in the middle. Then the phlebotomy is made across it gradually, not laterally nor according to length; and let the opening be wide, and let your phlebotomy be such that you make it on the juncture of the hand a little.
But if the exit of blood is prohibited then immerse your hand in warm water and let the blood flow into the water until you achieve what you need.
But if you are in the summer days, then you excuse yourself from repeating the hand in warm water. And indeed most of all the course of blood is put in warm water in the time of winter.
And the phlebotomy of this vein is safer than all veins, because there is not a pulsating vein under it, nor a nerve.
But phlebotomy of the Saluatella vein of the right hand brings illnesses of the liver.
And the quality of its phlebotomy is, that you bind the back of the hand with a ligature, or your hand, after you have immersed it in warm water, until the vein is inflated and made very apparent to the sense. Then phlebotomize it transversely. And if you entirely miss it, it will not harm her in any way.
And preserve, that you do not finish your hand with phlebotomy. For under it are the nerves of the fingers laid bare from flesh.
Then repeat the hand to warm water, and let the blood run into it. For if you do not repeat it in warm water, the blood coagulates in the orifice of the vein, and is prohibited from the course.
Therefore when you draw out a necessary quantity of blood, then place oil and salt upon the vein: so that it does not quickly consolidate.
And similarly it is necessary, that you do with every strict branch.
But the advantage of its phlebotomy from the left hand is: because it brings illnesses of the spleen: and similarly do in its phlebotomy, just as you did in the second equally.
But the advantage of the Saphenae phlebotomy is for illnesses which are in the lower part of the body, such as illnesses of the wombs, and retention of menses, and infirmities of the kidneys, and ancient ulcers of the hips and legs, and similar from illnesses.
And the quality of his bloodletting is that the sick man should introduce his foot into warm water and make friction upon it until the veins protrude; and he should tie above the junction of the foot with a ligature. And indeed the place of the veins is near the heel, apparent towards the thumb; and many branches ramify from it on the surface of the foot.
Therefore let the phlebotomist make the incision in a larger branch of it, or at the heel in its aggregation; for this is better and safer.
But if the phlebotomist makes the incision on the surface of the foot, then let him beware of the nerves which are beneath it upon the surface of the foot.
And let your phlebotomy be such that the phlebotomist makes it transversely, as if he wished to cut it; and let the phlebotomy be Nesil.
But if the flow of blood is prohibited, then immerse his foot again in warm water and let the blood run into it until it is evacuated.
But if the phlebotomist misses the vein at the first attempt, then he should repeat the phlebotomy a little higher up, for this place is safe and you need not fear any harm from it, when you are careful of the nerves, as we have said.
