And similarly with the Saphena vein of the other foot equally.
But the place of the Sciatic vein, as we have said, is near the heel on the outer side.
And the benefit of its bloodletting is for pain in the loins when it is due to hot blood.
And the quality of its bloodletting is that the sick man should enter a bath and hurry; and he should constrict his leg from the side of the loins up above the heel with four fingers with a thin, long strip; for it does not appear except because of this.
When it therefore appears, then let the phlebotomist make the incision in whatever way is possible for him, or according to a transverse direction; and it is better that either he should cut into it with an incision or divide it with a section. For its location is safer, and it itself is very hidden in many people.
For its location is safer, and it itself is very hidden in many people. But if you do not find it, and it does not appear to the senses at all: then let phlebotomy take some branch of it, and that is the one which appears on the back of the foot, and towards the auricular: and be cautious of the nerves, and make it run out with as much blood as you wish.
Then loosen the stricture, and put skin over the place of phlebotomy, and bind the place. For it heals very quickly.
Concerning cupping, and the quality of its use.
Cups are made from horns, and from metal, and from glass.
And cupping indeed is done according to two methods. With scarification, and extraction of blood: and the other is cupping without scarification.
And this cupping which is without scarification is according to two methods. For it is either with fire. Or it is without fire.
And there are fourteen places on the body where cups which are administered with scarification and extraction of blood are applied. Of which one is cupping Alnocrati, and it is the back of the head: and Alkehel, and it is the middle of the neck: and cupping Alhagdahim, and they are two laminae of the neck from both sides at the same time: and cupping menti, and it is under the jawbone in the lower part of the mouth: and cupping of two spatulas: and cupping Alachosos on the top end of the coccyx: and cupping of two focilium, and they are the middle of the two forearms: and cupping of two legs: and cupping of two caulae.
And cupping indeed does not extract blood from you from the tiny veins scattered in the flesh, and therefore virtue does not fall away from them, as is the case with it from phlebotomy. Nor should ventilation be administered with or without fire in any illnesses which arise from repletion until the whole body is evacuated.
But if necessity compels to ventilation from illness or on account of custom we administer it at any hour in the beginning of the month, at the end of it and in the middle of it, and at whatever time it may be.
This is because there are men who when blood multiplies in them until they need its extraction through ventilation find heaviness and a headache in their head.
And among these men are those who find repletion and redness in their face, head and neck. And among these men are those who find itching in their face and forehead and darkness and dullness in their eyes.
And among these men are those who scratch the place of their ventilation. And among these men are those whose laughter multiplies. And among these men are those who find the taste of blood in their mouth or whose tongue swells and sheds blood. And among these men are those whose sleep multiplies. And among these men are those who see in their dream blood and redness and the slain and wounds and things that are similar to these.
Therefore when we see anything of these things, and especially if it is in the middle third of the month, we then order ventilation to be done after two or three hours have passed from the day.
The benefit of ventilation of the nape of the neck is because it brings relief to heaviness in the head and what flows down to the eyes. But it is necessary that it be done after evacuation of the whole body.
And this indeed, ventilation is in place of cephalic phlebotomy.
And it is not permitted for one of cold complexion or who has catarrh to use it. For it brings great harm to him.
And therefore old men should not use them, and those whose heads have cold ailments.
But whoever becomes accustomed to it, forgetfulness is generated upon him.
And therefore you must instruct the one who uses the cupping glass to put down his hand with the cupping glass for a short time towards the lower parts, fearing the generation of forgetfulness.
Cupping or Alkehel is in the place of phlebotomy for dark-complexioned people and phlebotomy for pale people, and therefore it brings relief from asthma, constriction of breath, fissure of the instrument of breathing, cough, and repletion.
And the cupping glass Alkehel should be applied for a short time, because if it is placed lower, it generates weakness in the heart and stomach.
But cupping with Alhagdahim brings relief from pains happening in the head, ophthalmia, hemicrania, perforation, and pain in the roots of the teeth; and it is in the place of phlebotomy for pale people.
And you must instruct the cupper not to press his hand with the scarring; so that he does not strike an artery and there is a flow of blood.
Cupping under the chin brings relief from ulcers in the mouth and corruption of the gums and similar ailments which are in the mouth; and it stands in the place of phlebotomy for alierich which are in the two lips.
Cupping with spoons brings relief from Alchafacham, which comes from repletion and heat.
Cupping of the interior parts of two focilium brings relief to phlebotomy of the three veins, pale and dark-complexioned and cephalic; because it draws blood from all those small veins which are in the flesh; and those small veins draw from other veins larger than them until the attraction reaches the three veins.
And you must instruct the one who performs cupping not to proceed too much in scarifying; because the place is stripped of flesh and beneath it are nerves and arteries.
But the cup which is cupped on the bare skin brings hemorrhoids to the anus and lower ulcers.
And you must instruct the one who performs cupping that the cup be large and that it be of metal; because the place needs strong suction and perhaps slender cups will break; and let it be scarified with a large scarification.
Cups on the legs diminish manifestly; because they draw blood from the whole body and bring ancient pains to the kidneys, womb, and bladder: they provoke menses, and bring about relief from dropsy and asthma: and they stand in place of two phlebotomies of the saphenous veins and alhorcobeni.
However, they weaken the body greatly and cause fainting to occur in many people.
And cupping of the alhorcobeni is benefit near to the benefit of cupping two legs.
The quality of the position of the cups is, that you place the cup first empty and suck with temperate suction; and do not prolong the position of the cups but put them quickly and remove them quickly so that the humors may come to the place with equal arrival: and do not cease to iterate and continue that until you see the place already reddening and swelling and the redness of blood appears; then scarify, and repeat the suction gradually. Then consider in the arrangement of bodies.
For he who is of men with tender flesh and rare pores, it is necessary that you scarify him once, no more; so that the place is not ulcerated.
And instruct the one who performs cupping to enlarge the scarification and make it shallow; and to moderate the suction in ease, swiftness, and gentle motion.
If there is a thickening in the blood, then it must be bled twice. Indeed, the first opening is made by thinning the blood and its wateriness; and in the second, by a careful extraction of thick blood.
But if the blood is very foul, then let a third bleeding be done so that the last result may follow.
And finally, when we want to extract blood gradually or a little, one bleeding is enough for us. But if we want many extractions of blood, we make many bleedings.
And if we measure that the blood is thick, then it must be bled deeply; and the equal end in bleeding, is the depth of the skin.
What should be administered from oils at the placement of cupping and what should a cupper beware of?
Indeed, for one whose skin is thick, hard, dark and whose pores are tight, it must be anointed with opening, softening, resolving oils; if it is in the summer season, such as oil of alkane or oil of violets or oil from sweet almonds or oil from pumpkin seeds.
And if it is in the winter season, then narcissus oil, or lily oil, or chamomile oil, or syrup oil and those similar to them.
But if the excess is thick and cold then let the oil be bitter almond, or oil of balsamic, or white oil, or anise oil and those similar to them.
But if the cupper is for those with open pores and tender flesh, then oil must be prohibited, and they must wash the cupping places afterwards with rose water, or cold water, or sun water, or pumpkin water, or purslane water, and similar to them.
He whose blood is of much humidity should wash with vinegar, or myrtle water, and sumacs, and the like.
But he who has thick superfluities should wash his wounds with old wine, or with samsuck water, or a decoction of anise, or chamomile, and the like.
And it is necessary that washing be done in a bath, or in the track of a bath, indeed it is necessary that it be given after leaving the bath for an hour, or for two hours.
And no one should sleep after washing.
What is it by which the wind-remover, or phlebotomist should be regulated before washing?
And after that it is necessary for you to consider first, then if the wind-remover or phlebotomist is choleric and his blood has sharpness and inflammation, then he should take cold things, such as pomegranate juice; and also endive with vinegar and lettuce, and scandibin, and iuleb, and the like.
And make his foods chicks, and boiled eggs, and foods seasoned with sour things; and the like.
And he whose complexion is cold should take syrup of honey, or of almonds, or alsecaniabin made from seeds; and take fragrant wine which is between old and new; and be directed to use a small amount of food. And make his food chicks, and canaries; and sparrows, that is, small birds; and dove chicks.
And it is necessary that drink on the day of washing and phlebotomy be more than food; and it is necessary that in some people a tyriaca alfaroch, or medicine from musk, that is, musk, or alesselithe be given in drink before washing, and before phlebotomy, or after: so that the main limbs may be strengthened, and the blood thinned. And it is not necessary for them to take it in a heated drink.
On Winds Without Scarification. But those winds which are without scarification are winds which are placed upon the liver and spleen and podagra and upon the belly, navel, and places of the kidneys, and the thick ankle; because these members do not suffer scarification upon themselves.
And it is not sought through them except an attraction of blood from a member to a member; such as we place a cupping glass upon the nipples in the sickness of a discharge of blood from the nose. And we use them so as to loosen cold and invisible windiness in a member; such as we place a cupping glass upon the belly and navel. For it thins the member and warms it and removes the pain therefore because it has resolved that windiness.
It is also placed upon the kidneys when there occurs a stoppage or a stone in them. For by its power of attraction it perhaps opens the stoppage or removes the stone from its place.
And similarly it does when placed upon the liver and spleen in order to ward off windiness in both.
And these cupping glasses are administered empty with incision only. And sometimes they are administered with fire.
And sometimes they are administered full of warm water in illnesses of pleurisy; and this is so that the cupping glass should be filled and be large with hot water alone or with water in which herbs have been boiled which are suitable.
On the Form of a Cupping Glass Administered With Fire.
Let the width of its opening be two fingerbreadths open, according to what we have formed; and let its depth be half a palm; and let there be on its side in a place which is around its middle a small hole according to the quantity by which a needle may enter it. Make it from brass or bronze, thick rims, smooth, equal, polished: so that it does not injure the member at its position upon it; and let there be in the middle of it a crosswise rod from brass or iron where a candle with fire is placed.
And now this is made large and airy, greater than we have described, and slightly smaller than that, according to the illnesses and age of him who uses it.
For cupping glasses of children and thin people are different than cupping glasses of men and fat bodies.
The quality of placing this cupping glass with fire on a limb is to light a flax wick with fire from linen, or a small candle from beeswax; and place it over the middle of the transverse rod which is in the middle of the cupping glass: so that the fire is ignited around the lower part of the cupping glass, so that the body of the sick person is not burned.
Then place it on the limb; and a finger should be on the hole which I mentioned, until whenever we hold the cupping glass: while we need to, we remove the finger, and vapor will exit over that hole, and the cupping glass will be released immediately.
Then light the flax wick according to its manner, and repeat it if you need it.
But the cupping glass which is administered in the illness of pleurisy with water, does not have a transverse rod, nor a hole; and it is not administered unless it is filled with water, and placed on the limb only.
And this is its form.
And this cupping glass, the larger it is, so that it takes a lot of water, is better.
Concerning the Suspension of Bloodletting Instruments.
Bloodletting instruments are not administered in many, unless in those limbs in which the position of cupping glasses is not possible due to their small size, such as the lip, and gum, and those similar. Or in a limb stripped bare of flesh, such as a finger, and nose, and those similar.
And the quality of using them is: that you should come from bloodletting instruments which are in sweet waters, clean from decay.
Then they should be immersed day and night in fresh water until they are lean and there is nothing left in their bellies. Then the body should first be evacuated with bloodletting and with enemas. Then wash the limb of the sick person until it reddens. Then place them upon it.
When they are therefore full and fall off, and suction of the place is possible with an enema, then this is the best method for healing; and if not, then wash the place with vinegar. Then with plenty of water, rub it and squeeze it.
But if the flow of blood continues after the falling off of the bloodletters and it oozes, then infuse a cloth of flax into cold water and place it on top until the oozing is stopped.
But if the blood increases, then sprinkle powdered tragacanth or gallnuts and things similar to these astringents on top until the bleeding is stopped.
Or place bean halves that have been skinned on the spot and leave them until the beans adhere to the spot, for the blood will be stopped.
And it is necessary if a repetition of the bloodletting is needed, to change them when possible.
But if the bloodletters are prevented from sucking, then wash the place with fresh blood. Or insert a needle into the place until something comes out of the blood. Then place them, for they themselves when they sense something from the blood, will adhere immediately.
Therefore when you wish for them to fall off, sprinkle something ground from aloe, or salt, or ashes on top of them and they will fall off immediately.
This chapter is also very often necessary in the art of medicine, and it is the restoration of fractures, and dislocations of accidents in bones.
Know ye, O Sons, that foolish physicians and the common people boast of this chapter, who have never looked upon an ancient book, nor read a letter from it.
And for this reason it is that this method, from the sciences, is private in our land. And I never found anything good in it at all, and I did not obtain from it what I obtained except for the length of reading ancient books, and study upon their understanding, until I extracted the knowledge of it from them.
Then I adhered to experiment and the length of study in my life.
And now I have described to you from it in this chapter everything that contains my knowledge: and upon what passed experiment mine, after I abbreviated it for you, and freed it from the occupation of elongation, and abbreviated it without abbreviation, and showed it without declination: and I formed in it many forms from the forms of instruments which are administered in it, with being from the addition of declination, just as I did in the two preceding chapters.
Head II.
It is necessary that we say in the prologue of this chapter, summaries of speech and chapters, to which intellect the craftsman is compelled in the first place for station upon their truth: namely so that you, and those who are ready, may know or learn this noble art, and others from you.
I say, therefore, that when it happens to someone a fracture, or dislocation, or torsion, or a fall: then it is necessary that you hurry first to let blood from him, and loosen him, or both together: if nothing prevents it, such as weakness of power: or he is a child or a decrepit old man to whom it has happened: or it is a time of intense heat, or very intense cold.
Then let him be content with his food, cold herbs, and poultry and kid meats; and forbid wine, and fatty meats, and to be filled from foods and all food which fills the veins with blood. Until when you are safe from the hot abscess, and do not fear the effusion of matter to the limb: then let the sick man return to his first regimen with which his custom ran.
Therefore when a broken bone begins to be restored, it is necessary that the sick man eat foods nourishing with much nourishment, thick, heavy, in which there is viscosity: such as food made from cooked wheat with water, and rice, and heads, and feet, and entrails of cows, and eggs, and fresh fish: and thick wine, and things similar to these. For with this regimen coagulation of the fracture happens more quickly, and better.
And know that broken bones, when they are in those who are in the first old age and decrepit, it is not possible that they continue and are consolidated according to their nature always, because of the drying up of their limbs: and those which are consolidated and continued from them are at the end of their tenderness, such as the bones of young children.
Truly nature annexes upon a broken bone from every part something similar to glue, in which there is thickness, so that it adheres and binds itself, until they are joined together and are bound together, until it comes into the end of strength and firmness, as it was at first, until it does not prohibit anything from its operations: and for this reason it is necessary that you put nourishment of the sick man with foods in which there are heaviness, viscosity, and thickness, as we said.
And know that fractures are of different kinds, according to the diversity of limbs: for a fracture of the leg is different from a fracture of the bone of the head; and a fracture of the breastbone is different from a fracture of the back bone; and similarly all other limbs are different from each other: and I came with the recollection of every kind of fracture exposed in its own Chapter, distinguished from another. And the kinds of bone fractures are also diversified among themselves.
For it happens that its fracture is arched, besides which stars occur in it. And sometimes its fracture happens according to the length of the bone; and there are its fractures that are splintered, that is, stars, and additions proceeding, such as are in a reed pen, when it is fitted with a knife for writing. And not proceeding; and there is a fracture with a wound and a disruption in the head; and there is a small fissure fracture.
And to each species there is an ingenuity or instrument proper to its restoration, according to as its remembrance will come, distinguished in its places.
And from that by which a bone fracture is recognized, it is its crookedness and its prominence; and its appearance to the sense, and its sound when you compress it with your hand.
When therefore there is no apparent crookedness in the place, nor a sound at your touch when you touch the bone with agitation, nor does it find great pain: then there is no fracture there; but it is possible that there is crookedness or a small fissure. Therefore it is not necessary that you move it with your hand and compression altogether, but rather lay upon it from medicines of which its remembrance will come after that moment which suits the place; and then let a fine bandage be applied.
And know that when a place arches and splits into two, besides which splinters occur in it; nevertheless every part is removed from its companion: then it is necessary for you to begin from your hour to its rectification, equalizing it before an inflammatory swelling comes upon it.
But if an inflammatory swelling should come upon it, then let it alone for several days until the swelling subsides. Then equalize it according to whatever method is possible from gentleness and ingenuity.
And know that its restoration and equalization is easier than that of a bone in which splinters occur.
Then bind it according to how you will remember it.
But if there are splinters in the bone, then it is necessary to extend this fractured limb from two parts, if it is the hand or foot; or with your hand, if the limb is small; or with two cords, or with a cord and hand.
And let your position be such that you place the limb on an equal surface, according to its natural shape, until when the parts of the fractured limb are extended; then try to return those additions with all your ingenuity, and in whatever way you can into their places; and beware, according to your strength, lest pain or injury befall the weak one during your operation; and try, according to your strength, to apply one of the two bones with its companion, according to the better forms.
And it is necessary at that hour for you to try and touch it with your hand. If therefore you see something different there, correct it, and equalize it according to the amount of your strength.
And beware of vehement extension and strong compression, as many stupid people do. Very often they cause a hot abscess or contraction to occur in the limb through their operation, as we have already seen that often from their operations.
Then bind after the setting and decent operation and constriction of that limb, and quietude and peace; and warn the weak one about it. And make him beware not to move it in the hour of his vigils and sleep during his turning and stirring and at elimination, and all his movements with the last of his strength. And that he should beware so that the position of the limb is not one with which pain follows. Which is because when he feels pain or injury in the position of the limb, then it is necessary for you to change it to another position from which he does not feel pain.
And be careful with it, so that its position is equal and straight, so that torsion does not occur in the limb when it is restored.
And the quality of the constriction of the fractured limb is, according to what I describe.
Know that fractured limbs are differentiated by their number, and their size, and their form.
Those of them which are small, such as the arm, and the finger, and the calf, and those similar to them, then it is necessary that there be an envelopment of soft, fine cloth. Those which are large, such as the hip and leg, it is necessary that there be a broad, firm envelopment in them.
Because a broad bandage connects the large limb and constricts it from every part with equal pressure, so that no blemish enters it.
Then begin after your evacuation from equalizing to place a suitable unguent on it, in a soft pledget over the place of its fracture. Then begin to enwrap the bandage over the place of its fracture with three or four wrappings, according to what the limb deserves, and constrict your hand a little with the bandage.
Then go with it to the upper part from the place of the fracture and constrict less than the constriction which you made at the fractured place. Then extend yourself with the wrapping from the fractured place a little and soften the constriction gradually until you take a great thing from the sound place.
Then take another splint and wrap it again over the fractured place with wrappings. Then go with the wrapping to the lower part from the fracture. And let your operation be in the constriction of the wrapping and its softening, according to what we said in the first upper wrapping.
Then place between the wrappings some soft pledget or cloth with which the torsion of the fracture may be equalized if there is torsion in it; and if not, do not put anything in it.
Then wrap another bandage over it. Afterwards, immediately place even stars upon these wrappings, if there is neither swelling nor hot pus in the area.
But if there is swelling or pus there: then place upon it something which will draw out that pus and remove the swelling; and leave it for several days. Then bind stars upon it at that time.
And let the stars be made from strips of cane, wide, planed, and cleverly shaped; or they may be made from wood from which strainers are made, such as pine or boxwood, that is, palm branches, or calamus reeds; and let the width of each star be according to this form and shape.
However, it is necessary that the star which is placed upon the wound itself be somewhat thicker and wider than the other stars; and the length of the star should be according to what is fitting for the limb in size and smallness.
Then bind the stars with another bandage, according to the same first binding. Then bind over it with threads evenly, according to what we said about binding; and let your binding be tighter over the place of the fracture; and the further it is from the fracture, the looser the binding should be.
And it is necessary that the threads be of medium thickness and fineness, and are made from soft linen.
For if the threads are thick, as I have seen from the practice of stupid people who put their own threads made from twisted linen cords, it is a great error; because the binding falls apart with them beyond quality.
And again, fine threads are not suitable: because you do not achieve what you want through binding with them.
And it is not necessary that there be less than a finger's width between star and star.
And if a weak person is injured at the ends of the stars after binding in healthy places; then put under them some soft tow or carded wool until nothing is hurt by it.
When there is a fracture with a wound, and disruption occurs in the skin, I will come with remembrance of it in the second chapter singularity.
And you should know that not every fractured limb ought to be bound with splints from the first day. For this is because when a limb is large, it is not necessary to put splints on it unless after five days, or seven, or more, according to your safety from the event of hot inflammation.
And this is the method of plasters, with which the ancients restored, which are put on fractures and dislocations.
Method of a common shortened plaster, restoring a fracture, suitable for many complexions, especially women and children, because it is temperate, neither hot nor cold.
And it is as follows: you take from the flour mill powder, which is the medulla of grain that hangs on the walls of the mill at the movement of the millstone; and you prepare it as is, except that you sieve it with egg white: and put the confection of it neither thick nor thin. Then use it.
Method of another plaster, restoring a fracture, and dislocation, and torsion. Take almonds, and opium, and acacia, and enulacampane, and mugwort; and juice of all, each ten drachmas; myrrh and aloes, both five drachmas each; and alethil twenty drachmas, let them all be ground, sifted, and mixed with alethil water: or with egg white if the complexion of the sick person is hot. Then use this plaster. For it is of good composition, restores broken bones quickly, and benefits many people.
Method of another plaster which also benefits fractures and torsions. Take mugwort, and mess, and white alteae, all ten drachmas each; myrrh and aloes, both five drachmas each; acacia six drachmas; armenian bole twenty drachmas; let them all be ground with much pounding, sifted, and prepared with water or egg white; and let them be administered.
Method of plaster for joints, and separated bones from their places: and it soothes the pain happening to them, and the crack of bone, and the fracture. Take wool that has been squeezed dry, and submerge it in vinegar, and boiled oil: and place it upon the location.
And in this indeed plaster there is no power of restoration, but it is good in the soothing of a hot abscess: and it expels pains properly.
Method of plaster which restores a broken bone. Take leaves of the deaf fig, and leaves of the wild poppy: and grind all, and make a plaster from both.
Method of shortened plaster, administered at the restoration of a bone fracture: and when you wish the resolution of a remaining abscess. Take root of althea, and chamomile, and flowers of violets, and flour of herb, all of one part: grind all, and make them with wine, if the member is not heated.
But if it is heated, then make it with water of humid coriander, or with water, and use it.
Method of another stronger plaster in the resolution, than this first, when there occurs a hard abscess at the restoration of a limb. Take root of althea, and flaxseed, and balaustium, and melilot, and samsuck, and flower of violets, and chamomile, all of one part, grind all, and make them with willow water, or with sweet water, or with wine. All that according to the heat of the limb, and the rest of its heat.
However, Hippocrates did not say in his book, that one should place upon a fractured limb at its restoration, except a plaster made from wax, and oil: nothing else: and he related, that it should be a medium between thickness and thinness.
Galen thought that for the restoration of a broken limb things should be placed upon it in which there is dryness of heat, such as myrrh and aloe and frankincense, and things similar to them from those which we have mentioned.
Method of a Plaster Which Brings Together the Injury and Pain. Take mugwort and chickpeas and hairs of a man cut short or a bird's feather, and altea and salt in equal parts; grind them, and sift them, and make a plaster from it.
The quantity however according to which it ought to remain so that the ligature is then loosened is this, that you consider at that time whether there does not occur a weak pain nor itching nor is the fractured bone moved from its place; then do not loosen it for many days. But if a vehement itching occurs to it in its place, or an unsettling pain, or swelling; then hasten and loosen it within the hour, and remove the plaster from it; and take soft linen or a soft sea sponge, damp: and submerge it in lukewarm water and wash the place with it until the itching subsides and the pain ceases.
But if a vehement itching occurs to it in its place, or an unsettling pain, or swelling; then hasten and loosen it within the hour, and remove the plaster from it; and take soft linen or a soft sea sponge, damp: and submerge it in lukewarm water and wash the place with it until the itching subsides and the pain ceases. Then let the limb rest for one hour. Then place wool cut up soaked in vinegar and oil or rose oil upon it; and bind for one night until you are secure from a warm abscess and the swelling of the limb subsides and its pain is removed.
Then repeat it to a subtle tightening and a small plaster; and do not bind it tight with your first binding and a subtle one with it until it is healed.
But if you see that the abscess and redness and pain and swelling and all accidents have now been entirely removed and the plaster and binding are no longer needed; then repeat it as you did at first equally.
But if nothing of those things which we have said happens to the limb, then do not loosen it except after three days, or four, or five, or seven. And when that which is sent away for twenty days has completely adhered according to what appears to you from the resolution of the limb, as we have said, until when the fracture adheres and the coagulation of the flesh approaches it: add then also in the strapping more than your first whole strapping; and add also in the thickening of food for the weak, according to the preceding remembrance thereof.
But if you see the place of the fracture already drying out and wasting more than it should: then know that nourishment is prohibited from reaching it. Therefore embrocate it with warm water at every time you loosen it, and let it be so for three days, and relieve the strapping a little.
Through this operation nourishment runs to the limb and heals quickly.
That which foolish restorers do from a fractured limb with another vice, when it is not restored at first according to what is right, and it is restored according to crookedness, is an error from their operation, and great fear: and if it were right, the ancients would have said it in their books, and they would have operated with it.
And I have not seen any trace of them anywhere at all in that matter, and it is better that it should not be done.
Concerning the making of an accidental injury to the head.
The kinds of fractures are diverse and their figures are many: and the causes thereof are distinct.
For from a fracture is that which happens from a blow with a sword: and the blow takes away the whole bone until it reaches the siphac, which is under the bone, just as an axe does in wood: and on account of that this species of fracture is called Axial.
Or a sword strikes a certain bone and strikes its face and the incision does not penetrate to the very end thereof: and this species of fracture is called Kalaan, that is, Elevation entirely.
And it is a wound of two fractures, either large or small.
And from fracture is that which is a crushing, or grinding; and its cause is a blow with a stone, or a fall onto a stone, or things similar to these.
And this fracture is also either penetrating, which already approaches the panniculus, which is under the bone; or it is on the face of the bone; and the wound of these two fractures is also either small or large.
And from fracture is that which is hidden in the bone in the subtlety of a hair; and it is a small fissure, and on account of this this species of fracture is called Capillary.
And from these is a fracture which occurs from a fall, or a blow of a stone, and things similar to these, which cause the lamina of the bone to enter inwards, and a cavity is made in its place; just as it happens with caldrons of air, when a blow strikes them, and a part of them enters inwards.
And very often indeed this happens in the heads of soft bones, such as are the heads of children.
And all these species of fractures are separated by sutures, and I will come with a remembrance of the whole of it in its place.
And we recognize all these species of fracture by probing upon it, and inquiry into them with probes, and removal of corrupted flesh which are upon them.
The species Capillary, however, is recognized by this, that it is probed upon the bone, and cleaned away, and polished upon it with a cerate; for the fracture appears black.
But in the treatment of a fracture, consider first the accidents of the sick person; then if you see from the accidents that it signifies with manifest indication fear, such as vomiting cholera, and extension, and loss of intellect, and stoppage of voice, and syncope, and acute fever, and egress of eyes, and redness of them, and things similar to these accidents in many dispositions, without a doubt it will not be healed; and if you see accidents which are not terrifying to you, and you hope for salvation: then begin its treatment.
What it is, if he comes to you wounded at first when he is wounded: and that is in the winter days, then it is necessary that you strive in the removal of the bone before the fourteenth day according to all arrangement.
And if it is in the summer days, then it is necessary that you hurry to remove the bone before the seventh day, before that which is under the bone from the fatty tissue is corrupted, and those accidents which we have related occur.
And if the fracture of the bone has already reached the fatty tissue touching the brain, and it is with small particles, and attraction: then it is necessary that you cut into the crushed and rubbed part, according to what I tell you.
And it is necessary that you shave the head of the wounded man: and expose above the bone according to whatever way is possible for you: and according to the shape of the wound, and what is light upon the sick person.
And if it should happen to you at the exposure of the bone a flow of blood, or a hot abscess: then oppose it with that which is necessary. And it is necessary that you fill the place with cloths dipped in wine and rose oil: and make the wound rest, until the abscess settles, and you are safe from the flow of blood. Then begin in the healing of the bone, and its removal.
And that is done according to one of two methods.
The first method indeed is to cut into the bone with an incised strict blade: and this is its form.
Then apply another incisor after the incisor somewhat wider: and this is its form.
Then apply also another incisor even wider than the second.
And the second method is that you have at hand a number of diverse incisors: some of which are wider than others, and some of them shorter than others: and they are sharp at their last extremities, and are made of steel, or iron: and use ease in percussion above the incisor so that it does not shake the head and injure it.
If the bone is strong and hard, it is necessary to perforate it around its circumference before you apply the cutting instrument, with awls which are called non-penetrating awls; they are not so named unless because they do not pass through the thickness of the bony substance to that which is beyond it. For this reason the awl has a rounded end above that which is below its sharp point, similar to a margin, and a small circle prevents submergence and passage through the thickness of the bone.
And it is fitting for you to take a large number of these awls, each of which suits the thickness of the bone, until you have an awl present for every skull: whose sharp end in length and shortness should be according to the quantity of the thickness of that skull.
And this is the form of three kinds of awls, or trepans.
The quality of perforating around the broken bone is: to place the awl upon the bone and turn it with your fingers until you know that the bone has already been penetrated. Then change the awl to another place and put what is between every hole a quantity of the thickness of the radius, or approximately that.
Then cut with cutting instruments what is between every hole from the bone; and make it more subtle than you are able above that which is at its end, as we said, until you eradicate the bone, either with your hand or with some other instruments which you have prepared for this: such as tenaculum forceps and fine pincers.
And it is necessary that you be cautious with all care lest the awl or cutting instrument touch anything of the meninges.
When therefore you have gone around the bone perforating and it has been separated from the meninges if it was continuous with them and you have extracted it, it is necessary that you shave and smooth the extremity of it which remains in the bone with another instrument similar to a cutting instrument. However, it is necessary that it be thin and more delicate than other cutting instruments.
And if anything remains of the small bones and splinters, then take them with ease using what instruments you have. Then treat the wound afterwards with linens and ointments as we have said.
And another method of surgery is easy, altogether far from fear, which Galen said and praised it with great praise; and this is his word. It is necessary for you first to begin by exposing the part of the bone from the place where the fracture is more violent and apparent; until when you have exposed that part, and placed under it the extremity of this lenticular cutting instrument, which has this form.
And the lenticular part, being smooth, does not cut anything; and its sharp part on both sides thereof extending in length, as you see; so that the lenticular part be applied to the bone, with its face incident acutely on the bone.
Then strike upon the cutting instrument from one side with a small mallet until you cut through the whole bone easily, as it revolves; and you be secure from the pannicle, that what you fear does not happen to it altogether.
And if the physician were not more stupid than men, and ignorant of it, nor were he sleeping, then if anything remained attached to the bone from the pannicle in some places, he would cut it away from it with the extremity of his lenticular cutting instrument, and free it with ease. For it is freed from it without harm, and without fear.
And if the fracture of the bone does not penetrate as far as the panniculus, and what is cut away from the bone is its surface, and roughness and subtle splinters remain in it; then it is necessary that you shave away that roughness, and eradicate those splinters with subtle rasps from which you have prepared a number of different sizes, so that it be possible for you to administer from them in every suitable place more convenient ones, and straighter ones, according to that which the operation leads you to, and the fractured bone itself, and its figure. And it is necessary that you administer in your reasoning, which first roots out the mouth, wider than those razors. Then use after it a more subtle one.
And it is necessary that you administer in your reasoning, which first roots out the mouth, wider than those razors. Then use after it a more subtle one. And do not cease to do that according to continuity, until you arrive at administering a thinner and more subtle one of them all.
But in every one of the remaining small fissures and subtle fractures, it is necessary that you administer care according to that which leads to its correction.
And it is a thing that is not concealed from anyone who has any study whatever in this art, and has stood upon that which we have written and shown in large fractures.
But if the panniculus that is laid bare is retained when you root out the mouth: then it is necessary that you take a linen cloth according to the quantity of the wound and submerge it in rose oil, and place it over the orifice of the wound. Then take another cloth doubled or tripled and submerge it in wine and rose oil, and place it over the first cloth: and do that as easily as you can over it so that it does not weigh down the siphac.
Then administer a wide ligature over it: and do not tighten it except by the quantity which holds the wound only: and leave it with this care for one day or two days until you are secure from hot abscess. Then loosen it and administer afterward medicines with which there is superfluous dryness such as lily roots, herb flour, frankincense powder, aristolochia and similar to them: and it is necessary that you make a powder from these medicines, which is powdered over the wound as they are dry.
And universally administer in it every medicine of a property which is to cleanse and not bite. And beware according to your virtue that the wound be clear in your healing: and there be neither filth of ointment nor dirtiness in it.
And beware according to your virtue that the wound be clear in your healing: and there be neither filth of ointment nor dirtiness in it. Do not allow pus to collect on it at all: for when pus gathers upon the membrane of the brain, it corrupts it and putrefies it, and there follows upon the sick man thereafter a great ill. And this has already occurred in the skull, when the opening is cleaned from it, especially when its care is neglected, blackness arises on its surface.
Therefore consider then, if accidents happen to the sick man which we have said, know that it is undoubtedly pernicious.
And if blackness does not occur except from the medicine which is put upon it, and it is in the power of that medicine to make that blackness: then it is necessary that you take one part from honey, three parts from rose oil, and lines with them a cloth. Then place it upon the skull: afterwards care for it with kinds of treatments which suit, until it is healed.
Concerning the restoration of the nose when it is broken.
Know that the nose is not broken except its two upper parts together, or one of them: forasmuch as it itself has two bones: because the lower part of it is cartilaginous, it is not broken and there does not happen to it except attrition, crookedness, and bruising.
If one of the two parts is broken, then it is necessary that you introduce a small finger into the nostril and equalize that fracture with your index finger, and thumb outside until the nose returns to its natural shape: and let this be done artfully, with ease. And be careful that during your operation, that pain does not befall the sick man.
But if the fracture is in the upper part of the nose and the finger does not reach it: then it is necessary that it be equalized by the extremity of a radius, in which there is a small thickness.
But if the fracture is in both parts, then do the same as that: and hasten with its restoration on the first day of the fracture, if it is possible. And if not, then after the seventh or tenth day, apply a warm poultice at rest.
Then introduce into the extremity of the nose a linen wick if the fracture is in one part; or introduce two wicks if the fracture is in two parts; and let it be of such thickness as to fill the nostril.
And some ancient restorers have said that the wicks should be steeped in butter and changed every day. But that does not seem right to me; rather, the wick should be steeped in egg white mixed with powdered starch. Then leave the wick until the mouth is set and the cartilage hardens.
And sometimes a goose feather is introduced into the nose in place of the wick after it has been wrapped in a soft cloth; and this holds the nasal fracture more firmly and so that the weak person is not prevented from breathing.
And this is not necessary. If you wish, do that; and if you wish, use wicks.
If it should happen that the nose breaks again while you are working on a hot poultice, plaster the nose with carrot or with quince infused in rose oil, or a little from dragon's blood.
But if there is no hot poultice, then it is necessary that the plasters be made with sesame flour and frankincense powder mixed with egg white. Then place a soft swab on top of it and do not bind the nose in any way at all.
But if the nasal bones are fractured in small pieces or crushed, then it is necessary that you split them apart and remove them with instruments suitable for the purpose. Then suture the incision and care for it with plasters or ointments suitable for that purpose until it heals and unites.
If a wound should occur between the nose, then it is necessary that you heal it with ointments and apply a tube of lead until it heals.
On the restoration of the lower jaw, when it is broken.
When the lower jaw is broken and the fracture is not with a wound, then observe if its fracture is only outward, and it is not broken into two, and curves inward: then its recognition is easy.
Therefore it is necessary that if its fracture is on the right side, that you insert the index finger of the left hand into the mouth of the infirm: similarly if the fracture is in the left jaw, then insert the index finger of the right hand; and press with it the gibbosity of the fracture inward with ease to the exterior with your other hand from the inner part of the bone decently, and equalize it with it.
But if the fracture of the jaw is already separated into two, then it is necessary that you apply extension from both parts, according to straightness: until its equalization is possible.
But if there should already occur a concussion or disruption in the teeth: then bind those from which you hope that they will remain with gold thread, or silver, or silk. Then place a cerecloth over the fractured jaw. Then place a doubled cloth over it, and put a large well-made starched bandage over the cloth: or a piece of leather equal to the length of the jaw.
Then tie it from above according to how its binding is prepared for you and suits its application until it is not destroyed, and order the infirm person to be in tranquility and quiet; and have their food be mild draughts.
But if you think that something is already changing in some way: then hurry and untie it on the third day. Then straighten what has been altered from it and plaster it with powder of millstones, with lime water, or with sesame flour after you have removed the cerecloth; and put soft wool over the plaster.
Therefore while that plaster remains applied over it and the bone's position is not changed, leave it alone until it heals or bind the fracture. This fracture is often bound in three weeks.
But if a hot abscess should occur meanwhile, then administer that which we have often said in its treatment until that abscess is removed.
And if the fracture is with a wound, consider it. For if now a fragment is separated from the bone or from the sinews, then take great care in this so that you remove those fragments of sinews with what is fitting for you from instruments in their removal.
But if the wound opening is narrow, then enlarge it with a phlebotomy according to the amount of your need.
Then when you have removed those sinews and nothing remains of them, suture the wound opening if it is wide; and if not, apply over it one of the plasters which are suitable for that, and let it become incorporated until it heals.
On the restoration of the Furcula when it is fractured.
The Furcula is most often fractured from the front part from the shoulder and its fracture occurs according to one of three ways.
For it is either fractured and separated into two, besides which sinews occur in it; and this is easier for its restoration.
Or so that sinews occur in the bone; and this is more difficult for its restoration.
Or so that it is a fracture with a wound.
And the operation on it when the fracture is without a wound is that you have two assistants present, so that one of them holds the following fractured Furcula and the other extends the neck towards the side.
Then equalize the fracture with your fingers until its form is made as it should be and there be neither prominence nor concavity in it.
If the extension is not sufficient, it is necessary to place a sphere made of cloth or wool under the fork of the fractured limb; and its size should be according to the amount needed: extend and raise the fork and constrict the sphere with your hand until you equalize the fracture as required.
If you cannot draw the extremity of the fork outwards because it has reached a depth, then it is necessary for the patient to be laid on his back and for a medium-sized cushion to be placed under his shoulder, and for an assistant to press his shoulder down until the mouth of the fork which is in the depth is raised upwards; and then straighten the fracture and equalize it with your fingers.
But if you feel a splinter from the fork and it moves, then you must split it above that and extract the splinter easily.
But if it is held fast in the bone, then apply ingenuity in its incision with one of the incisors which you have prepared for that purpose after placing under the fork an instrument which retains the splinters; and this instrument is made of wood or iron. And this is its form.
It resembles a spoon which has no concavity.
And its width should be according to that which you need from the magnitude of the bone and its parity. Its length however should also be as much as possible for you for the operation.
And it has two extremities, as you see: one extremity is broad and the other narrow.
But if the disruption of the wound which you have made when extracting the bone splinter is wide and you are apprehensive of a hot abscess, then join the two lips of the wound together with sutures.
And if the disruption is small or you fear a hot abscess, then fill the wound with cloths and lint according to the amount of the wound's fissure.
If a hot abscess should occur, then pour a cloth in rose oil, vinegar, and wine, and put it on top of it.
But when a bone stricture is without wound and without incision, apply an embrocation made from mill flour with egg white on the bone. And place a soft pledget on top of it. Then place a sphere under its nipple, if that is needed. Then take a himemat, that is, a wheel which the Saracens hold, which is very long, and let its width be a palm or thereabouts. Then place a doubled cushion on top of the pledget and embrocation. Then take a splint from a thin board, whose width is three fingers, and similarly in length. Then wrap it in cloth. Then wrap that cloth with the splint in the place of the himemat which falls upon the fracture and place. Then place the himemat upon the fracture as it revolves, and wrap it upon its neck, and under its healthy nipple, and under its unhealthy nipple: and reduce it over every part, and as you see that the stricture holds the fracture strictly; and this is that which is not hidden from you, and all so that the splint is not removed from on top of the fractured bone. And for this it is necessary that you look at the weak [person] every day. Whenever therefore the ligament is softened, and you see the splint already moving: then straighten that, and tighten the ligament.
Then place the weak person's sleep upon his back, and put under his nipple at his sleep a small cushion at night, so that it may be raised with him as aid from his side. The fracture of the clavicle is therefore elevated by elevation of the shoulder.
Or bind his arm to his neck: and do not untie the bandage unless itching or an abscess should occur in the place, up to twelve days.
Then renew the plaster if you see his face towards it. And add to the constriction and let him go until it is restored and coagulates.
And indeed a fracture of the clavicle is bound and strengthened for the most part within twenty-eight days: and sometimes it occurs in some men in a lesser time.
On the restoration of a scapula fracture.
The scapula is rarely fractured, and in its wide part: and the margins of it are not broken except from it.
Therefore when a place is broken from it and broken in its middle and it is not known except by touch; then according to what the fracture's shape is, try its preparation. And reduce it to its natural shape in every way possible for your ingenuity.
Then place over the place miller's dust with egg white and a soft swab, and put a poultice of doubled cloth on top. Then place upon it a wide splint from a thin board according to the quantity of the whole scapula, or a little wider.
But if there is a concavity under the splint in any part of the scapula, then equalize it with a soft swab until the splint descends according to equality.
Then bind it on top with a long bandage, with decent constriction and firmness of the splint until it is not removed from its place.
And consider the bandage every day. Whenever the bandage softens, tighten it and equalize the splint if it is removed from its place; and let the patient lie on his healthy side.
And a spatula is indeed restored in twenty days, or in twenty-five days.
When therefore this number is completed, then untie the ligature: and you will be in safety. For it is from bones whose union is not feared, nor their solution.
But if a fragment proceeds from a bone, and pricks under the skin: then split above it, and remove it. And do what we said in the fracture of the clavicle from the repose of a hot poultice, if anything should happen from it.
On the Restoration of a Fracture of the Breastbone.
The breastbone is sometimes fractured in its middle, and this rarely happens. Its extremities indeed are most often broken, and are shattered from its accidents.
When its middle is fractured, which inclines downward, and it suffers violent pain, and difficulty in breathing, and coughing: and perhaps vomits blood, and a concavity is shown in the broken bone, and is not hidden from the senses, Its restoration is, that the weak person lies on their back; and place pulvinar between its spatulas.
Then press its shoulders, and join the ribs from both sides or flanks with hands: and gently bring it into equality, according to every way possible and suitable, until the shape of the bone returns, according to what is necessary.
Then place plaster and a wad on top of it, and put an splint from a thin board of willow, or elder, and those similar in lightness on top, after you have wrapped it in cloth. Then gently bind it over the broken bone, so that it does not move: and go with the bandage according to its roundness to the back several times, and tighten it with a decent constriction.
Then consider the bandage every hour, and whenever it becomes loose, tighten it: and if its necessity provokes its loosening at itching, or corrosion that happens in the place, or pain, or an abscess: then hurry, and untie it, and remove the plaster, and correct what happened with the method of its treatment.
Then apply a plaster if you see that the face requires it, and bind a bandage to it until it is healed.
On the restoration of ribs when they are broken.
Know that a fracture of the ribs does not occur except in thick places which follow the back, and from their extremities on the front side. And attrition does not happen to them unless it is because they are cartilaginous. And the knowledge of it is not hidden from feeling by inquiry with the fingers.
And its restoration is done in such a way that you equalize the fracture with your fingers as much as possible, until the shape is equalized according to what is necessary. Then apply plasters and bind the broken bone with splints if it needs it.
And if the fracture of the ribs is sloping inwards, then there occurs a severe pain and stabbing, like that stabbing which occurs to one who has pleurisy; therefore because the bone stabs the membrane, and also difficulty in breathing occurs to him, and coughing, and vomiting of much blood; and this is a difficult cure.
And the ancients have already devised many ingenuities in this matter; and among them are those who say that you should give the sick person foods which produce inflammation and windiness so that the belly becomes inflamed and expands and the fracture is pushed outwards.
We, however, detest doing this so as not to cause a quick formation of a hot abscess if it does not occur. But if it already occurs, then you add to it.
And some of them said: place something windy on top, and then suck it with force, and this is a similar reasoning. However, it is feared that the windy superfluities draw to the place because of the weakened condition.
And some of them said: it is necessary that the place be covered with wool which has already been soaked in warm oil, and poultices be placed between the ribs until it is filled; so that there is an equal binding when you encircle it according to its roundness.
Then the weak patient should be treated for pleurisy with diet and medicine.
If the illness constricts the patient severely, in a way they cannot tolerate, and their mouth is pierced by the inflamed membrane with a painful sting, and we fear for the patient: then it is necessary to cut above the place and expose the fractured rib. Then we should place an instrument that preserves the sifac upon it, the memory of which has preceded: and we cut the mouth with ease and cut it itself.
Then we should bring together the two lips of the wound if it is large with suture: and we should treat it with plasters until it heals.
If it should happen that a hot abscess occurs then quickly infuse poultices in rose oil and place them upon it: and the patient should be treated with what is suitable for an abscess.
Meanwhile he should also lie on his side upon which he sleeps until he heals.
On the restoration of a fractured vertebra of the back and neck.
A fracture of the bones of the neck sometimes occurs and attrition rarely happens to them; and similarly with the vertebrae of the back. When therefore it happens to someone and you wish to know whether it will heal or not: then consider. For if you see their hands already softened and stunned and dead; and they cannot move them nor extend them nor constrict them; and when you constrict them with your fingers and prick them with a sharp point, they do not feel it, nor do they feel pain in them: then know that it will not heal and is mortal in many dispositions.
But if they move them and feel constriction with the fingers and pricking: then know that the nape of the bone is sound and that the patient will be healed with treatment.
But if it should happen to the vertebrae of the back similarly to that and you wish to know whether it will heal or not then consider their feet: then if you see that they have become softened and what happens in the hands occurs in them; and when he lies on his back, flatulence and excretion exits without volition; and when turned over onto his stomach, excretion exits without volition: and when turned over again onto his back and wishes to urinate and cannot: then know that it is mortal. Therefore do not labor in its cure.
But if nothing happens to him from it, the matter is lighter.
And the treatment of what happens from it is to try calming of a hot abscess so that you place on a tampon ground rose oil alone or with boiled yolks.
Place that on it several times a day until when the hot abscess is calmed; then place on the spot one of the comforting and drying plasters and bind it with a bandage; and order the sick person to use quiet and tranquility. And let him not sleep on the side where he finds pain until he is healed.
But if it happens at the rubbing of a broken bone or something that is already separated from it; then it is necessary that you cut above the skin and take it out. Then bring together the two lips of the wound if it is large with suture. Then care for it with some flesh-colored unguent until it heals.
But if the last bone of the humerus is broken and it is the end of the tail then it is necessary that you insert the thumb of your left hand into the anus and equalize the broken bone with the other hand as much as possible and in a second equalization.
Then place something on it and take it off; and care for the wound with the care that preceded until it is healed.
Concerning the restoration of a shoulder blade.
Chapter XI.
Rarely are the bones of the shoulder blades broken. But if they are broken then their fracture is not except for a fragmentation at their ends and they split lengthwise and lean towards the inside and it happens to the sick person pain in the place and stinging and the limb that is broken becomes numb.
And the restoration of it indeed is to extend your hand upon it until you stand above the fracture as its form is.
If the fracture is only in its extremities, then equalize that fracture, according to what is prepared for you from equation, until it is assimilated to its natural shape.
But if the fracture is in length, or already leans inward: then make the sick person lie on their stomach until restoration of that fracture is prepared for you. When therefore you have equalized it, put a plaster on top of it; then put an splint made of wood or leather on top of it; and bind a bandage over it so that you do not fear displacement of the fracture nor removal of the splint.
And equalize the concavity of those with what fills it, until the bandage takes it according to equality; and order the sick person to sleep on their back or on their sound side.
But if a hot abscess should happen to him: then cease from its extension and its restoration until the hot abscess subsides; and put on top of it what subsided on it, according to what preceded, then return to its restoration and bind it as is necessary.
But if a bone should lodge or be crushed, extract it.
Concerning the restoration of the fracture of the Adjuvant.
The Adjuvant is that which is between the elbow and the head of the Spatula.
If therefore it is fractured, then its restoration is according to one of two methods.
One of which is: that you take a flat piece of wood of medium thickness, according to this shape, which follows.
And two bandages are tied at its two ends.
Then it is suspended from an elevated place; and the sick person sits on a seat. Then let them project their fractured arm onto the wood until it reaches its nipple, in the middle of which the curvature of the wood is attached.
Then suspend something heavy from above it so that the attendant stretches it downwards. Then the physician shall equalize the fracture with his hands at the same time, until the fracture returns as it should be.
And another method is for the sick person to be laid on his back; and suspend his hand from his neck with a ligament. Then order two attendants, so that one of them holds that which is above the fracture with his hands; and that the other holds below it, and each extends towards his own side.
And if you wish there to be a stronger extension, then bind below the fracture with a ligament, and above it with another ligament; and let each of the two attendants extend them towards their two sides.
If therefore the fracture is near the extremity of the shoulder, then it is necessary that one ligament be placed under the armpit and another under the fracture towards the elbow.
And similarly if the fracture is near the elbow; then it is necessary that a ligament be placed above that place and above the elbow itself.
Then equalize the fracture with ease and without labor until when it is equalized as it should be and a good dressing is applied; then bind it if a hot abscess does not occur.
But if a hot abscess occurs, then loosen the binding until the seventh day. And place upon it wool dipped in vinegar and rose oil until when the abscess subsides; then bind it; and the method of binding it is to put a plaster over the fracture.
Then place an envelopment from new cloth over the plaster. Then let him bind his arm upon the support and put his hand open upon his shoulder.
And place cloth and bind it above the support upon the arm so that the arm remains in the place of the stars; if nothing prevents you from it nor anything changes upon it from the broken bone.
If you fear that something might change over you from a broken bone, then use splints; and it is, that you place upon the fracture itself a splint which is wider and stronger than the other splints. And place between every splint the width of a finger; and let the length of the splints be according to the fracture with the addition of three fingers on each side.
Then bind above the splints a binding which I mentioned at the beginning of the chapter.
And it is, that your binding be tighter over the place of the fracture. And the more the fracture lengthens, the looser the binding should be.
Therefore if you see the position of the splints and the binding, as we said, in the hour that you restore the limb immediately do so; and if you fear a hot inflammation, then loosen the binding and the splints until the seventh day, as we said.
Then consider the bandage every three days so that itching or swelling does not occur in the place, nor is food prevented from reaching the limb because of the abundance and tightness of the bandage. Therefore correct the whole thing according to what we said.
But if you are confident that nothing will happen from it, then do not loosen the bandage except after three days or more.
And let the sick person sleep on his back, and his hands upon his stomach; and put under him a full bolster equally padded with wool.
And consider every hour of the night or day that the shape of the broken limb not be loosened, or that the bandage be softened. Therefore correct all this with your skill.
And give the sick person food according to the order which we have previously prescribed, so that it be delicate food at first until his mouth begins to tighten; then it is necessary that you increase his food.
For the custom of the helper and the leg is that it tightens within forty days; then therefore it is necessary that it be loosened and a bath be given and treated with ointments that correct it.
If the fracture is foul and comminuted, then do not remove the ligature and splints from it for up to fifty days, and up to two months.
On the restoration of a fractured arm.
The arm is composed of two bones, which are called the two humeri.
One of them is small and it is that which follows the thumb. The other is large and it is that which is placed under the smaller one from its lower part.
Therefore perhaps only the larger humerus is broken, or perhaps the smaller one; and perhaps they are broken together.
When therefore the smaller upper humerus is broken, then its restoration is easy, and its healing is easier.
And when the lower humerus is broken, its fracture is bad, and its healing difficult.
And it is worse when the two bones are broken together.
If therefore the bone which is broken is the smaller upper humerus, then it is fitting for the physician at its restoration to place a small extension with ease until it itself equals.
But if the larger humerus is that which is broken: then it is necessary that he place a stronger strapping very forcefully.
And it is necessary that he place the shape of the hand at its restoration, and its extended extension upon a cushion. And let the thumb of the hand be higher than all the fingers, and let the lower joint be below all the fingers, or the rest; and let a weak seated square be upon itself, and let the cushion be before it, or in its direction, at a height so that the weak person does not incur labor.
Then let the attendant extend the arm from its lower part, either with his hand or with a bandage. And let another attendant extend it similarly from above.
Then let the physician equalize the mouth, until he reduces it according to as good a form as he is able.
But if there are splinters in the bone fracture, then endeavor to reduce every fragment into its place by your power.
But if a separated splinter appears in it, and pricks the skin, and you have no hope in its restoration: then cut above it, and remove it according to the way we said in those things which were set forth before.
But if the fracture is with a wound, I already made for that a separate chapter, therefore take its treatment from there.
But if a hot abscess should happen at the beginning of your restoration, then make a cloth with cerate made from rose oil, and white wax, and let it be medium between thickness and subtlety; and bind the cloth over it with a subtle binding until when the abscess is subsided: then remove the cerate, and put on a plaster prepared from powder of millpond snails with egg white. Then put on splints, and let the splint that is put over the fracture itself be a little wider, and stronger.
And know that the number of splints for the arm is six in most arrangements: whether the fracture is in one focus, or in two focuses together.
Then put your binding over the place of the fracture stronger and more vigorous, and somewhat more with binding toward the superiors of the fracture, or toward its inferiors: or put a softer binding than its binding a little, according to as the previous recollection of it was in the beginning of the chapter.
And let the cloths that are wound over the fracture be soft cloths, damp: and let them not be hard, rough. And let the thread by which it is bound be flax properly, medium between fine and thick as we have related.
And consider the limb and the bandage after days. And if anything should happen of which correction is needed, such as itching that happens to the limb: then it is necessary, that you embrocate the limb with warm water until that itch subsides, and let the limb go unconstrained until it subsides, then repeat the binding.
If the stricture has already been softened and the mouth already removed, and similar things: then correct all of that with your own skill and consider it again.
For if nourishment is prevented from reaching the limb due to excess stricture, then it is necessary that you soften it a little and let it be for several days until nourishment flows to it, then constrict it.
But if none of the things we have said happen to the weak one, then it is not necessary that it be loosened except after twenty days, or thereabouts.
Then suspend the weak one's hand to its neck. And let its neck be level, and let it be guarded by its own strength from agitated movements. And make him sleep on his back.
And know that this fracture is restored in thirty days, or in thirty-two days: and perhaps it is restored in twenty-eight days. All of that is according to the dispositions of complexions, and the dispositions of strength.
On the Restoration of Fractures of the Hand.
Know that fractures of the chest of the hand and of the knuckles rarely occur. And it does not happen to it except through much attrition.
Therefore when a fracture or attrition of the sole occurs: it is necessary that the weak one sits square: and in front of him let there be a seat according to equality. Then place his hand extended upon it.
Then let the attendant extend the broken bones, and let the physician equalize them until they are joined in a good conjunction.
Then it is necessary that he place a plaster and bandage, if hot swelling does not occur. Then place a splint on top according to the quantity of the area: and you have already wrapped it in linen cloth.
If the fracture is about the lower part of the sole near the belly, then make a ball shape from cloth and tell the patient to press it on top of it with his broken foot; then tie it tight with a long strip of cloth.
And let there be a star made from leather, in which there is softness so that the leather may be applied with the body of the foot; and bind it as needed.
But if the fracture is on the outside, then it is necessary: to put a star on top and another star from below onto the sole; and let the hand be open and straight. Then place a binding, as the hand turns, and extend it between the fingers with a bandage.
And if a fracture happens to any of the nodes of the fingers, then if it is the thumb, it should be equalized as needed. Then tie it with the sole; and if you wish, put a small straight star on it so that the fracture is raised and does not move.
But if the fracture is in the other fingers, such as the middle, index, or ring finger, then it should be equalized and tied with the healthy finger that follows it; or they should all be tied according to their sequence, and this is better. Or put a small star on top of it, as we said for the thumb.
And consider in your restoration and after the placement of a warm poultice; and oppose it with what is necessary when something happens from it, according to how its account proceeds.
On the Restoration of a Fracture of the Hip.
The hip bone is often fractured, and it is revealed to the senses because it turns towards the inner and rear part.
And its restoration is done in this way: a bandage is tied above the fracture, and another bandage below the fracture. And let the patient lie face down.
Then let the minister extend all the ligament to its part according to equality; and this, when there is a fracture in the middle of the bone.
But if there is a fracture near the root of the hip, then let the ligament be to the part of the pectineus, so that the extension falls upwards; and another ligament under the fracture.
And thus similarly if there is a fracture near the knee, then let the ligament be near the knee, so that the extension is downwards.
Then let the physician equalize the bone with both of his hands until he reduces it according to the likeness of the natural figure; and let the bone be conjoined by a good conjunction.
Then it is necessary that you put on a plaster and strapping, if an abscess does not occur in the limb. But if an abscess occurs in it: then let it alone for several days until the hot abscess subsides, then return to your healing.
And its strapping thus shall be. It is necessary that you envelop over the fracture a hard linen cloth, that is, a bandage; and double it twice, or thrice; and let there remain an excess from it. Then envelop the leg until the heel reaches at the root of the buttock.
And insert a long thread between the hip and leg near the knee downwards; and let the ends of the thread come from the upper part of the two parts. Then revolve over the leg and hip what remains excess from the linen cloth.
Then place on the hip in the place of the fracture itself splints; and place from them one splint over the bone of the leg.
Then fill the voids between the leg and hip with soft cloths so that the strapping is equal.
Then begin with strong strapping from the middle upwards to the place of the fracture with three or four windings. And the more you extend with the ligament from the place of the fracture, let your strapping be smaller and softer, and gentler.
Then attend to the two ends of the thread, which you have inserted between the thigh and the leg. Therefore tie with both three knots which are above. Then go with the two ends of the thread downwards, until you reach the heel of the foot. And with both constrict also the extremities of the knots from the other side, so that the ligature is not removed from its place.
Then release the constriction upon it while it remains so that neither corrosion nor an abscess nor swelling nor anything similar to them may occur to the limb. But if anything of this sort should happen. Then hasten with its solution and rectify everything that happens from it altogether, according to what we have said many times.
But if there is a piece of bone in the bone that is sharp, then it must be made even if it is possible for you to do so. And if not, cut upon it and remove it; and attend to it as we have previously mentioned until it heals.
And whenever this fracture of the thigh is constricted besides the addition of knots to it as we have said in regard to the arm and forearm.
However, in its restoration thus fractured, a weak limp does not occur. And if it is restored alone besides which the other leg is applied, then it is necessary that he who owns it should always limp.
And know that the thigh is constricted for fifty days or a little more or less; all of this is according to the diversity of complexions and other dispositions.
On the restoration of a fracture of the patella.
Know that a fracture of the patella rarely occurs, but attrition happens to it very often.
