But if a fracture should happen to it, then it is not unless a fissure or a hole in its parts. And it is with a wound, and without a wound. And it is placed entirely upon that with sensation.
And its restoration is such that it equals what has been broken off from its parts with fingers, until it is gathered together and joined according to what is possible from equality and ease, and making.
Then place a plaster of sesame flour, or miller's flour, with egg white; and place a round star on top of it if that is needed; and bind over it a suitable bandage for that.
Then consider all the arrangements which we have narrated in the other fractures: such as a hot poultice, and things similar to it; so that you oppose every accident with what suits it until it is healed.
On the restoration of a leg fracture.
Know that the leg has two bones, one of which is thick and is called the Tibia; and the other thin, and is called the Fibula.
And it happens to both of these kinds of fractures as it does to the two bones of the arm; and because of this its restoration is done like the restoration of the arm equally, and the operation is one.
If the two bones are broken together, the leg turns in all directions.
And if the thinner bone is broken, the leg turns to the front part.
And if the thicker bone is broken, you will find it lower down; and it is among those that are not hidden from you.
Then administer extension and setting, and binding of stars according to that equally.
However it is necessary, if the leg fracture is a foul one, having many splinters; that there be less extension and gentler alleviation by you.
And in the leg indeed there is something more from the operation than in the arm; and it is that when you set the stars and complete all your operation, then take two strips of pine wood which are used in making sun-dials to be put under tables; and they are made from palm spatulas: and choose from them those which have a small thickness and are not thin ones; and let their length be according to the length of the leg from the knee downwards.
Then wrap each one in a cloth with two wrappings according to its length. And place one part of the leg from one side and the other from the other side; and they shall be from the knee down to below the foot.
Then bind two bandages in three places from the two extremities and the middle. For when constriction is forbidden, the leg inclines to the right and left, and stands in a good position.
And now indeed let a crib be administered from wood according to the length of the leg, and let the leg be placed in it so that it preserves it from movement.
And indeed it is very necessary that you make that in a fracture when it is with a wound properly.
Then consider the leg every day and strive with the utmost care from abscesses or swelling or other things. If therefore anything of those things should happen, then oppose it with what is necessary until it is healed, if God wills.
Of the fracture of the bones of the foot and toes.
A fracture indeed does not occur thoroughly in the heel bone.
But a fracture sometimes occurs in the bones of the foot and rarely also in the toes; and it does not occur to them except by attrition in many positions.
And if a fracture should occur in the bones of the foot and you see those bones elevated above each other, then let the patient place his foot on prepared ground as if walking.
Then stand and place your foot on that which is raised from those bones; then press or step on it and equalize it until it returns to its place.
Then apply a plaster and a wad on top; and place under the sole of the foot a small board which has two flat heads.
Then bind it with the foot with a decent bandage, after you have wrapped it with cloths and other things it needs.
Therefore, when three or four days have passed, then dissolve the ligature. For you will find the bones equalized, whether they are fractured or dislocated. The operation on them is therefore as you see.
But if some fingers are broken, then restore them and equalize them according to what I have told you about the restoration of the fingers of the hand. Then place a splint with your finger according to the length of the finger, and let it be a little wider; then place this board under the foot which I have told you about, and bind it with a decent binding.
But if what is broken from the bones is two, or three, or more, then place a splint of its own size over each finger, wrapped in linen cloth; and bind the board under the foot; and let it stand on one head of the board outside the belly of the foot so that it holds with good retention.
And it is fitting for you not to forget that you consider all that I have told you in the remaining fractures from the accidents we mentioned, and oppose everything with what is necessary.
Concerning the fracture of a woman's vulva, and the breastbone, and a man's rib.
When a woman's vulva is fractured, then make her sit square. Then incline her slightly to the back of herself, and let it be held back.
Then let the midwife fill her vulva with cotton, until she fills it; and let it be made in the vulva like a sphere from it.
Then let the woman be shaken, and raise her back gradually. For the cotton comes out, until it is made like a sphere before the vulva gate, and the broken bone returns.
Then place a poultice on her back. Therefore when he wishes to urinate, let him remove the bandage easily and then urinate; and let him reintroduce it in the same way that you first inserted it; then let him return to his original position.
Let him do this for seven days or so, for the mouth itself is being restored.
And if you wish, take a bladder of a sheep and fasten a reed tube upon its opening, and introduce the whole bladder into his vulva. Then blow into the tube with force until the bladder is inflated within his vulva for the fracture will reunite.
Then let it be filled with lint and let it remain for several days, as we have related, until it is healed.
And when the breastbone of a man or woman is fractured, then apply in its restoration and to its reunion that which we have related in the case of the shoulder blade bone.
And do not conceal the rectum in these neighboring fractures which occur rarely, especially for one who has some skill or practice.
And understand this book of mine well, for the fracture itself indicates to you in many situations the right way in its restoration and its reunion; therefore understand it.
But when the penis of a man is fractured, take the gullet of a goose and insert the penis into it. Then place an application of cloth upon it and let it be wrapped up, and let it remain for three days or so; and it will be healed if God wills.
On the restoration of fractures of bones when there is a wound.
It is fitting for one to whom a fracture occurs with a wound, especially if it is large, such as the hip bone or the femur or similar to those, that you hasten and phlebotomize him immediately; if the conditions of phlebotomy are attested to you as we have previously stated.
If a wound bleeds, then it is necessary that you hurry to cut it off so that powdered charcoal be sprinkled upon it: if you do not have anything else present.
Then begin to restore the fracture on that same day and do not postpone it unless an abscess occurs.
But if a hot abscess occurs, then delay its restoration until the ninth day, until the abscess subsides. And do not approach it on the third and fourth day at all, for you would cause bad things to happen to it.
But if the broken bone is prominent above the skin exposed: then it is necessary that you try to reduce it and equalize it with your hands with little effort and extension.
But if its extension and equalization are not prepared by your hands then reduce it with this instrument. And it is an instrument made of iron, whose length is the quantity of seven fingers or eight and its width is according to the quantity of the wound. And for that reason it is necessary for the doctor to take three or four of them according to the quantity needed for its healing in every kind of fracture.
And let it be round and have a small thickness so that it is not doubled upon compression on it during the hour of the operation and let its extremity be sharp with an edge at its ends. And let the upper part of it extend in thickness from its middle to its bottom.
And this is its form.
It is necessary in this operation that the sharp curved extremity of it be placed upon the extremity of the prominent bone and press it with it together until the bone returns and equalizes with some equalization.
Therefore, try to equalize the extremities of the fracture to each other.
But if the extremity of its fracture is slender and does not receive good acceptance from the instrument then it is necessary that you cut into the extremity of that bone until the instrument has power over it.
If you cannot reduce them entirely with what we have said, then cut it with one of the knives which we have mentioned; or saw it with one of the saws in any way possible for you.
Then scrape away what remains in the bone from roughness and fine scales.
Therefore, when the bone is reduced and it finds vehement, painful aching after its reduction, then know that the bone has not returned to its natural place.
Do it therefore. For you confer a great benefit on the infirm when you complete your restoration which you make to the bone.
Then immerse a cloth in black astringent wine. Especially if it is summer. And do not place anything on the wound which contains wax or oil; so that it does not cause putrefaction and corruption in it.
Then administer splints in the hour following your restoration of the bone. And leave the wound exposed: so that you may cut a hole with forceps in the folds according to the quantity of the wound.
And beware with all caution not to constrict the wound with a fracture. Very often foolish doctors have done this and caused death, corruption or suppuration, that is, discharge, to befall their patients. And let your constriction be soft, gentle, different from the constriction of other fractures.
And if the wound is bad or large and you fear some of the evils which we have mentioned may befall it and you find pain suspended in the place then it is not necessary to apply splints over it. And make folds from hard cloths instead of splints and constrict it with them.
But when after a day or two, you see the wound already beginning to form pus in it; then remove from on top of it the cloth which you placed on it with wine. Then apply linen cloths and ointments with which we are accustomed to treat wounds, such as the Tetrapharmacum ointment and those similar to it.
And it behooves you to loosen the ligature. And consider the wound every day in the morning and evening until it is consolidated and healed.
And it is necessary that the limb be prepared so that pus flows from it to the lower parts with ease.
If many days pass and the wound does not consolidate nor is pus separated from it, then know that there are small bone fragments there. Therefore it is necessary to inquire about the wound with touch. However many of those fragments are separated, remove them and extract them. And those which are not separated and prick the limb and cause pain to occur, try to cut into them and remove them in any way possible for you.
But if a wound should happen to develop phlegmon, or erysipelas, that is, eschar, or some other kind of corruption and putrefaction: then it is necessary to oppose to each of these with what suits it from its treatment, of which we have previously spoken in its own chapter.
And in the case in which my speech must stand, and to which you should direct your mind when a bone is broken and protrudes above the limb, such as the hip bone, the femur, and those similar to them from large limbs, it is that you do not presume to draw it out, for very often death results from it; rather, leave it alone until it putrefies.
For perhaps it will fall away by itself after twenty days or thirty.
Then, therefore, care for the wound if it seems right to you; and if not, leave it alone.
Concerning the treatment of a Swelling at the Site of a Fracture.
It often happens that thisNodding occurs in the course of healing a fracture. Especially that which is near the joints; and it spoils the shape of the limb from it. And perhaps it hinders the limb from its natural operation.
Therefore consider then, if the nodding is recent: apply to it medicines which have astringency, such as aloe, and frankincense, and myrrh, and sarcocolla, and acacia, and those similar to them.
So that you may take some of these, or all of them; and mix them with astringent wine, or with the white of an egg, or with vinegar; and put them upon the nodding in a pledget, and bind them over it with a good constriction.
And leave the constriction, and do not loosen it for many days. Then loosen it, and repeat another, until the nodding is removed.
Or bind a sheet of lead upon it crosswise. For lead has the property of removing everything that is feared from the limbs.
But if the nodding has already turned into stone, and is tight, and urgent necessity quickly arises to remove it: then make an incision upon its upper part, and cut away the excess that projects; or shave it with certain razors until it is removed; and treat it until it heals.
Concerning the treatment when restored, and the limb remains afterwards slender, not according to its nature.
When a fracture of a bone is restored, and the limb remains afterwards slender, weak; then it does not happen except for many causes. One of which is, the multitude of loosening of the ligature and its ligation not according as it ought; or on account of an excess of constriction of the ligaments, until nourishment be prevented from flowing to the limb; or on account of a multitude of superfluous embrocation in an unseasonable hour; or on account of a scarcity of blood in the body of the sick person, and its weakness.
And its treatment is to feed it weak and soft food, until the blood multiplies within it. And administer a bath. And introduce joy and happiness over it.
Then place pitch on the limb, so that the pitch draws much nourishment to it. And continue its embrocation with warm water, until nourishment flows and it returns to its natural shape.
On the treatment of broken bones when they are restored twisted.
When it happens that after the restoration and healing of a limb, there is a twisting and prominence of the broken bone, or knotting, and the shape of the limb is spoiled, yet nevertheless the limb is not prevented from its natural operation, then you must not heed the one who thinks that the limb should be broken again.
And there have already been many foolish physicians and restorers who have done that in our land.
And this operation is indeed very blameworthy, leading to death, or great fear, so that it may be mortal.
But if the limping and knotting are recent, then it is necessary that it be embrocated with warm water in which softening herbs have been boiled, such as leaves of marshmallow, and its root, and melilot, and those similar to them. And let a plaster be made for it with a softening plaster, such as well-made diachylon.
Or take the mucilage of marshmallow root and mix it with chicken fat or sesame oil and plaster it with that.
Or take fat figs and grind them with dove dung and those similar to them which are called in medicine reducing consolidation.
And sometimes the knot is loosened with diligent, ancient, and gentle friction which is done with wet hands. And let motion be given to the limb in all directions every hour.
But if crookedness has already preceded and become strong and been converted into stone and necessity for its treatment with iron has arisen, then it is necessary that it be cut above it and the continuity of the bone be loosened and what overflows from the knot or bone be incised with fine incisors; and facility be given to it with care and attention.
Concerning speech in dislocation.
Dislocation is the exit of any joint from its place. Therefore it is prohibited from its motion and the figure of the limb is spoiled. And it causes severe pains and vehement injuries to befall the weak one.
Therefore when dislocation happens to someone, then it is necessary to hasten to its restoration and not delay it at all.
For if it is delayed, the place becomes inflamed and perhaps reduction of the dislocation with it.
Therefore it is not necessary that it be moved or extended in the hour of its inflammation. For very often it causes spasms and harmful pains to happen.
But when that happens, then you ought to hasten to the phlebotomy of the weak one. Then let him be dismissed until the inflammation subsides.
Then let the limb be embrocated with warm water and oil. Then let it be reduced with ease.
And let all the limb be treated along with it whose remembrance will come into its place.
I myself have already arranged the chapters of dislocation, even according to what preceded in fracture, from the upper part of the body down to its lower part.
Concerning the treatment of dislocation of the lower jaw.
Rarely do the two jaws dislocate except rarely.
And dislocation of both occurs according to one of two methods. For either they are removed from their places by a small removal and softened for a short time. Or they are dislocated by a complete dislocation until they are softened to the chest, until the saliva of the sick person flows and he cannot hold it, nor can he close his mouth, and his tongue is involved with speech.
When, however, its dislocation is small, then it returns in several positions by itself with little effort.
But if it is a complete, total dislocation, then it must be administered reduction quickly and not delayed at all: and the attendant should hold the head of the sick person: and the physician should insert the thumb of one of his hands into the root of the jaw of the dislocation between his mouth, if the dislocation is from one side. Or he should insert both of his thumbs together, if the dislocation is from two sides. And let the remaining fingers of his hands be outside, with which he equalizes.
And instruct the sick person to cleanse his jaws and release them for eating on all sides. And the physician equalizes the jaw and pushes the jaw until it returns to its place.
But if its reduction is difficult, especially if both jaws are together: then administer vaporization with warm water and oil until an easy reduction of both is made: and do not delay the reduction of both at all, as we said.
When, therefore, they return and are equalized, and the mouth of the sick person is closed, and they are not softened: then place poultices of cloths on them, with cerate made from wax and rose oil.
Then it is tied with ease with soft bandages.
And let the sick person's sleep be on his back and his head stand between two pillows so that he does not move it to the right and left: nor should he attempt or be forced to chew anything: rather, let his food be a gentle sip until when the pains are removed and the jaw is bound: then let him eat what he wants, and administer it with ease.
Let him not labor over an operation upon his own mouth at eating and drinking and kissing until the mandible is joined and healed.
But if the reduction of the two mandibles becomes difficult when they are dislocated at some hour and do not return to their places; then often fevers and constant thirst arise from this; and perhaps the bowels of the sick man are loosened, and perhaps he vomits pure bile.
Therefore when you see this, know that it is destruction and often he to whom this happens dies in ten days.
Concerning the reduction of a dislocation of the collarbone and extremity of the shoulder.
The collarbone indeed does not dislocate from the inner side because of its continuity with the chest. And it sometimes dislocates to the outer side and that is made clear by sensation.
And its restoration is such that the sick man lies on his back and extends his arms. Then with your palm constrict the place with strong constriction for it will return itself.
Then put plaster on it and lint and bind it tight.
But the extremities which follow the elbow and are continuous with it do not dislocate except rarely. But if it should dislocate on some day then it must be reduced and equalized according to what we said and that which is prepared for you.
Then put plaster on it and lint and a bandage. And advise the sick man to adhere to quiet and rest until he is healed.
And with this same treatment the extremity of the shoulder is reduced when it too is separated from its place.
Concerning the reduction of a dislocation of the humerus.
Know that the humerus does not dislocate except according to three ways.
One of them is to dislocate it towards the side of the fibula below. The second is to dislocate it toward the side of the chest. And perhaps it may be dislocated to the upper part of the humerus, and that happens rarely.
And it does not dislocate backwards, because of the spatula. And it does not dislocate forwards, because of the nerves.
And indeed it most often dislocates and comes out below towards the fibula, especially in those whose flesh is scant. Because it comes out quickly in them and goes back in quickly.
But in those whose flesh is plentiful, it is the opposite of that, namely because it comes out with difficulty and goes back in with difficulty.
And perhaps it happens to some people that a blow or a fall occurs, and the humerus becomes inflamed with hot inflammation, and it is thought because of that, that it has already been dislocated.
Therefore, you ought to try that until you stand upon certainty of it, and then proceed in its treatment.
And dislocation is recognized when it is below towards the fibula and you distinguish between a dislocated humerus and a sound humerus. For you find a manifest difference between them and you find in the head of the humerus a concavity, and under the fibula at the touch the head of the humerus as if it were an egg itself, and he who is infirm cannot return his hand to his ear nor move it with all motions.
And likewise, if it dislocates towards the chest or upwards then you find a manifest touch and it is not concealed.
And the reduction of this dislocation is easy when it is recent or the infirm one is a boy.
And its reduction is for the attendant to raise his hand upward. Then put your thumbs under his finger, and raise the joint with strength upwards to its place; and let the assistant raise his hand and extend it upwards. Then let him put it below, for he returns quickly.
But if it does not return through what we said and there have been many days from the beginning since the dislocation occurred, then it is necessary that the weak person bathe in warm water, and an embrocation be administered which softens and soothes, such as if the roots of marshmallow, fenugreek, and melilot are decocted in water and administered.
Then let the weak person be laid on his back; and let a sphere and wool be put under his finger, which is equal between softness and tightness.
Then let the physician put his heel on the sphere, and raise the head of the weak person's shoulder with force, and pull the hand of the weak person downwards.
And if you wish, reduce him according to this method; and it is that you make a man who is taller than the weak person be present, and he stands on the side, and inserts his shoulder under the finger of the weak person: and raises the finger upwards until the weak person is suspended in the air. And let another assistant pull the hand of the weak person downwards to his belly. But if the weak person is light, then it is necessary that he be suspended with something else so that it weighs him down; for the dislocation returns immediately.
And it is also reduced according to another method; and it is that a long piece of wood be fixed in the ground, whose head is round in shape: such as a mortar handle: which is neither thick nor thin. Then let it be put under the weak person after soft cloths are placed on top of the head of the wood; and let the weak person stand according to the length of the wood.
Then his hand should be extended downwards on one side and his body extended on the other side with force; for the joint returns to its place quickly.
But if its reduction is difficult by all that we have said, then use this care: and it is that you take wood whose length is the quantity of two arms, and its width the quantity of four fingers, and its thickness the quantity of two fingers; let it have a round head so that its entry into the depth of the joint is eased, according to this form.
Then soft cloths should be tied over the round head so that the wood does not injure the weak limb.
Then place it under the weak joint so that the hand or arm is extended upon the wood up to the lower parts; and bind the wood above the support, the arm and the extremity of the hand.
Then place the arm upon a step of a ladder by its width, and extend the hand downwards, and let the remaining body hang suspended from the other side. For the joint enters immediately.
Therefore when its entry is complete according to whichever way is possible: then it is necessary that you put under the joint a sphere of equal size from wool. Then place a plaster prepared from miller's powder with frankincense and white of egg over the whole shoulder, just as it turns over.
Then bind under the joint with a decent bandage; and let the bandage be wound over the plaster from above; and attach his hand to his own neck and let it go, and do not move his hand for seven days.
And it is necessary that little food be given to the weak one until the limb is strengthened; for this is quicker for its healing.
Then a wider bandage should be placed after seven days or five, and he should be raised with movement. If therefore it is made firm and not softened, then it is now healed.
But if the junction is dislocated many times, because of moisture which happens to it, or because of another cause: then it is necessary that a cautery with three holes be applied to it, according to what has been stated in the chapter on cautery.
But if this whole thing is done, and the ligament is loosened after seven days, and the junction is not confirmed, and the plaster and constriction are applied upon it several times, and it is not made firm, and it falls and cannot be raised to the upper parts: then you will know that its nerves which are at the head of the shoulder have now been cut, or stretched, or softened: and you will know that the junction will never be fixed in its place.
But the dislocation which occurs towards the chest and nipple, or to the back reduction, is made with a push and extension with the hands: until it returns, and the remaining constriction and care are administered to it, until it is healed.
But if it should happen after healing that heaviness occurs in the limb, and slowness in its motion, then let the weak person use a bath many times, until that heaviness is eased and it returns to its former nature.
Concerning the treatment of dislocation of the elbow.
Know that the elbow joint is dislocated with difficulty, and similarly returns with difficulty also: and indeed it dislocates to all sides, and especially to the front, or to the back.
And its dislocation is not hidden from you: because it falls under sight, and under touch, according to whichever shape it may be dislocated. And when you join the dislocated elbow with the sound one, it is declared to you manifestly. For the joint is hollowed, and the arm cannot bend, nor touch its own shoulder with it.
And it is necessary that you hasten and reduce the dislocation immediately, before a hot abscess occurs to him.
For if a hot abscess should occur to him, its reduction becomes difficult and perhaps it will not heal at all, especially when it is towards the back. For it is worse than all kinds of dislocations and more vehement pain to them and very often death descends with it.
And the restoration of it when it is from those which are possible to reduce is that his hand be extended with two hands and that his arm be extended, and the hands of the physician be above the elbow and beneath it, and he himself presses the joint with the thumbs of his hands together or with the root of his palm until it returns to its place.
Or truly the dislocation is to the front: then it returns so that he doubles his hand with his own hand until he strikes with the root of his palm the elbow which is dislocated.
But if the dislocation does not yield to reduction then administer strong, vehement extension and it is that two assistants extend the arm and that two others hold it firm so that it is not moved during the extension.
Then revolve the arm to every part after it has been wrapped with a long doubled cloth or a broad bandage over his hand.
And when the physician touches the joint with his hand let him anoint it with oil so that it may be one of those things which assist in lubricating the joint with ease. Then he presses the joint with a vehement thrust until it returns.
And after its reduction it is necessary that a plaster be placed upon it in which there is astringency and drying with egg white and let it be bound with a decent bandage. And let the arm be suspended from the neck of the sick person and dismiss him for several days.
Then let it be loosened. But if the joint is made firm in its place then untie the bandage from it and dismiss it.
And if you see the junction not well joined, then repeat the plaster, and the bandage, and leave it for several days, until it is joined. Then dissolve it.
But if gravity happens to it after the reduction of the junction, and the disposition is in motion: then administer moisture in a bath, and subtle friction, and compression, until it is lined, or place over the junction iliac fat, that is, the nape of a ram. Then it is tied, and leave it upon it day and night. Then remove it, and introduce it into a bath. When therefore it sweats, then rub the junction with tempered friction. Then repeat over it alizata juice once, and twice, and thrice with the entrance of the bath, until it is softened.
And if you wish, place over it cow dung, moist, heated with butter, and bind above it.
Do that several times. For it is softened, and returns to its disposition.
On the treatment of a dislocation by Al-Zahrawi.
Almassem's hand dislocates very often, that is, the junction which is between the arm and the hand.
And the reduction of his dislocation is easy. Contrary to other junctions. However it is necessary that swiftness be in the reduction of his dislocation in the hour in which it dislocates, before the place swells: or a hot abscess occurs in it.
And the reduction of the dislocation in him is that you place Almassem's hand weak upon a table and let an attendant extend his hand: and let the doctor place his palm upon the eminence of the junction, and push it until it returns.
Nevertheless, it is necessary that consideration be given, if the palm has already receded to the belly of the hand: then let the weak person place the back of their hand upon the table at extension and reduction; and if the dislocation is proceeding to the back of the hand, then let the position of their hand be intrinsically upon the table, that is, the inner part of their hand be placed upon the table so that the hand of the physician may fall upon the eminence of the joint.
If therefore it returns immediately, it is good; and if not, then bind it with a soothing plaster for the abscess, and dismiss him, and do not touch it again.
For if it does not bear, and you are unable to achieve reduction upon it, after days have passed, nevertheless the joint remains according to its crookedness; and it does not harm the weak person anything except that the hand is softened and cannot grasp anything; then know that the nerves are cut or rubbed.
There is therefore no recourse in it except that it be bound with a cautery near its fall. And perhaps it does not contribute anything.
When therefore Almahassem does not return, then put upon it the plaster which we have described. Then let it be bound and dismissed for five days; afterwards let it be loosened and dismissed, and let the hand be exercised.
But if its motion is deprived and some weight occurs in it: then soften it with warm water and repeated friction until it is softened.
On the treatment of dislocations of the fingers.
When a finger is dislocated from them to any part, or a finger from them to the back of the palm, or its belly; then extend the finger and push the dislocation with your thumbs until it returns.
Then support the head of the finger and suspend it toward the part to which it is dislocated, and dismiss it for two days.
Then suspend him, and extend it, until the straight one is equal in its day.
Therefore, when it is night, bind it according to the very account; and do not cease to loosen it during the day; and exercise it with motion; and bind it at night.
Do that for several days, until it is tightened.
And do likewise with it if it dislocates towards the belly of the hand; and bind it to the same side, and perform your first operation on it until it is healed.
And do likewise with it when it dislocates to the other parts.
Concerning the treatment of dislocation of the vertebra of the back.
When it happens to someone that the vertebra of the back or neck is completely dislocated, or many vertebrae are moved from their places; then there is no cure for it, because death hastens for the weak one.
And the sign of it is that the excretion of the weak one goes forth without volition, and he cannot hold it; and very often some limbs are softened from it, or two legs, or two arms of his, or one of them; or one vertebra is moved from its place.
Very often, indeed, it is moved, because very often the removal of them happens to four parts.
That which is therefore moved backwards is called Kyphosis.
The treatment of it, however, is that you consider then if the Kyphosis happens from childhood; then there is no cure for it, nor is there healing at all from it.
In that which happens from a fall, or a strike, or similar things to those, the ancients have already said modes of treatment in lengthy speech. Of which most does not return with profit.
I, however, have already shortened it from that, which excuses a little from much of it because of my proximity to intention; and by my exposition in which I exposed it, and shaped its instrument in a different way from that which they showed and exposed it.
Therefore I say that in a hump which occurs from the front part, there is neither skill nor cure from it.
And similarly in that which happens on both sides also.
And it is not cured from them except that which occurs on the back properly, with what I describe; and it is, that he extend infirm above his face above an equal platform near the wall; and let straw matting be spread underneath him so that his chest is not injured.
Then let a straight piece of wood be placed fixed in a hole in the ground, at the extremity of the platform, around his head; and another piece of wood around his feet at the other extremity of the platform; and let an attendant hold the wood, and let it not be fixed in a hole. And let another attendant hold the other one.
Then let a soft, firm bandage be wrapped over the head of the infirm person, and under his shoulders; and extend the end of the bandage to the wood which is by his head; and tie it there.
Then bind another bandage above his hips, and above his knees, and at his ankles. Then bring together all the bandages, and tie them to the other wood which is by his feet.
Then let all the attendants extend their side to the wood bandage; and let the two pieces of wood not be removed from their places where they are fixed; but nevertheless, let them not be fixed, as we said.
And the physician put his palms on the spine with strength, until it returns; or let him put them on a board over it. Then he attaches his feet on a board until it returns.
But if it does not return by this treatment, then take a board whose length is about three arms. And dig a hole in the wall of which we said that it is near the infirm person, a place into which the end of the board may enter. Then place the middle of the board on top of the spine. And let the doctor put his feet on the other extremity. And let him tighten it well until the spine is constricted and returns to its place.
Then if you wish, operate with a tourniquet which is wound with your hand; and it is, that you fix in the ground at the head of the sick man on the last step two pieces of wood, whose length is one arm's length; and at his head on the last step two pieces of wood.
And let the length which is between all the wood be one palm; and now a hole has been made in every place in which the tourniquet runs; and let all the wood be firmly fixed in the ground so that it does not move at all.
And insert a round piece of wood; and it is a tourniquet in which the ligature revolves in two holes of two pieces of wood.
And at its extremity there is a hole in which the wood is fixed, whose length is one palm with that which revolves; and similarly in the two other pieces of wood.
Then tighten the ligatures which you have tightened on the chest of the sick man with the tourniquet which is at his head; and the ligatures which you have tightened on his legs with the tourniquet which is at his feet.
Then let an assistant stand by every tourniquet, who twists his hand with licorice, with which the tourniquet revolves; and the doctor adjusts the spine according to what we have said.
This is the form of the tourniquet, which follows.
Then after the spine returns and the place is leveled, it is necessary that you put a drying plaster with white of egg on it. Then a swab. Afterwards place on top of the plaster a splint from a board, whose width is three fingers or thereabouts. And its length is a quantity that takes the place of the Gibbosity and above the healthy part of the Spine.
Then it is tied with the ligament which we have said. And the weak person should use subtle nourishment until they are healed.
And if something of a prominence remains in the place at the end of healing: then it is necessary that medicines which soften and soothe are administered, with the application of the board which we have described for a long time. And now a sheet of lead is applied to that area.
And sometimes it happens that a prominence occurs in the last vertebra of the back and dislocation is thought to be through it, and that is the bone which has already protruded. Therefore it is not necessary for an assumption to be made about this with this medicine: for it may perchance cause death.
On the treatment of a dislocated Ancha.
Know that a dislocation does not occur in the Ancha joint and the shoulder joint except for a dislocation: nor does what happens to the other joints from a small removal and concavity happen to them.
And indeed the Ancha joint is dislocated according to four parts. Which is because it dislocates anteriorly and posteriorly.
And indeed it most often dislocates medially: and rarely dislocates anteriorly and posteriorly.
And the treatment of its dislocation medially is: because when you join the leg of the healthy patient with the unhealthy one, it is longer; and their knee is more prominent than that of the healthy one, and they cannot bend their foot at their groin; and the place following the groin is swollen with a warm poultice, therefore, because the head of the femur has already reached there.
And the sign of the one who has a dislocation laterally is that they are the opposite accidents to these accidents.
And the sign of him to whom dislocation has happened to the front is that he extends the sound leg according to complement; however, he does not duplicate it without pain which is in the knee; and if he tries to walk, he cannot pass beyond that to the front; and his urine is retained, and his groin becomes abscessed, and at walking there is a pressing of his foot upon his heel.
And the sign of him to whom dislocation has happened to the back is that he does not extend his knee, nor can he double it before he doubles his groin; and his leg is also shorter than the other; and his groin is softened, and the head of the thigh bone is at the place of the ilium.
The reduction truly of these kinds of dislocations is that you consider then, if the dislocation is old, which already grows old with him having it. Then if you administer its reduction and it remains according to its own arrangement, then there is no cure at all in it. Therefore it is not fitting that a complete presumption be made upon him.
But in him whose dislocation is recent and is from one of the four kinds of dislocations then hurry to this so that you wish to join it and extend it inwards and outwards and move it right and left, perhaps indeed it returns and does not need other care.
But if it does not return in this way, then it is necessary that you prepare a strong attendant and he extends his leg from the lower part, either with his hands or with a bandage which is tied above his knee: and another attendant extends his hand from above so that he inserts his hands under his tibias. Then let him bind with a soft bandage above the root of the thigh and hold the extremity of the other bandage.
And let the extension of his be either from the front part of the groin to the part of the fork, or from the back to the part of the back.
And let the extension of them all be with one force, until the weak person is raised from the ground with his body, and remains hanging.
For this kind of extension is a common kind of the four kinds.
Therefore if the dislocation returns with what we said, it is good; and if not, then it is necessary for every kind that which I say about its proper cure.
In the reduction indeed of it when the dislocation is inward, it is necessary that the weak person be laid on his sound side. Then let him place a bandage over the root of the thigh, in that which is between the head of the thigh and the place which is under the groin. Then extend the bandage upward from the groin to a higher part of the body, to the collarbone.
Then let another assistant take hold of his strong arms and reduce the thick place of the weak thigh, and extend it outward with a forceful extension. Now indeed it returns to its place.
And this kind is easier than the other kinds of cures by which this limb is reduced.
But if it escapes from you, and does not obey you to enter with this kind of cure altogether: then it is necessary that two feet of the weak person be tied together with a strong, soft bandage, over the two heels and over the two knees; and let the length of each be four finger-breadths from its companion; and let the weak leg be extended more than the other by the quantity of two finger-breadths.
Then let the weak person be suspended over the head of a beam which is in the house; and let the length of it from the ground be the quantity of two arm-spans.
Then direct a strong young man to brace the head of the thigh; and let another young man be suspended with the weak person; and let another young man push, bracing the thigh with strength, for the joint returns to its place quickly.
In its reduction, however, when there is dislocation outwards, it is necessary that the patient lie on a bench, as we have described for one who has a hump. And let the ligature be tightened above his thigh, properly the weak one, and above his chest.
Then let two pieces of wood be placed, one at his feet and the other at his head. Then let a further piece of wood be placed in the middle of the bench, fixed well: on which wet cloths should be wrapped so as not to hurt the patient: and let the wood be between his thighs so that it is not pulled downwards during extension.
Then let every assistant extend to their part, and the doctor equalize the reduction with his hand.
If therefore it yields to reversion, it will be good.
And if not, place it upon the very board and press it equally as we said for a hump. However it is necessary that the patient lie on his sound side.
And when the dislocation is to the front, then it is necessary that the weak leg be extended quickly: and this is according to the same account on the bench: and let the doctor place the palm of his right hand upon the groin of the weak one. Then he should compress him with his other hand and with that compression place him extended downwards towards the knee.
And if the dislocation is to the rear: then it is not necessary that the patient be extended downwards, and he should be raised above the ground: rather he should be placed upon a hard object, as it is necessary, as it also happens when his ankle dislocates outwards, according to what we said about his reclining position on two supports: and this is upon his face and with extended ligatures: as we said recently.
And it is necessary that compression be administered with a board also upon the place to which the joint travels, when reduction is completed as it should be.
And the sign of its return is not hidden from you; and it is, that you extend the two legs of the weak one; and when you see them equal, and the weak one draws up his leg, and expands it without excuse: then you will know that the limb has now returned, according to what is necessary: then join the two hips, and put on a plaster, and bind with an imemati, that is, a wheel; so that the ancha does not move to any part: and let the weak one adhere to quiet for three days, or four.
Then untie the ligature and the plaster, and compare the leg with the other; then if you see them equal in position, then you will know that the dislocation has now been fixed; therefore discharge the weak one to walking.
And if you see anything of softening; then return and plaster that, and bind it according to your first binding; and let it also remain for three days.
Then untie it and make him delay walking on both for several days until it is well strengthened.
Concerning dislocation of the knee.
The knee dislocates according to three modes. It dislocates outward, inward, and downward, that is, backward.
And it does not dislocate forward.
And the sign of its dislocation is that you command the weak one to apply his leg to his thigh. But if it does not adhere to the thigh: then you know that the knee is dislocated.
And the restoration of all modes of its dislocation is to make the weak one sit upright so that he now extends his leg if he can over it, and leans his back against your face; and place his foot between your feet.
Then make his knee adhere with your palms, and pass them straight between your fingers over his knee. Then apply with your hand the two sides of his knee with strength. And another attendant should extend his foot until the knee returns to its place.
And the sign of reduction is that the leg adheres to the hip with ease, not laboriously.
Then plaster it, and make the leg adhere to the hip. Then bind them together with a splint for three or four days. Then untie them.
And let the weak one not walk for several days until he is healed.
But if reduction is refused to you, then administer strong extension with bandages, of which the preceding account was given in the treatment of a sprain, until it returns.
On the treatment of dislocation of the heel.
The heel is sometimes moved by a small removal and sometimes dislocated according to its complement. And its dislocation occurs, either inward or outward.
And the sign of its dislocation is that you see the heel swollen, protruding to the side to which it is dislocated.
But the treatment of its removal is easy reduction: and it is that it be extended with ease with the hands, and equalized until it returns.
When treating it and it is dislocated according to its complement, you must make the weak person sit straight; and let a strong servant hold him firmly from behind at the middle of his back.
Then you take his foot with your right hand from its upper part and with your left hand from the lower part of his foot in the place of the heel.
Then draw the foot towards you with your right hand. Then push twice with your left hand.
Then extend with your left and press the chest of the foot with your right towards the leg without effort.
Do that twice as we have described.
Then press the chest of the foot towards the leg in the third turn and you draw the heel.
Therefore if it returns in one or two turns according to this method and you see the foot equalized, it is good; and if not, then repeat the operation upon him for it will return.
But if its reduction is prevented from you with what we have described: then make the weak person lie on his back on the ground and fix a bolster firmly in the ground so that it falls between his thighs; and now wrap a cloth over him so as not to injure the weak person.
Then let a servant take hold of his thigh; afterwards let another servant extend the foot either with his hands or with a bandage which he ties above the ankle. Then let all the servants extend against the extension of their companions and let the bolster stand between the two thighs of the weak person holding him so that his body is not drawn downward during extension.
Then the doctor equalizes the dislocation with his hands and let another servant hold the sound leg downwards. For the dislocation returns with speed.
Therefore when the dislocation returns and its restoration of health is declared to you then apply a plaster and compress it firmly with bandages and tie the foot to the leg with a bandage.
And you must be careful or afraid of the nerves which are above the heel behind, so that a bandage is not tight over them and injure them.
Then let him go for two days, or three. And if the bandage is loosened, tighten it; then untie it on the third or fourth day; and forbid the weak person from walking for forty days. But if he tries to walk before that time, there is no safety lest the dislocation be undone over him and be corrupted and not receive healing afterward.
But if a hot abscess should happen to him: then it is necessary that you administer to him in the quieting of it that which our narration previously set forth in another place from its treatment, and an application, until it is removed.
Concerning the dislocation of the toes, and its treatment.
It is necessary that what is dislocated be made equal to those with a little extension without labor; and indeed this is not difficult, but rather easy.
But if the dislocation is in some knots of the back of the foot: then it is necessary that the weak person make his foot sit upon an equal place to the ground, or upon a board; and he stands thus as if walking.
Then you stand and put your foot upon that which protrudes from those joints. Then press that with your foot with strength, until it returns and you see it already equal, and there does not appear a prominence in its place.
Then put under the belly of his foot a board which takes the whole foot, to which are two heads. Then tighten it with a decent bandage for three days. Then untie it and make him rest from walking for many days until it is tightened and secured from reduction.
Concerning the kinds of dislocations with a wound or fracture.
When something of this kind occurs and you attempt its treatment and restoration, very often death follows; and therefore it is not necessary that one presume over the treatment of such a thing unless he is prudent in the art of long practice, merciful, pious, slow, neither bold nor presumptuous; and that he use in the beginning medicines which only subdue hot inflammations: and dismiss the sick to divine judgment. Unless you see or hope for salvation from destruction with the mildness of the illness and where something of hope appears. Therefore attempt its reduction immediately at the beginning of the matter, before a hot inflammation occurs.
If then the limb returns as we wish, then administer a regimen which subdues inflammations; and care for the wound with what suits it from drying ointments.
But if the dislocation is with a fracture and separate splinters occur in the bone, then attempt their removal and do in it what we said in simple illnesses from that which preceded in the places of them.
And be cautious according to your virtue and guard yourself from entering the way of fear according to what my previous preaching was to you.
For that is better for you with your Creator and more lasting for your glory and safer for your body among them in your time.
